Al-Anbabi's Refutation of the Suitability Claim
Al-Anbabi rejects the claim that the will is suitable for all permissible things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Anbabi's refutation of the claim that the divine will is suitable for all permissible things, and his distinction between potential and actualizing attachment.

Core Idea
The will's suitability does not necessitate restriction to a particular; it only specifies the unspecified some.

Al-Anbabi responds to a claim about the divine will's suitability. He argues that the claim is not accepted and its reasoning leads to contradiction. He introduces a crucial distinction: potential attachment (al-suluhi) is for the unspecified possible, while actualizing attachment (al-tanzihi) is for the particular. This short passage refines our understanding of the will's operation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation to get the overall meaning.
Notice the compressed style; al-Anbabi is responding to a previous claim.
2
Identify the claim being refuted
The claim is that the will's suitability (salahiyya) for all permissible things implies it can specify any particular. Find where al-Anbabi rejects this.
The phrase 'هذه الدعوى غير مسلّمة' signals rejection.
3
Understand the objection
Al-Anbabi says the reasoning leads to 'combining two contradictories'. Think about how: if the will is suitable for all, it would need to specify every particular simultaneously, which is impossible.
4
Grasp the distinction
Al-Anbabi introduces two types of attachment: potential (suluhi) for the unspecified possible, and actualizing (tanzihi) for the particular. This resolves the contradiction.
The terms 'البعض الدائر' and 'المعين' are key.
5
Reflect on the resolution
Consider why this distinction avoids contradiction: the will's potential covers many possibilities, but actualization selects one particular without implying it must specify all.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي (قوله: يَقْتَضِيهِ لِأَنَّهَا تَصْلُحُ ... إلخ) هذه الدعوى غيرُ مُسَلَّمَةٍ، وعلَّتُه ممنوعةٌ؛ لما يلزم عليه من الجمع بين النقيضين غاية الأمر: أن الصُّلُوحِي إنما هو للبعض الدائر، والتنجيزي للمعيَّن، وليس في قولهم ببعض ما يجوز عليه التقييد بالمعيَّن له.
Annotations of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi: (His words: 'it necessitates because it is suitable... etc.') This claim is not accepted, and its reason is rejected because it would entail the combination of two contradictories. The ultimate point is that the potential attachment is only for the unspecified some, and the actualizing attachment is for the particular; and there is no restriction to the particular in their statement 'some of what is permissible upon him'.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
Al-Anbabi here critiques a claim that the will's suitability (salahiyya) for all things implies that it can specify any particular. He argues that this claim is not admitted (ghayr musallama) and its reasoning is flawed because it leads to a contradiction. Rather, the potential attachment (al-suluhi) relates to the unspecified possible (al-ba'd al-da'ir), while the actualizing attachment (al-tanzihi) relates to the particular (al-mu'ayan). The phrase 'some of what is permissible upon him' does not imply restriction to the particular.

Key Terms
صلوحي
تنجيزي
دعوى
البعض الدائر
المعين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if the will is suitable for all permissible things, it must actually specify every particular thing.
Correction: Al-Anbabi clarifies: potential attachment is only for the unspecified possible; actualization selects one particular without contradiction.

Checkpoint
According to al-Anbabi, to what does the potential attachment of the will relate?
A) The particular possible
B) The unspecified possible
C) All possible things
D) Only pre-eternal things

Key Takeaway
The potential attachment of the will is for the unspecified possible, not the particular, avoiding contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a major decision and feel torn between many 'permissible' options, do you secretly believe that God must have a single specific will for you even before you choose? How does al-Anbabi's distinction between potential and actualizing will free you from the anxiety of 'missing' God's particular plan, and instead trust that His potential will covers all good possibilities until you act?
?
Consider an area of your life where you demand clarity from God—a specific sign or guidance. Are you willing to accept that His will may remain in the 'unspecified some' (al-ba'd al-da'ir) for now, and that the actualizing will only comes with your choice? What would change if you let go of the need for a predetermined particular?
?
Al-Anbabi rejects a claim because it leads to 'the combination of two contradictories'. Reflect: In your own thinking about God, have you ever held two beliefs that logically contradict each other (e.g., God's mercy and justice seeming incompatible) and instead of seeking a distinction, you ignored the tension? Will you now commit to using the kind of careful distinction al-Anbabi employs to resolve apparent contradictions in your faith?

Shaykh Tu'aylib's View on the Will's Attachment
Al-Anbabi reports Shaykh Tu'aylib's view and the objection about specification in pre-eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Shaykh Tu'aylib's view that the divine will has only a temporal attachment and the objection that an eternal attachment implies specification in pre-eternity.

Core Idea
Eternal attachment to the will implies impossible pre-eternal specification.

Shaykh Tu'aylib held a distinctive view on how the divine will relates to its objects. He argued that the will's attachment is temporally actualizing, not eternal, citing Quranic verses. The main objection to an eternal attachment is that it would constitute specification in pre-eternity, which cannot be because specification requires prior equality of possibilities. This passage presents his view and that objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage is a marginal annotation by al-Anbabi.
2
Identify Shaykh Tu'aylib's position
Find the statement of his view: that the will has only a temporal actualizing attachment. Note the Quranic evidence he adduces.
The verse referred to is 16:40.
3
Understand the objection
The objection to the eternal attachment is that it means specification, which in pre-eternity requires prior equality of possibilities, an impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي (قوله: وَعَلَى هَذَا فَيَكُونُ لَهَا تَعَلَّقَانِ... إلخ) اختار الشيخ تعيلب: أنها تتعلق تعلقاً تنجيزياً حادثاً فقط، مستدلاً بالآيات الكثيرة: إنَّمَا قَولُنَا لِشَن إِذا أَرَدْتَهُ () إلى غير ذلك، مُسْتَشْكِلاً القول بالتنجيزي القديم بأن معناه التخصيص، ولا تخصيص في الأزل؛ إذ هو يُشْعِرُ بسَبْقِ استواء. وأجاب عن هذا الإشكال على تسليم إثبات التنجيزي القديم بأن كيفية التعلَّق مجهولة لنا، وككُنْهِ الصفات والذات. قال: وبه يُجَابُ عما وَرَدَ في العلم من لزوم سَبْقِ العلم بالمفردات على العلم بالأحكام معلّلاً ذلك بما عُلِّلَ به في الشاهد اهـ. وقوله يشعر أي لأن معناه قضرَ الممكن على الوجود بدلاً عن العدم مثلاً، فلا بد أن يكون استواؤهما فيه قبل ذلك القصر، وهو لا يصح. وقوله: بسبق استواء، أي: وهو لا يوجد إلا فيما يزالُ. ولك أن تقول: المدارُ على عِلْمٍ الاستواء؛ وإن لم يُوجَدِ استواء بالفعل، فالله يعلم أَزَلاً استواء الممكن في الوجود والعدم فيما لا يزال. وقوله: بما علل به، أي: من أن الحكم على الشيء فرغ عن تصوره، فالتصورُ سابق في الشاهد، أي: الحاضر لنا، وهو الحادث.
Annotations of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi: (His words: 'And on this basis it has two attachments... etc.') Shaykh Tu'aylib chose that it (the will) has only a temporal actualizing attachment, adducing numerous verses: 'Our saying to something when we intend it' (Quran 16:40) and others, objecting to the view of an eternal actualizing attachment because its meaning is specification, and there is no specification in pre-eternity since it implies precedence of equality. And he answered this objection, while conceding the proof of the eternal actualizing attachment, by saying that the modality of the attachment is unknown to us, like the essence of the attributes and the Essence. He said: and by this one answers what is raised regarding knowledge concerning the necessity of the precedence of knowledge of the particulars over knowledge of the rulings, reasoning it by what was reasoned in the witnessed realm. And his word 'implies' means that because its meaning is 'He limited the possible to existence instead of non-existence, for example', so there must be equality of the two before that limitation, which is not valid. And his word 'by precedence of equality' means that it does not exist except in what is temporal. But you may say: the criterion is knowledge of equality; even if there is no actual equality, God knows from pre-eternity the equality of the possible in existence and non-existence in what will come. And his word 'by what was reasoned' means that the judgment on a thing is preceded by its conceptualization, so conceptualization is prior in the witnessed realm, i.e., for us, and it is temporal.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This is a long marginal annotation by al-Anbabi reporting the view of Shaykh Tu'aylib, who holds that the divine will has only a temporal actualizing attachment, based on Quranic verses. The objection to the eternal actualizing attachment is that it implies specification in pre-eternity, which suggests prior equality of possibilities, which is invalid. The answer to that objection, while still affirming the eternal actualizing attachment, is that the modality (kayfiyya) of the attachment is unknown, just as the essence of the attributes and the divine Essence is unknown. This same principle is used to answer the objection about knowledge: the objection is that knowledge of particulars must precede knowledge of rulings (since ruling presupposes knowledge of the thing), but this is not a problem because the modality of God's knowledge is also unknown. The explanation continues by clarifying that the 'precedence of equality' is not a problem because God's knowledge of the possibilities' equality in pre-eternity suffices, even if actual equality does not occur until later. The final note explains that the reasoning in the witnessed realm is that judgment follows conceptualization, but for God, that is not the case.

Key Terms
تعلق تنجيزي حادث
تخصيص في الأزل
استواء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that specification in pre-eternity is unproblematic.
Correction: Specification requires prior equality of possibilities, which cannot exist in pre-eternity because there is no temporal succession.

Checkpoint
What is Shaykh Tu'aylib's view on the will's attachment?
A) The will has an eternal actualizing attachment
B) The will has only a temporal actualizing attachment
C) The will has both attachments
D) The will has no attachment

Key Takeaway
Shaykh Tu'aylib denies an eternal attachment for the will because it would require impossible pre-eternal specification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself thinking that God must have 'chosen' your destiny from a set of equal options, as if He were weighing possibilities like a human? How does Shaykh Tu'aylib's argument challenge that assumption? Will you today consciously affirm that God's will acts without any prior indifference?
?
If the divine will attaches only temporally to each moment of your life, does that change the way you turn to Him in prayer for guidance? How will you live out this truth in your next decision?
?
The objection to eternal specification shows that God's will is not bound by any external cause. Are there areas of your life where you act as if God's will is dependent on your choices? Commit to one area where you will entrust the outcome entirely to Him.

Answering the Objections: Unknown Modality
Al-Anbabi reports Shaykh Tu'aylib's answer and its application to the knowledge objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the answer to the specification objection and how the principle of unknown modality also resolves the objection about God's knowledge.

Core Idea
The modality of divine attributes is unknown, so objections based on human reasoning are not decisive.

Having presented the objection to an eternal attachment, al-Anbabi now reports Shaykh Tu'aylib's answer: even if one accepts the eternal attachment, the modality of that attachment is unknown, just as the essences of the attributes and the divine Essence are unknown. This same principle is then applied to a related objection about God's knowledge: the objection that knowledge of particulars must precede knowledge of rulings is based on the witnessed realm, but for God, the modality is different. The passage also clarifies the meaning of 'precedence of equality' and how God's knowledge covers it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage containing the answer
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that this passage continues the previous discussion and presents the response.
2
Identify the principle of unknown modality
Shaykh Tu'aylib says that the modality of the attachment is unknown, like the essence of attributes. This is a key principle.
3
See how it resolves both objections
The same principle is applied to the knowledge objection. Also, the clarification about precedence of equality shows that God's knowledge suffices.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي (قوله: وَعَلَى هَذَا فَيَكُونُ لَهَا تَعَلَّقَانِ... إلخ) اختار الشيخ تعيلب: أنها تتعلق تعلقاً تنجيزياً حادثاً فقط، مستدلاً بالآيات الكثيرة: إنَّمَا قَولُنَا لِشَن إِذا أَرَدْتَهُ () إلى غير ذلك، مُسْتَشْكِلاً القول بالتنجيزي القديم بأن معناه التخصيص، ولا تخصيص في الأزل؛ إذ هو يُشْعِرُ بسَبْقِ استواء. وأجاب عن هذا الإشكال على تسليم إثبات التنجيزي القديم بأن كيفية التعلَّق مجهولة لنا، وككُنْهِ الصفات والذات. قال: وبه يُجَابُ عما وَرَدَ في العلم من لزوم سَبْقِ العلم بالمفردات على العلم بالأحكام معلّلاً ذلك بما عُلِّلَ به في الشاهد اهـ. وقوله يشعر أي لأن معناه قضرَ الممكن على الوجود بدلاً عن العدم مثلاً، فلا بد أن يكون استواؤهما فيه قبل ذلك القصر، وهو لا يصح. وقوله: بسبق استواء، أي: وهو لا يوجد إلا فيما يزالُ. ولك أن تقول: المدارُ على عِلْمٍ الاستواء؛ وإن لم يُوجَدِ استواء بالفعل، فالله يعلم أَزَلاً استواء الممكن في الوجود والعدم فيما لا يزال. وقوله: بما علل به، أي: من أن الحكم على الشيء فرغ عن تصوره، فالتصورُ سابق في الشاهد، أي: الحاضر لنا، وهو الحادث.
Annotations of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi: (His words: 'And on this basis it has two attachments... etc.') Shaykh Tu'aylib chose that it (the will) has only a temporal actualizing attachment, adducing numerous verses: 'Our saying to something when we intend it' (Quran 16:40) and others, objecting to the view of an eternal actualizing attachment because its meaning is specification, and there is no specification in pre-eternity since it implies precedence of equality. And he answered this objection, while conceding the proof of the eternal actualizing attachment, by saying that the modality of the attachment is unknown to us, like the essence of the attributes and the Essence. He said: and by this one answers what is raised regarding knowledge concerning the necessity of the precedence of knowledge of the particulars over knowledge of the rulings, reasoning it by what was reasoned in the witnessed realm. And his word 'implies' means that because its meaning is 'He limited the possible to existence instead of non-existence, for example', so there must be equality of the two before that limitation, which is not valid. And his word 'by precedence of equality' means that it does not exist except in what is temporal. But you may say: the criterion is knowledge of equality; even if there is no actual equality, God knows from pre-eternity the equality of the possible in existence and non-existence in what will come. And his word 'by what was reasoned' means that the judgment on a thing is preceded by its conceptualization, so conceptualization is prior in the witnessed realm, i.e., for us, and it is temporal.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This is a long marginal annotation by al-Anbabi reporting the view of Shaykh Tu'aylib, who holds that the divine will has only a temporal actualizing attachment, based on Quranic verses. The objection to the eternal actualizing attachment is that it implies specification in pre-eternity, which suggests prior equality of possibilities, which is invalid. The explanation continues by clarifying that the 'precedence of equality' is not a problem because God's knowledge of the possibilities' equality in pre-eternity suffices, even if actual equality does not occur until later. The final note explains that the reasoning in the witnessed realm is that judgment follows conceptualization, but for God, that is not the case.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's knowledge must follow the same temporal order as human knowledge.
Correction: God's knowledge is not bound by temporal sequence; the modality of divine attributes is unknown.

Checkpoint
How does Shaykh Tu'aylib answer the objection that the eternal actualizing attachment implies specification in pre-eternity?
A) He denies the eternal actualizing attachment
B) He says the modality of the attachment is unknown
C) He argues that equality is not required
D) He says specification occurs in time only

Key Takeaway
Shaykh Tu'aylib defends the eternal attachment by appealing to the unknown modality of divine attributes, and extends this to resolve objections about God's knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a divine truth that your intellect cannot fully grasp—like the modality of God's attributes—do you accept it with humility, or do you feel frustrated? Will you consciously submit to God's transcendence, even when it defies your reasoning?
?
Have you ever dismissed a clear teaching of revelation because your logic found it problematic, like the objection about precedence of knowledge? Will you repent for letting your intellect create a barrier between you and full submission?
?
This lesson insists that we simply do not know the 'how' of God's attributes. Does that unsettle you or bring you peace? How does your inability to fully comprehend God affect your worship and trust in Him today?

Ruling on Attributing Action to God's Power
Sanusi rules on statements attributing action to God's power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ruling on common expressions that attribute action to God's power, and how the underlying belief determines the severity.

Core Idea
Ascribing action to God's power is dangerous if it implies independence from God.

This short but critical passage from Sanusi addresses a common pitfall in everyday speech. Many people, out of simplicity, say things like 'power is active' or 'look at the act of power'. Sanusi provides a precise ruling that depends on what the speaker actually believes. This lesson will unpack that ruling and its implications for faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic once, then the translation. Notice the key terms: haram, makruh, kufr.
2
Identify the two possible rulings
Sanusi gives two opinions: haram and makruh. What condition distinguishes them?
The condition is whether the speaker believes power acts independently.
3
Understand the condition for kufr
If the speaker believes power effects by itself (without God), that is kufr. Why? Because it denies God's sole agency.
4
Reflect on real-life examples
Think of common phrases like 'my hand did it' vs 'Allah made my hand do it'. The nuance matters.
This passage guards against attributing independence to created means.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قول العامة: القُدْرَةُ فعالةٌ، أو انظر فِعْلَ القدرة، أو نحو ذلك؛ فحرام، وقيل: مكروة ما لم يعتقدوا أن القدرةَ تُؤَثِرُ بنفسها، وإلا كفروا والعياذ بالله تعالى.
As for the statement of the common people: 'Power is active', or 'Look at the act of power', or similar, it is forbidden (haram), and it is said: disliked (makruh) so long as they do not believe that power effects by itself; otherwise they disbelieve (kafaru), may God protect us.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage from Sanusi's main text provides a legal ruling on a common expression. The phrase 'power is active' or 'consider the act of power' attributes action directly to God's attribute of power (qudra). Sanusi declares this to be forbidden (haram) according to one view, and disliked (makruh) according to another, provided that the speaker does not believe that power acts independently. If they do believe that power is an independent agent, that belief constitutes disbelief (kufr) because it denies that God alone is the true agent. This ruling protects the Islamic doctrine that all acts are directly from God, and attributes are only instrumental.

Key Terms
قدرة
فعالة
حرام
مكروه
كفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that ascribing an act to God's power is always permissible without considering the underlying belief.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that if one believes power acts on its own, that is kufr. The ruling depends on the belief.

Checkpoint
What happens if a person believes that power (qudra) effects by itself, without God?
A) It is only makruh
B) It is haram
C) They commit kufr
D) It is allowed

Key Takeaway
The ruling on statements about God's power hinges on the speaker's belief: if they attribute independence to power, it is kufr.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever said something like 'My power did this' or 'Look at what my ability can do' without realizing it might imply independence from God? How does this lesson challenge you to watch your tongue and your heart?
?
The person who says 'Power is active' may not believe it, but the person who does believe it falls into kufr. Are there any areas in your life where you outwardly affirm tawhid but inwardly rely on your own strength or means as if they are independent of God?
?
If a loved one casually says 'I have the power to make this happen', how would you lovingly correct them based on this lesson? What would you feel in your own heart knowing the severity of the matter?

The Six Contrasting Possibles
Sanusi enumerates the six contrasting possibles and provides a poetic summary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what the six contrasting possibles are and why they are essential to understanding God's will regarding creation.

Core Idea
The six pairs of possibles define the full range of choices in creation.

Sanusi now clarifies a key phrase in the discussion of divine will: 'some of what is permissible upon Him.' He explains that this refers to six contrasting pairs that cover all possible determinations in creation. This enumeration is foundational for kalam reasoning about God's omnipotence and the contingency of the world.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage slowly, then the translation. Let the author’s own rhythm and listing come through before you analyze.
2
Identify each of the six pairs
The passage lists six pairs. Find each one: existence vs. non‑existence, specific attribute vs. other attributes, specific time vs. other times, specific place vs. other places, specific direction vs. other directions, specific quantity vs. other quantities.
Notice that each pair involves a specific determination and its alternatives.
3
Understand 'contrasting possibles' and the poem
Sanusi names these the 'contrasting possibles' (mumkināt mutaqābilāt). The poem summarizes them. Ask yourself why they are called 'contrasting'.
The poem is a mnemonic to help remember the six.
4
Reflect on the marginal note about quantity
The marginal note by al‑Ajhurī specifies that 'specific quantity' means length, shortness, or mediumness. This clarifies that quantity is not just any measure but a particular one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمراد ببعض ما يجوز عليه الأشياءُ السِّتَّةُ التي تقابلها ستة أخرى، وتلك الأشياءُ هي الوجودُ بدلاً عن العَدَم والصفةُ المخصوصة بدلاً عن سائر الصفات، والزمانُ المخصوص بدلاً عن سائر الأزمنة، والمكان المخصوص بدلاً عن سائر الأمكنة، والجهة المخصوصة بدلاً عن سائر الجهات والمقدار المخصوص بدلاً عن سائر المقادير. وهذه الأشياء تسمَّى المُمْكِنَاتِ المتقابلات. وقد نَظَمَها بعضُهم بقوله: الْمُمْكِنَاتُ الْمُتَقَابِلَاتُ وُجُودُنَا وَالْعَدَمُ الصَّفَاتُ أَزْمِنَةٌ أَمْكِنَةٌ جِهَاتُ كَذَا الْمَقَادِيرُ رَوَى الثَّقَاتُ حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري (قوله: وَالْمِقْدَارُ الْمَخْصُوصُ) وهو الطول والقصر والتوسط.
And what is meant by 'some of what is permissible upon him' are the six things which are faced by six others. Those things are: existence instead of non-existence, the specific attribute instead of other attributes, the specific time instead of other times, the specific place instead of other places, the specific direction instead of other directions, and the specific quantity instead of other quantities. These things are called the 'contrasting possibles'. Some of them have versified them: The contrasting possibles: our existence and non-existence, attributes, times, places, directions, and likewise quantities – the reliable ones have narrated. Marginal note of al-Ajhuri: (His word: 'the specific quantity') it is length, shortness, and mediumness.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage from Sanusi defines the phrase 'some of what is permissible upon Him' (ba'd ma yajuzu 'alayhi) which appears in discussions of divine will and power. He enumerates six pairs (sitta) of possible determinations that God may choose for a thing: existence vs. non-existence, a specific attribute vs. other attributes, a specific time vs. other times, a specific place vs. other places, a specific direction vs. other directions, and a specific quantity vs. other quantities. These are called 'contrasting possibles' (mumkinat mutaqabilat). A poem summarizing them is quoted. The marginal note by al-Ajhuri adds that the specific quantity refers to length, shortness, or mediumness.

Misconception: One might think 'some of what is permissible' refers to actions rather than these six aspects of creation.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly defines it as these six contrasting possibles, covering existence and all determinations of contingent things.

Key Takeaway
The six contrasting possibles – existence, attribute, time, place, direction, and quantity – encompass all the ways a contingent thing can be determined by God's will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider that God chose for you a specific time, place, and quantity of existence. If any of these had been different, you would not be the same person. How does this truth affect your gratitude for being alive exactly here and now? What will you do today to live in awareness of His deliberate choice?
?
Among the six possibles, attribute is included. Think of one specific attribute you have—physical or character—that you often complain about or dislike. How does knowing that God could have given you any other attribute, but chose this one, change your perspective? Will you thank Him for it today?
?
You could have been created at any time in history, in any place, with any quantity. Yet God placed you in this era. What responsibility does that place on you, knowing that He deliberately chose this time for you to know Him and to act? What is one concrete step you will take this week to fulfill that responsibility?

Distinguishing Divine Will from Command
Sanusi distinguishes between divine will and command, refuting the Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between divine will and command in Sunni theology and how it contrasts with Mu'tazilite views.

Core Idea
God’s will and command are distinct and separable.

This passage from Sanusi clarifies a critical distinction in Islamic theology between God's will (irada) and His command (amr). The Mu'tazila held that they are identical or inseparable, leading them to deny that God wills evil. The Sunni position, however, affirms that God may will something without commanding it, and vice versa. This distinction has profound implications for understanding divine decree and moral responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the key terms 'irada' and 'amr' and the opposing views of the Mu'tazila.
The passage ends with an ellipsis, but the main argument is complete.
2
Identify the two Mu'tazilite positions
The text mentions two Mu'tazilite views: some say will and command are identical, others say will is inseparable from command. Understand their consequence: God does not will evil.
3
Understand the Sunni position
Sanusi states that will and command are distinct and separable. This yields four logical combinations. Identify each: (1) will without command, (2) command without will, (3) both, (4) neither.
This shows that God can will evil without commanding it.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this distinction resolves the problem of evil: God as Creator wills all things, but as Legislator commands only good. The Mu'tazilite error is conflating these two roles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن الإرادة والأمر متغايران ومُنْفَكَّان خلافاً للمعتزلة حيث قال بعضهم بأنهما متَّحِدَان وقال بعضهم: بأن الإرادة لازمة للأمر. وبَنَوْا على ذلك: أنه لا يريد الشرور ،والقبائح وينبني على مذهب أهل السنة أنه تعالى قد يُرِيدُ الشيء ولا يأمرُ به، وقد يأمُرُ به ولا يريده، كما أنه قد يريده ويأمرُ به، وقد لا يريده...........
And know that the will and the command are distinct and separable, contrary to the Mu'tazila, for some of them said they are identical, and some said the will is inseparable from the command. And they built upon that that He (God) does not will evil and abominations. And it follows from the doctrine of the People of the Sunna that the Exalted may will something and not command it, and may command it and not will it, just as He may will it and command it, and may not will it...
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
Sanusi presents an important theological distinction between God's will (irada) and His command (amr). He states that they are distinct and separable, contrary to the Mu'tazila, some of whom identify them and some of whom say the will is inseparable from the command. The Mu'tazila concluded from this that God does not will evil and abominations (since He commands only good). The Sunni position, however, is that God may will something but not command it (e.g., He wills the existence of evil but does not command it), and He may command something but not will it (e.g., He commands obedience but may not will it from someone), and both may occur together or both be absent.

أهل السنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God wills something, He must also command it.
Correction: The passage shows that God may will something without commanding it, as in the case of evil, which He wills to exist but forbids.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila mentioned, what follows from the identity of will and command?
A) God wills evil
B) God commands evil
C) God does not will evil
D) God does not command good

Key Takeaway
The distinction between divine will and command allows Sunni theology to affirm that God wills all things, including evil, without commanding them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God wills everything that happens, including the evil you witness in the world, does that ever shake your trust in His goodness? How does knowing that His will is not the same as His command help you reconcile His sovereignty with His moral perfection?
?
Think of a sin you committed recently. Do you believe God willed that sin to occur? If so, do you also believe He commanded it? How does the distinction between will and command change your understanding of personal accountability and divine justice?
?
When you pray for something good but it does not happen, you might wonder if God’s will and command are aligned. How does this lesson help you submit to God’s will while still striving to obey His commands, even when they seem to conflict?

Ruling on the Common Saying About Power
Al-Bajuri states the ruling on the common saying that power is effective.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ruling on the common saying 'power is effective' and the condition that determines whether it is merely disliked or constitutes disbelief.

Core Idea
The ruling on a phrase depends on what you believe about power's independence.

This lesson addresses a common phrase used by laypeople regarding divine power. Al-Bajuri explains that while the expression might seem harmless, its ruling varies based on the speaker's belief. If said carelessly without believing power acts independently, it is disliked; but if one attributes independent efficacy to power, it becomes disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and its literal translation. Notice the key terms: 'power', 'effective', 'forbidden', 'disliked', 'disbelief'.
2
Identify the two possible rulings
The passage mentions two rulings: forbidden (ḥarām) and disliked (makrūh). Note which is stronger and under what condition each applies.
The difference hinges on the speaker's belief.
3
Understand the crucial condition
The passage states that if the speaker does not believe power has effect by itself, the saying is disliked. If they do believe that, it becomes disbelief (kufr).
Belief in independent causation is the decisive factor.
4
Reflect on why belief matters
Reflect on why attributing independent effect to power is considered disbelief. Consider the Islamic doctrine that Allah alone is the creator and sustainer, and no created power acts independently.
This connects to the core idea of tawheed (monotheism).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قول العامة: القُدْرَةُ فعالةٌ، أو انظر فِعْلَ القدرة، أو نحو ذلك؛ فحرام، وقيل: مكروة ما لم يعتقدوا أن القدرةَ تُؤَثِرُ بنفسها، وإلا كفروا والعياذ بالله تعالى.
As for the common saying: 'Power is effective,' or 'look at the act of power,' or similar, it is forbidden (ḥarām), and it is said: disliked (makrūh) as long as they do not believe that power has effect by itself, otherwise they disbelieve (kafarū) – and refuge is sought with Allah the Exalted.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage gives the ruling on a common expression used by laypeople about divine power (qudra). The author says that if someone says 'power is effective' or 'look at the act of power' or similar, it is either forbidden (ḥarām) or disliked (makrūh) depending on the scholar. The crucial condition is whether the speaker believes that power has effect by itself (i.e., independently of God). If they do not believe that, and say such a phrase carelessly, it is merely disliked. But if they actually believe that power acts by its own essence, that would be disbelief (kufr) because it associates partners with God's exclusive creative power. The phrase 'and refuge is sought with Allah' shows the seriousness of this error.

Key Terms

مكروه
كفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'power is effective' is always permissible.
Correction: The ruling depends on whether one believes power acts independently; if so, it is kufr.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the ruling on the common saying 'power is effective' if the speaker does not believe that power has effect by itself?
A) It is haram.
B) It is makruh.
C) It is kufr.
D) It is permissible.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of the phrase depends on the belief behind it; attributing independent effect to power is disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself relying on your own strength or abilities, do you internally attribute the outcome to Allah alone, or do you feel a sense of self-sufficiency? What phrase could you say to realign your heart?
?
If a fellow Muslim casually says 'My power got me this job,' how would you lovingly remind them of the lesson from this passage? What would you say to protect their faith without alienating them?
?
Is there an area of your life where you unconsciously believe that your own effort has independent effect? Spend one minute today silently affirming 'La hawla wa la quwwata illa billah' and mean it from the depths of your heart.

The Distinction Between Divine Will and Command
Al-Bajuri distinguishes divine will from command and refutes Mu'tazila with four cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari theology distinguishes between God's will and command, and why this matters for understanding divine decree and moral responsibility.

Core Idea
God can will something without commanding it, and command without willing it.

This passage tackles a subtle but critical distinction in Islamic theology: the difference between what God wills and what He commands. The Mu'tazila conflated them, leading to problematic conclusions about evil. The Ash'ari response, articulated here by al-Bajuri, preserves both God's comprehensive will and His moral command. Let's examine the four logical possibilities.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazila position
Read the passage to see how the Mu'tazila either identified will and command or made will inseparable from command. This is the view the Ash'aris reject.
Note that the Mu'tazila concluded God does not will evil acts because He commands only good.
2
Grasp the four logical possibilities
The passage lists four cases: (1) will and command together, (2) will without command, (3) command without will, (4) neither. Study these carefully.
These are derived from the Ash'ari premise that will and command are distinct and separable.
3
Apply the examples
Match each case to the examples given: Abū Jahl (disbelief willed but not commanded), Abū Bakr (faith willed and commanded), and the unnamed examples.
The fourth case (neither will nor command) is also illustrated by disbelief of a person whose disbelief is known, like the first? Actually careful: the text says 'as in the disbelief of one mentioned' which may refer back to Abū Jahl or another.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن الإرادة والأمر متغايران ومُنْفَكَّان خلافاً للمعتزلة حيث قال بعضهم بأنهما متَّحِدَان وقال بعضهم: بأن الإرادة لازمة للأمر. وبَنَوْا على ذلك: أنه لا يريد الشرور ،والقبائح وينبني على مذهب أهل السنة أنه تعالى قد يُرِيدُ الشيء ولا يأمرُ به، وقد يأمُرُ به ولا يريده، كما أنه قد يريده ويأمرُ به، وقد لا يريده ولا يأمر به، فالأول كما فى كفر من تعلق عِلْمُ الله بكفره كأبي جهل، والثاني كما في إيمان من ذُكِرَ، والثالث كما في إيمان من تعلقَ عِلْمُ الله بإيمانه كأبي بكر، والرابع كما في كُفْرِ من ذُكِرَ.
And know that will (irāda) and command (amr) are distinct and separable (mutaghāyirān wa munfakkān), contrary to the Mu'tazila, for some of them said they are identical, and some said that will is inseparable from command. And they based on that that He does not will evils and ugly acts. And based on the school of Ahl al-Sunna, He the Exalted may will something and not command it, and may command it and not will it, just as He may will it and command it, and may not will it and not command it. The first is as in the disbelief (kufr) of one whose disbelief Allah's knowledge is attached, like Abū Jahl; the second is as in the faith of one mentioned; the third is as in the faith of one whose faith Allah's knowledge is attached, like Abū Bakr; and the fourth is as in the disbelief of one mentioned.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage addresses the relationship between divine will (irāda) and command (amr). The author states that they are distinct (mutaghāyirān) and separable (munfakkān), meaning God may will something but not command it, and command something but not will it. This opposes the Mu'tazila who either identify them or make will inseparable from command. The Mu'tazila argued that God does not will evil and ugly acts because He commands only good. The Ash'ari position is that God indeed wills all things, including evil and disbelief, as part of His overall decree, but He commands only good. Four logical possibilities arise: (1) He wills and commands – e.g., the faith of Abū Bakr; (2) He wills but does not command – e.g., the disbelief of Abū Jahl (God willed his disbelief but did not command it); (3) He commands but does not will – e.g., the faith of one whose disbelief is known (God commands faith but does not will it for that person); (4) He neither wills nor commands – e.g., the disbelief of one whose disbelief is known (God neither wills nor commands his disbelief, but this seems to contradict the first case; the examples are based on God's foreknowledge and decree, and the details are nuanced). The examples illustrate how God's will is comprehensive, encompassing good and evil, while His command is only for good.

Key Terms
الإرادة
الأمر
متغايران
منفكان
المعتزلة
أهل السنة
أبو جهل
أبو بكر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God must will whatever He commands, and command whatever He wills.
Correction: Ash'ari theology holds that will and command are distinct and separable: God may will something He does not command (e.g., disbelief) and command something He does not will (e.g., faith from a disbeliever).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Ash'ari position on the relationship between divine will and command?
A) They are identical.
B) They are distinct and separable.
C) Will is inseparable from command.
D) They are opposites.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari distinction between will and command allows God to will all things—including evil—while commanding only good, preserving both divine sovereignty and moral accountability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read that God wills the disbelief of some people—like Abu Jahl—yet commands them to believe. Think about a person in your life whom you consider far from faith. How does this lesson change the way you pray for them? Are you willing to accept that God may not will their guidance, yet still command you to call them? How does that sit with your heart?
?
The lesson says God may command something and not will it. Consider a commandment you struggle to obey, like prayer or honesty. God commands it, but does He will it for you in that moment? What does that mean for your effort? Will you obey even if you feel no inner inclination?
?
Ahl al-Sunna holds that God wills both good and evil—but commands only good. Look at the evil you have done. God willed it by His decree, but He did not command it. That means you cannot blame God; the sin is entirely yours. As you read the example of Abu Jahl, do you see your own accountability more clearly? What are you going to repent of today?

Attributing Evil to God's Will: Context Matters
Al-Bajuri presents the disagreement and correct solution on attributing evil to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ruling on attributing evil actions to God's will and the important distinction between teaching and non-teaching contexts.

Core Idea
Attributing evil to God: permissible in teaching, not in ordinary speech.

This passage addresses a delicate theological question: can we say that God willed evil deeds like adultery or disbelief? Al-Bajuri presents the scholarly disagreement and the correct solution based on context. As you read, notice how the Ash'arite position balances truth with respect.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once through, then the English translation. Let the text speak for itself before analyzing.
The passage is short but dense with theological nuance.
2
Identify the two opposing views
The passage mentions two groups: those who permit attributing evil to God's will and those who prohibit it. Find the reasons behind each view as implied by the text.
The text does not give explicit reasons, but the context suggests truth vs. respect.
3
Understand the correct view's distinction
The correct view distinguishes between 'maqam al-ta'līm' (teaching context) and other contexts. Understand what each context means and why the ruling differs.
Teaching context is for explaining theological principles; ordinary speech is for everyday interaction.
4
Reflect on the wisdom behind the distinction
Why is it acceptable in teaching but not in general? Consider both doctrinal accuracy and practical respect for God's majesty.
This mirrors the Ash'arite principle that God creates all actions, but we should not emphasize evil in casual speech.
5
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to reinforce what you have learned.
The question is directly from the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلف في جواز إسناد الشرور والقبائح إلى إرادة المولى ، كأن يقال: أراد الله زِنَا زِيدٍ وكُفْرَ ،عمرو، فأجازه بعضهم، ومنعه آخرون، والصحيح: التفرقة بين مقام التعليم وغيره، فيجوز في الأول، ويمتنع في الثاني.
And there is disagreement on the permissibility of attributing evils and ugly acts to the will of the Master, such as saying: 'Allah willed Zayd's adultery and Amr's disbelief.' Some permitted it, others prohibited it. The correct view is to distinguish between the context of teaching (maqām al-ta'līm) and other contexts. So it is permissible in the former and prohibited in the latter.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage deals with a sensitive issue: whether one may ascribe evil actions like adultery or disbelief to God's will. The Ash'arite position is that God wills all things, including evil, but it is not appropriate to attribute evil to Him in ordinary speech. Scholars disagreed: some allowed it because it is true, others forbade it because it is disrespectful. The correct solution is to differentiate between contexts. In a teaching setting (maqām al-ta'līm), where one is explaining the theological principle that God is the creator of all actions, it is permissible to say 'God willed Zayd's adultery' as a statement of fact. But in general conversation, it should be avoided because it may cause misunderstanding or disrespect to God.

Key Terms
الشرور
القبائح
إرادة المولى
مقام التعليم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think it is always forbidden to attribute evil to God, even in theological discussions.
Correction: The correct view allows it in teaching contexts to explain the doctrine of God's universal will, but prohibits it in ordinary speech.

Checkpoint
According to the correct view in this passage, when is it permissible to attribute evil deeds to God's will?
A) Always
B) Never
C) Only in educational contexts
D) Only when discussing destiny

Key Takeaway
Attributing evil to God's will is permissible in teaching contexts to convey theological truth, but prohibited in ordinary speech to maintain reverence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This lesson teaches that even truth can be inappropriate if spoken in the wrong context. Are there truths about God that you have spoken carelessly? Commit to being more careful with your words.
?
Why is it easier to attribute evils to God in private justification than to take responsibility? How does this lesson challenge your inclination to blame God for your own actions?

Classification of Attributes of Meaning by Attachment
Al-Bajuri classifies the attributes of meaning into four types based on attachment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the seven attributes of meaning are classified into four categories based on the nature of their attachment.

Core Idea
Attributes of meaning attach differently: none, influence, disclosure, or indication.

Al-Bajuri builds on the earlier discussion of the two attributes of meaning to provide a comprehensive classification of all seven. He defines attachment as the attribute's requiring an additional matter beyond the essence. Then he shows that life has no attachment, power and will attach via influence, knowledge, hearing and sight via disclosure, and speech via indication. This classification is fundamental for understanding how divine attributes relate to their objects.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of attachment
Read the passage and note that attachment (ta'alluq) is defined as the attribute requiring something additional beyond the essence. This sets the framework for the classification.
The definition is crucial for distinguishing types.
2
Identify the four categories
The passage lists four types of attachment: no attachment, attachment of influence, attachment of disclosure, and attachment of indication. Find each in the text.
These correspond to the nature of the attribute's relation to its object.
3
See which attributes fall into each category
Match each attribute to its category: life (none), power and will (influence), knowledge, hearing, sight (disclosure), speech (indication).
Note that power and will have a potential (ṣulūḥī) eternal attachment, not actual.
4
Grasp the significance of these distinctions
Reflect on why this matters: it shows that not all divine attributes relate to their objects in the same way. Some produce effects, some reveal, some indicate.
This classification helps avoid oversimplifying God's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: الْمُتَعَلَّقَتَانِ) أي: تعلُّقاً صُلوحياً قديماً لا تنجيزياً قديماً أو حادثاً؛ لأنهما لا يتعلقان بجميع الممكنات التعلق المذكور، والمراد بالتعلق: اقتضاء الصفة واستلزامها أمراً زائداً على الذات. واعلم أن صفاتِ المعاني منها ما لا يتعلق أصلاً وهي الحياة، ومنها ما يتعلق تعلق تأثير وهو القدرة والإرادة بناء على ما هو المختار من أن التخصيص تأثير، ومنها ما يتعلق تعلق انكشاف وهو: العِلْمُ والسمع والبصر، ومنها ما يتعلق تعلق دلالة: وهو الكلامُ كما يُعْلَمُ من تتبع كلام المصنف. فتلخص أن لها بالنسبة لذلك أقساماً أربعة.
His saying: 'the two attachments' – meaning a potential (ṣulūḥī) eternal attachment, not an actual (tanjīzī) eternal or temporal attachment, because they (the two attributes) are not attached to all possibles with the mentioned attachment. And what is meant by attachment is the attribute's requiring and entailing an additional matter beyond the essence. And know that the attributes of meaning (ṣifāt al-ma'ānī) include: that which does not attach at all – and it is life; that which attaches with an attachment of influence – and it is power and will, based on the chosen view that specification (takhṣīṣ) is influence; that which attaches with an attachment of disclosure – and it is knowledge, hearing, and sight; and that which attaches with an attachment of indication – and it is speech, as can be known from following the words of the author. So it is summarized that they have, relative to that, four divisions.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage explains the classification of the seven attributes of meaning (ṣifāt al-ma'ānī) – life, power, will, knowledge, hearing, sight, speech – based on how they 'attach' (ta'alluq) to objects. First, attachment is defined as the attribute requiring something additional beyond the essence. The phrase 'the two attachments' refers to the two attributes (power and will) that have an eternal potential attachment, not an actual one, because they do not attach to all possibles in an actual way. Then the classification: (1) Life does not attach at all because it is a condition for other attributes. (2) Power and will attach with an attachment of influence (ta'thīr) because they bring about existence, with the note that specification (takhṣīṣ) is considered a form of influence. (3) Knowledge, hearing, and sight attach with an attachment of disclosure (inkishāf) because they reveal the known. (4) Speech attaches with an attachment of indication (dalāla) because it indicates meanings. Thus, the seven attributes are divided into four categories based on the nature of their attachment.

Misconception: All attributes of meaning attach to their objects in the same way.
Correction: This passage shows that life has no attachment, power and will attach via influence, knowledge/hearing/sight via disclosure, and speech via indication. They are fundamentally different.

?
Speech attaches by indication: every word of the Quran is a direct sign from your Lord. When you open the Quran, do you approach it as a casual text or as a living indication calling you to account? What change will you make in your recitation this week to show you believe this?

The Four Cases of Divine Knowledge and Belief
Al-Bajuri enumerates four cases of belief and disbelief based on divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four cases of divine knowledge and belief as exemplified by Abu Jahl and Abu Bakr.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge encompasses all outcomes without compelling them.

Al-Bajuri unpacks a dense theological point: God's knowledge of good and evil does not force His command or will. He illustrates this with four cases of belief and disbelief, using the examples of Abu Jahl and Abu Bakr.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the English translation. Notice the structure: it lists four cases starting with 'fa-al-awwal...' (the first is), 'al-thani...' (the second is), etc.
The passage is a continuation of a previous statement about God's will and command.
2
Identify the four cases
Label each case: Case 1: disbelief (kufr) that God's knowledge is attached to, like Abu Jahl. Case 2: faith (iman) of the one mentioned. Case 3: faith that God's knowledge is attached to, like Abu Bakr. Case 4: disbelief of the one mentioned.
The 'one mentioned' refers to a person discussed earlier in the text.
3
Reflect on the distinction
Ask yourself: does God's knowledge of someone's disbelief make that person's disbelief inevitable? The passage shows that God knows, but He does not command or will evil. The examples illustrate that both types of belief and disbelief occur within divine knowledge.
This distinction is crucial for understanding divine justice and human responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يأمر به، فالأول كما في كفر من تعلق علم الله بكفره كأبي جهل، والثاني كما في إيمان من ذكر، والثالث كما في إيمان من تعلق علم الله بإيمانه كأبي بكر، والرابع كما في كفر من ذكر.
And He does not command it. So the first is like the disbelief of one whose disbelief God's knowledge is attached to, like Abu Jahl; and the second is like the faith of the one mentioned; and the third is like the faith of one whose faith God's knowledge is attached to, like Abu Bakr; and the fourth is like the disbelief of the one mentioned.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the earlier statement 'ولم يرد إلا الخير ولا يأمر به' (He only wills good and does not command evil). Al-Bajuri explains that there are four cases regarding divine knowledge and its relation to belief and disbelief. The first case is disbelief that God knows will occur, exemplified by Abu Jahl. The second is faith of a person who was mentioned earlier (presumably from the previous context). The third is faith that God knows will occur, exemplified by Abu Bakr. The fourth is disbelief of the person mentioned. The key point is that divine knowledge encompasses all outcomes, but the actual command and will are distinct.

Misconception: God's knowledge forces a person to act, so they are not responsible for their belief or disbelief.
Correction: God's knowledge only encompasses what will happen; it does not compel the action. The person is still responsible for their choice. The examples of Abu Jahl and Abu Bakr show that divine knowledge coexists with human responsibility.

Key Takeaway

Attributing Evil to God's Will: Context Matters
Al-Bajuri discusses the permissibility of attributing evil to God's will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic theological position on whether it is permissible to attribute evil actions to God's will, and the distinction between teaching and other contexts.

Core Idea
Context matters: attributing evil to God's will is allowed in teaching but not otherwise.

This lesson addresses a sensitive theological question: can we say that God wills sinful acts? Al-Bajuri presents the scholarly disagreement and then clarifies the correct view, which hinges on the context in which such statements are made. Understanding this distinction protects against misunderstanding God's nature while allowing for proper theological instruction.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and Translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the concise structure: disagreement, opinions, then the correct view.
The Arabic uses key terms like شرور and قبائح.
2
Identify the Two Opinions and the Correct View
The passage mentions that some permitted attributing evil to God's will, others prohibited it. Al-Bajuri states the correct view: a distinction between teaching contexts and others.
3
Reflect on the Rationale
Consider why the teaching context is special. In teaching, one explains that God's will encompasses all things, including evil, without implying approval. In other contexts, such statements could cause misunderstanding.
This connects to the broader doctrine of qadar (divine decree).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلف في جواز إسناد الشرور والقبائح إلى إرادة المولى، كأن يقال: أراد الله زنا زيد وكفر عمرو، فأجازه بعضهم، ومنعه آخرون، والصحيح: التفرقة بين مقام التعليم وغيره، فيجوز في الأول، ويمتنع في الثاني.
And there is disagreement on the permissibility of attributing evils and ugly acts to the will of the Master, such as saying: 'God willed Zayd's fornication and 'Amr's disbelief.' Some permitted it, others prohibited it. The correct view is the distinction between the context of instruction and other contexts; so it is permissible in the first and prohibited in the second.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage addresses a theological issue: can we say that God wills evil actions like fornication and disbelief? Scholars disagreed. Al-Bajuri states the correct view: it depends on the context. In a teaching context (maqam al-ta'leem), where one is explaining that everything occurs by God's will, it is permissible to attribute such actions to God's will. But in other contexts, it is not permissible because it might imply that God loves evil or commands it, which is not the case.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think it is always forbidden to attribute evil to God's will.
Correction: The passage clarifies that in a teaching context, it is allowed to attribute evil to God's will in order to affirm divine sovereignty.

Checkpoint

B) In the context of teaching

D) Never

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of attributing evil to God's will depends on context: it is allowed in teaching contexts to affirm divine sovereignty, but prohibited elsewhere.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Two Attached Ones and the Four Types of Attributes
Al-Bajuri explains the meaning of 'muta'alliqatan' and classifies divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of 'muta'alliqatan' as an eternal existential attachment and the fourfold classification of divine attributes of meanings.

Core Idea
Attributes either attach to objects or not, and attachment has different modes.

Al-Bajuri clarifies a key term in divine attributes: 'muta'alliqatan'. He explains that this attachment is eternal and existential, not temporal. Then he classifies attributes of meanings into four types based on how they attach to their objects. This classification helps us understand the nature of divine attributes and their relation to creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get familiar with the terms, then read the translation to understand the meaning.
Notice the technical terms like 'ta'alliq' and 'iqtida'.
2
Understand the definition of 'attachment'
Focus on al-Bajuri's definition of 'ta'alliq' as the attribute's requirement and entailment of something additional to the essence. This is the foundation for the classification.
Attachment here does not imply change in God.
3
Identify the four types and their examples
List the four categories: no attachment (life), attachment of effect (power, will), disclosure (knowledge, hearing, sight), and indication (speech). Note the reasoning for each.
Power and will are considered effect because specification is an effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: المتعلقتان أي تعلقاً صلاحياً قديماً لا تنجيزياً قديماً أو حادثاً؛ لأنهما لا يتعلقان بجميع الممكنات التعلق المذكور، والمراد بالتعلق: اقتضاء الصفة واستلزامها أمراً زائداً على الذات. واعلم أن صفات المعاني منها ما لا يتعلق أصلاً وهي الحياة، ومنها ما يتعلق تعلق تأثير وهو القدرة والإرادة بناء على ما هو المختار من أن التخصيص تأثير، ومنها ما يتعلق تعلق انكشاف وهو العلم والسمع والبصر، ومنها ما يتعلق تعلق دلالة وهو الكلام كما يعلم من تتبع كلام المصنف. فتلخص أن لها بالنسبة لذلك أقساماً أربعة.
His statement: 'the two attached ones' meaning an eternal existential attachment, not a temporal determining attachment old or new, because they do not attach to all possibles in the mentioned attachment. And the intended meaning of attachment is the attribute's requirement and entailment of something additional to the essence. And know that among the attributes of meanings: some do not attach at all, which is life; and some attach with an attachment of effect, which are power and will, based on the chosen view that specification is an effect; and some attach with an attachment of disclosure, which are knowledge, hearing, and sight; and some attach with an attachment of indication, which is speech, as is known from following the author's words. So it is summarized that they have, in this respect, four divisions.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification section. First, al-Bajuri explains the term 'muta'alliqatan' (the two attached ones) referring to two attributes that have attachment to objects. He clarifies that this attachment is an eternal existential attachment, not a temporal determining attachment. Then he defines 'attachment' (ta'alliq) as the attribute's requirement (iqtida') and entailment (istilzam) of something additional to the essence. Next, he classifies the attributes of meanings (sifat al-ma'ani) into four types: 1) Those with no attachment at all: life. 2) Those with attachment of effect (ta'alliq ta'thir): power and will, because specification (takhsis) is considered an effect. 3) Those with attachment of disclosure (ta'alliq inkishaf): knowledge, hearing, sight. 4) Those with attachment of indication (ta'alliq dalala): speech. This classification is based on the author's (Sanusi's) usage.

Correction: Al-Bajuri clarifies that the attachment is eternal and existential, and it is merely the attribute's requirement of something additional, not a change in God.

The Relational Meaning of 'Additional to the Essence'
Al-Ajhuri glosses 'amran za'idan' as the attribute's relational effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the phrase 'something additional to the essence' in divine attributes refers to the attribute's specific relational effect on its objects.

Core Idea
Attributes are defined by their effects, not as separate entities.

Al-Ajhuri clarifies a subtle point in the discussion of divine attributes. When scholars say an attribute is 'something additional to the essence,' they mean the attribute's specific function—like bringing into existence for will. This short gloss shows how each attribute's 'addition' is tied to its effect.

Guided Path
1
Read the gloss and translation
Read al-Ajhuri's Arabic gloss and its english translation to see the original phrasing.
2
Identify the mapping for each attribute
Notice how the 'additional matter' is specified for will, knowledge/hearing/sight, and speech.
3
Understand the relational nature
Reflect that the 'additional something' is not a separate entity but the attribute's effect or relation.
This corrects a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري (قوله: أمراً زائداً على الذات) عبارة غيره على قيامها بالذات، والمراد بذلك الأمر: الإيجاد والإعدام بالنسبة للإرادة والانكشاف بالنسبة للعلم والسمع والبصر والدلالة بالنسبة للكلام.
Gloss of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri: His statement: 'something additional to the essence' is an expression of others for their subsisting by the essence. And the intended meaning by that matter is: bringing into existence and annihilation with respect to will, and disclosure with respect to knowledge, hearing, and sight, and indication with respect to speech.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This is a short explanatory gloss. Al-Ajhuri comments on al-Bajuri's phrase 'amran za'idan 'ala al-dhat' (something additional to the essence). He says this phrase is equivalent to others saying 'their subsistence by the essence' (qiyamuha bi al-dhat). Then he specifies what that 'additional matter' is for each attribute: for will, it is bringing into existence (ijad) and annihilation (i'dam); for knowledge, hearing, and sight, it is disclosure (inkishaf); for speech, it is indication (dalala). This clarifies that the 'additional something' is the effect or the relation that the attribute has to its objects.

إيجاد

انكشاف

Misconception: Some might think the 'additional something' is a separate entity added to the essence.
Correction: The passage shows it refers to the attribute's relational effect—bringing into existence, disclosure, or indication—not a separate entity.

Checkpoint

A) Disclosure
B) Indication
C) Bringing into existence and annihilation
D) Subsistence

Key Takeaway
The 'something additional to the essence' in divine attributes is the attribute's specific relational effect on its objects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Al-Anbabi's Analysis on the Attachment of Power and Will
Al-Anbabi addresses an objection about power and will not attaching to all possibles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how power and will attach to all possibles on a substitute basis and the debate over whether specification is an effect.

Core Idea
Power and will attach to all possibles, but on a substitute basis — not simultaneously.

This passage from Al-Anbabi's gloss addresses a subtle objection about the attachment of divine power and will. He clarifies that the attachment is on a substitute basis, meaning either to existence or non-existence, and then touches on the debate about specification. Understanding this requires careful attention to the terms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: an objection, a proposed interpretation, and a citation.
The passage contains glosses on two separate statements from the main text.
2
Identify the two key points
Identify the first key point: power and will attach on a substitute basis. Identify the second: the disagreement on whether specification (takhsis) is an effect (ta'thir) or not.
These are two distinct but related discussions in kalam.
3
Reflect on the significance
Reflect on why these distinctions matter: they clarify how divine attributes relate to creation without implying change or multiple simultaneous determinations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبايي (قوله: لأنهما لا يتعلقان بجميع الممكنات) قد يقال: المعنى إنهما يتعلقان بجميع الممكنات تعلقاً تنجيزياً قديماً أو حادثاً، لكن على البدل، سواء كان على وجه الإيجاد أو الإعدام، وكأنهم فهموا أن التعلق لا بد وأن يكون على جهة الإيجاد. كذا قيل. وهذا لا يتضح إلا لو جعل تعلق القبضة من التنجيزي. (قوله: على ما هو المختار) في 'شرح منقذة العبيد' للشيخ الجوهري: واختلف هل التخصيص تأثير أو لا؟ على أقوال ثالثها: أنه تأثير في التمييز لا في الوجود.
Annotations of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi: His statement: 'because they do not attach to all possibles' — it may be said: the meaning is that they attach to all possibles with a determining attachment, eternal or temporal, but on a substitute basis, whether in the mode of bringing into existence or annihilation. And it is as if they understood that attachment must be in the mode of bringing into existence. Thus it is said. And this is not clear unless the attachment of the grasp is made from the determining. His statement: 'based on what is chosen' — in 'Sharh Munqidhat al-'Ubayd' by al-Shaykh al-Jawhari: there is disagreement on whether specification (takhsis) is an effect or not? Among the sayings, the third is that it is an effect in discrimination not in existence.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is a gloss by al-Anbabi addressing a potential objection to an earlier statement that power and will do not attach to all possibles in the mentioned attachment. Al-Anbabi suggests a possible interpretation: they do attach to all possibles, but on a substitute (badal) basis, meaning they attach to either existence or non-existence alternatively, not simultaneously. Some scholars understood that attachment must always be towards bringing into existence, which would be problematic. Al-Anbabi says this interpretation is not clear unless the attachment of the 'grasp' (qabda) is considered determining (tanjizi). Then al-Anbabi cites a discussion from another work about whether specification (takhsis) is an effect, with three opinions, the third being that it is an effect in discrimination (tamyiz) not in existence (wujud). These are fine points in kalam about the nature of divine attributes.

Key Terms
تعلق تنجيزي
على البدل
تخصيص
تأثير
تمييز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think power and will have no attachment at all to certain possibles.
Correction: They attach to all possibles on a substitute basis, meaning they determine either existence or non-existence alternately.

Checkpoint
How do power and will attach to all possibles according to one interpretation?
A) Simultaneously
B) On a substitute basis
C) Not at all
D) Only to existent things

Key Takeaway
Divine power and will attach to all possibles on a substitute basis, meaning they bring about either existence or non-existence, not both simultaneously.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's power and will attach to all possibilities on a substitute basis, meaning He brings about some into existence and others not, then what does that mean for the things you desperately want but have not received? Are you willing to trust that God's 'substitute' choice is perfect, even when it denies you what you desire? What would change in your prayers if you truly believed God's attachment is always appropriate, not arbitrary?
?
The passage mentions that specification is an effect in discrimination, not necessarily in existence. How does that affect your understanding of why God created some people, situations, or trials and not others? Do you ever secretly believe that God 'owes' you existence in certain forms? Are you ready to submit to His discrimination without demanding an explanation?
?
If you had to live today fully convinced that God's power and will are actively attached to every possible outcome in your life on a substitute basis, but only one outcome will materialize—would you still feel secure? Does your daily anxiety reveal a lack of trust in God's active, determining attachment? What would it take for you to rest in His substitute choice?

Refuting the Claim That God Can Take a Son
Al-Bajuri refutes the heretical claim about divine capacity to take a son using the story of Idris.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that God is capable of taking a son is false, based on the principle that divine power does not extend to impossibilities, and how the story of Idris and Iblis illustrates this.

Core Idea
God's power does not extend to the impossible, even if heretics misinterpret proofs.

This lesson addresses a heretical claim that God is capable of taking a son. The refutation relies on the established principle that power relates only to possible things. Al-Bajuri uses the story of Idris and Iblis to show that even a prophet's statement must be understood within rational bounds. As you read, notice how the commentator distinguishes between possible and impossible intents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Pay attention to the story of Idris and Iblis.
The story is used as an analogy.
2
Identify the heretical claim and its refutation
Find where the heretic's argument is presented and how the commentator dismantles it.
3
Understand the correct interpretation of the story
The commentator clarifies what Idris actually meant. Compare it with the heretic's misunderstanding.
This shows the importance of correct interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا يُعْلَمُ سقوط قول بعض المبتدعة : إن الله قادِرٌ على أن يتَّخِذَ ولداً؛ إذ لو لم يَقْدِرُ عليه لكان عاجزاً، وكأنه أَخَذَه من قصة إدريس مع إبليس، وهي أن إدريس كان يَخِيطُ حُلَّةً وهو يقول في دخول الإبرة وخروجها: سبحان الله والحمد لله، فجاءه إبليس في صورة إنسان بقشرة بيضة - وقيل بقشرة فستقة - وقال: هل الله يَقْدِرُ أن يجعل الدنيا في هذه القشرة؟ فقال: الله يَقْدِرُ أن يجعل الدنيا في سَم هذه الإبرة - أي: خَرْقِها ، ونَخَسَ إحدى عينيه، فصار أعور - قال بعضهم وأرجو أن تكون اليمنى ، واختار نخس إحدى عينيه ليُطْفِئَ نور بصره كما أراد أن يطفئ نور الإيمان، فإن الجزاء من جنس العمل. ووجه الأخذ أنه تُوُهِمَ أن مرادَ إدريس أن الله يَقْدِرُ أن يجعل الدنيا بهيئتها التي هي عليها في القشرة المذكورة بهيئتها التي هي عليها، مع أن هذا مستحيل؛ لاستحالة اجتماع الأجسام الكثيفة في حَيّز واحد، وليس هذا مراداً. بل المراد: أن الله يصغر الدنيا جداً أو يُكَتِرُ القشرة كذلك، ويجعل هذه في هذه، وليس بمستحيل، وإنما لم يُصَرّح له إدريس بذلك؛ لأنه سائل مُتَعَنِّت قبحه الله.
And by this it is known that the saying of some innovators—that God is capable of taking a son—falls away; for if He were not capable of it, He would be incapable. And it seems he took it from the story of Idris with Iblis. It is that Idris was sewing a garment while saying at the entry and exit of the needle: 'Glory be to God and praise be to God.' Then Iblis came to him in the form of a human with an eggshell—and it is said with a pistachio shell—and said: 'Is God capable of putting the world in this shell?' He said: 'God is capable of putting the world in the eye of this needle'—i.e., its hole—and he poked one of his eyes, so he became one-eyed. Some said, and I hope it was the right eye, and he chose to poke one of his eyes to extinguish the light of his sight just as he wanted to extinguish the light of faith, for the recompense is of the same kind as the deed. And the point of his objection is that it was imagined that Idris meant that God is capable of putting the world in its existing form into the mentioned shell in its existing form, while this is impossible due to the impossibility of dense bodies occupying a single space, and this was not the intent. Rather, the intent is that God makes the world very small or enlarges the shell likewise, and puts this into that, and this is not impossible. Idris did not clarify to him because he was a stubborn questioner—may God curse him.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage refutes a heretical claim that God is capable of taking a son. The commentator states that it is known from the previous discussion (about power not relating to impossibilities) that such a claim is false. The heretic argues that if God were not capable, He would be incapable. The commentator traces this claim to a misunderstanding of the story of Idris and Iblis. In the story, Iblis asks Idris if God can put the world in an eggshell. Idris replies that God can put it in the eye of a needle, and pokes out Iblis's eye. The heretic misinterpreted Idris's statement to mean that God can put the world in its current size into the shell, which is impossible because dense bodies cannot occupy the same space. The correct interpretation is that God can either make the world tiny or enlarge the shell and then place one inside the other; this is not impossible. Idris did not explain this because Iblis was a stubborn questioner. The story illustrates that divine power does not extend to impossible things.

Key Terms
القدرة
المستحيل
الولد
إدريس
إبليس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the story of Idris implies God can do the impossible.
Correction: The correct interpretation is that God can make the world very small or enlarge the shell, which is possible; the story actually shows that divine power does not apply to impossibilities.

Checkpoint
What is the correct interpretation of Idris's statement about putting the world in a needle's eye?
A) God can put the world in its current form into the eye of a needle
B) God can make the world very small or enlarge the shell and put one inside the other
C) God cannot put the world in any form into a small space
D) Idris was mistaken about God's power

Key Takeaway
The claim that God can take a son is false because it implies an impossibility; the story of Idris and Iblis illustrates the correct understanding of divine power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
