?
The heretic's argument seems logical but is actually a trap. In what ways do you find yourself using flawed logic to justify your desires, and how can you submit to what God has revealed?
?
Idris did not waste time arguing with Iblis because he was a stubborn questioner. When you encounter people who argue solely to defy God, how do you respond? Do you engage fruitlessly or do you trust that the truth stands?

Defining Divine Knowledge and Refuting al-Sa'd's Definition
Al-Sanusi defines knowledge and critiques al-Sa'd's definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of divine knowledge according to al-Sanusi and why al-Sa'd's alternative definition is rejected due to its problematic implications.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge cannot be defined as mere disclosure, as that implies prior ignorance.

In this passage, al-Sanusi gives a precise definition of divine knowledge, and al-Bajuri defends it by critiquing a competing definition from al-Sa'd al-Taftazani. The critique highlights three logical flaws, showing how careful terminology is essential in theology. You'll see how even a small phrase can lead to serious errors about God.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions
First, read al-Sanusi's definition of knowledge and compare it to al-Sa'd's definition. Note the key difference: the phrase 'without prior concealment' versus 'disclosure'.
The term 'disclosure' is the focal point of criticism.
2
Examine the three objections
Identify each of the three objections against al-Sa'd's definition. They all revolve around the implications of 'disclosure' and 'the known'.
Pay attention to the logical argument about circularity (dawr).
3
Understand the commentary expansions
Read al-Ajhuri's and al-Anbabi's notes. Al-Ajhuri elaborates on the circularity objection, and al-Anbabi clarifies the scope of the definition to exclude hearing, sight, and speech.
These clarifications show the scholarly rigor applied to definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
متن أم البراهين لأبي عبد الله السنوسي
(قوله: وَالْعِلْمُ) هي صفةٌ وجودية قائمة بذاته تعالى تتعلق بالشيء على وجه الإحاطة على ما هو به دون سبق خفاءٍ. كذا قال الكمال)، وهو أَحْسَنُ مما قاله السَّعْدُ (۳) وغيره من أنه: صفةً وجودية قائمة بذاته تعالى يَنْكَشِفُ بها المعلوم على ما هو به؛ لأنه قد اعتُرِضَ عليه بوجوه منها : أن التعبير بالانكشاف يُوهِمُ سَبقَ الخفاء؛ لأنه ظهورُ الشيء بعد خفائه، وذلك يقتضي سَبْقَ الجهل، وهو مُحَالٌ عليه تعالى. ومنها: أن التعبير بالمعلوم يقتضي أن صِفَةً المعلومية ثابتة له قبل الانكشاف مع أنها لا تَثْبُتُ له إلا بعده، وإلا لكان انكشافه تحصيلاً للحاصل، وهو مُحالٌ. ومنها: أن المعلومَ مُشْتَقُ من العلم والمشتق متوقف على المشتق منه، ومن المقرر : أن المعرَّفَ متوقف على تعريفه، وقد أُخِذَ فِيهِ مَا هُوَ مُتَوَقِّفٌ عَلَيْهِ، فأدى الأمر إلى أن كلّاً منهما متوقف على الآخر، وهو دَوْرٌ.
حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري .
(قوله: وَقَدْ أُخِذَ فِيهِ مَا هُوَ مُتَوَقِّفٌ عَلَيْهِ وهو المشتق الذي هو معلوم، ومعنى توقف المعلوم على العلم أنه لا يتصور المعلومُ إلا بتصور العلم؛ لأن تصور المشتق لا يمكن بدون تصور المشتق منه والفرضُ أن العلم لا يتصور إلا بتصور تعريفه، فصار تصور العلم متوقفاً على تصور التعريف وتصور التعريف باعتبار بعض أجزائه متوقف على تصور المعرف، وهو العلم، وهذا دور.
. تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي .
(قوله: تَتَعَلَّقُ بِالشَّيْء) لعلَّ المراد بكلّ شيءٍ موجود أو معدوم ليخرج السمعُ والبصر، وإلا كان تعريفاً بالأعم.
(قوله: مِنْهَا ... إلخ) ومنها: أنه غيرُ مانع لدخول السمع والبصر والكلام في التعريف. وأجيب عن هذا بأن (أل) في المعلوم للاستغراق، فخرج السمع والبصر، والمراد: الانكشاف لمن قام به الوصف فقط، فخرج به الكلام؛ لأنه يُكْشَفُ به للسامع.
(1) الكمال: محمد بن محمد ابن أبي شريف المقدسي، أبو المعالي كمال الدين، من فقهاء الشافعية، من أهل بيت المقدس مولداً ووفاةً له: "الفرائد في حل شرح العقائد" توفي سنة (٩٠٦هـ). (۲) السعد: مسعود بن عمر التفتازاني سعد الدين من أئمة العربية والبيان والمنطق، ولد بتفتازان من بلاد خراسان وتوفي بسمرقند، ودفن في سرخس له : شرح العقائد النسفية"، توفي سنة (٧٩٣هـ).
Text of Umm al-Barahin by Abu 'Abd Allah al-Sanusi.
(His saying: KNOWLEDGE) It is an existent attribute subsisting in His Essence, may He be exalted, related to a thing in a comprehensive manner as it is, without prior concealment. Thus said al-Kamal, and it is better than what al-Sa'd (3) and others said: that it is an existent attribute subsisting in His Essence, may He be exalted, by which the known object is disclosed as it is; because it was objected to it in several ways: among them, that the expression 'disclosing' suggests prior concealment, because it is the appearance of a thing after its concealment, and that necessitates prior ignorance, which is impossible for Him, exalted. And among them, that the expression 'the known' necessitates that the attribute of being known is established for it before disclosure, while it is not established for it except after it; otherwise its disclosure would be a realization of what is already realized, which is impossible. And among them, that 'the known' is derived from knowledge, and the derived term depends on the root, and it is established that the defined depends on its definition, and what it depends on was taken in it, so the matter leads to each of them depending on the other, which is a vicious circle.
Commentary of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri:
(His saying: AND WHAT IT DEPENDS ON WAS TAKEN IN IT) meaning the derived term which is 'known', and the meaning of the dependence of 'known' on knowledge is that 'known' is not conceivable except by conceiving knowledge, because the derived term cannot be imagined without the root; and the assumption is that knowledge is not conceivable except by conceiving its definition, so the conceiving of knowledge becomes dependent on the conceiving of the definition, and the conceiving of the definition by virtue of some of its parts depends on the conceiving of the defined, which is knowledge, and this is a vicious circle.
Annotations of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi:
(His saying: IS RELATED TO THE THING) Perhaps what is meant is by every existent or non-existent thing to exclude hearing and sight, otherwise it would be a definition by something more general.
(His saying: FROM THEM... etc.) And among them: that it does not prevent the inclusion of hearing, sight, and speech in the definition. And it was answered that (al) in 'the known' is for generality, so hearing and sight are excluded, and the intended meaning is: the disclosure only for the one in whom the attribute subsists, so speech is excluded because it is disclosed to the hearer.
(1) al-Kamal: Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn Abi Sharif al-Maqdisi, Abu al-Ma'ali Kamal al-Din, a Shafi'i jurist, from Jerusalem, author of 'al-Fara'id fi Hall Sharh al-'Aqa'id', died 906 AH. (2) al-Sa'd: Mas'ud ibn 'Umar al-Taftazani, Sa'd al-Din, an authority on Arabic, rhetoric, and logic, born in Taftazan in Khorasan, died in Samarqand, buried in Sarakhs, author of 'Sharh al-'Aqa'id al-Nasafiyya', died 793 AH.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage presents the definition of divine knowledge (al-'ilm) from al-Sanusi's text and critiques an alternative definition by al-Sa'd al-Taftazani. First, the author gives al-Sanusi's definition: knowledge is an existent attribute subsisting in God's essence, comprehensively encompassing the known object as it is, without prior concealment. He notes that this definition by al-Kamal ibn Abi Sharif is superior to al-Sa'd's definition, which uses the term 'disclosure' (inkishaf). Three objections are raised against al-Sa'd's definition: 1) 'Disclosure' implies prior concealment and thus prior ignorance, which is impossible for God. 2) 'The known' implies that the property of being known exists before disclosure, but it only comes after disclosure; otherwise disclosure would be redundant. 3) The term 'known' is derived from knowledge, leading to a circularity if it is used in the definition of knowledge. Then al-Ajhuri's commentary expands on the circularity objection. Finally, al-Anbabi notes that the phrase 'related to the thing' should be understood as every existent or nonexistent thing to exclude hearing and sight from the definition, and that the inclusion of speech is avoided because speech is disclosed to the hearer, not to the one who possesses speech. This guards against the misconception that divine knowledge can be simply defined as disclosure without accounting for the implications of the terms used.

العلم
الصفة الوجودية
الإحاطة
الانكشاف
المشتق
الدور
المعلوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that defining divine knowledge as 'disclosure' is harmless.
Correction: This passage shows it leads to impossible consequences such as implying prior ignorance, redundancy, and circularity.

Checkpoint
Why is al-Sa'd's definition of knowledge criticized in this passage?
A) Because it implies that knowledge is not an attribute.
B) Because the term 'disclosure' suggests prior ignorance.
C) Because it fails to exclude hearing and sight.
D) Because it defines knowledge by itself.

Key Takeaway
Al-Sanusi's definition avoids the pitfalls of implying prior ignorance, redundancy, and circularity that plague al-Sa'd's definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The scholars labored to remove any implication of imperfection from God's attributes. In your own understanding of Allah's knowledge, have you ever unconsciously attributed limitation or ignorance to Him? What specific belief about God must you correct today to avoid such pitfalls?
?
The circularity argument shows that using the word 'known' in defining knowledge leads to a logical dead end. Are there concepts about Allah that you use casually or vaguely without realizing they might contradict sound theology? Will you commit to studying precise terms to honor Allah as He deserves?
?
If a single word like 'disclosure' can imply prior ignorance of Allah, how carefully have you examined the words you use when speaking about Him? What phrases about God's knowledge or actions do you need to reconsider to ensure they reflect His perfection?

Two Definitions of Divine Knowledge
Al-Bajuri presents two definitions and states his preference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Bajuri prefers al-Kamal's definition of divine knowledge over al-Sa'd's, and the key difference between them.

Core Idea
A definition that implies change in God is inferior to one that avoids it.

Al-Bajuri begins his discussion of divine knowledge by presenting two competing definitions. The first, from al-Kamal, emphasizes that knowledge relates to things without prior concealment. The second, from al-Sa'd, defines it as an attribute by which the known is disclosed. Al-Bajuri judges the first superior, and in this lesson we will see why.

Guided Path
1
Read the two definitions
Read the Arabic passage containing both definitions. Notice that the first definition includes the phrase 'without prior concealment' while the second uses 'disclosure'.
The first definition is attributed to al-Kamal, the second to al-Sa'd.
2
Identify the key difference
The crucial difference is that al-Kamal's definition explicitly negates prior concealment (dūn sabq khafā'), while al-Sa'd's uses the term 'disclosure' (inkishāf).
This subtle difference has significant theological implications.
3
Understand why al-Bajuri prefers the first
Al-Bajuri states explicitly that al-Kamal's definition is better (ahsan). The reason is that 'disclosure' can imply prior ignorance, which is impossible for God. The first definition avoids this by stating that knowledge occurs without prior concealment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَالْعِلْمُ) هي صفةٌ وجودية قائمة بذاته تعالى تتعلق بالشيء على وجه الإحاطة على ما هو به دون سبق خفاءٍ. كذا قال الكمال)، وهو أَحْسَنُ مما قاله السَّعْدُ (۳) وغيره من أنه: صفةً وجودية قائمة بذاته تعالى يَنْكَشِفُ بها المعلوم على ما هو به؛ لأنه قد اعتُرِضَ عليه بوجوه منها : أن التعبير بالانكشاف يُوهِمُ سَبقَ الخفاء؛ لأنه ظهورُ الشيء بعد خفائه، وذلك يقتضي سَبْقَ الجهل، وهو مُحَالٌ عليه تعالى. ومنها: أن التعبير بالمعلوم يقتضي أن صِفَةً المعلومية ثابتة له قبل الانكشاف مع أنها لا تَثْبُتُ له إلا بعده، وإلا لكان انكشافه تحصيلاً للحاصل، وهو مُحالٌ. ومنها: أن المعلومَ مُشْتَقُ من العلم والمشتق متوقف على المشتق منه، ومن المقرر : أن المعرَّفَ متوقف على تعريفه، وقد أُخِذَ فِيهِ ما هو متوقف عليه، فأدى الأمر إلى أن كلّاً منهما متوقف على الآخر، وهو دَوْرٌ. وأجيب عن هذه الأمور، لكن ما لا يحتاج لجواب أولى مما يحتاجُ له. وفي قولهم: تتعلق بالشيء.... إلخ أو ينكشف بها المعلوم... إلخ إشارة إلى تعلقه تعلقه التنجيزي القديم، وهو بالشيء بالفعل أزلاً، وليس له إلا هذا التعلق، فليس له تَعَلَّق صُلُوحِي قديم ولا تنجيزي حادث، خلافاً لمن زَعَمَ أن له ذلك لما يلزم عليه من اتصافه تعالى بالجهل، لكنه يتعلق بالشيء قبل وجوده على وجه أنه سيكون، وبعد وجوده على وجه أنه كان فالتعبيرُ بكان أو سيكون إنما هو باعتبار المعلوم لا باعتبار العلم.
His saying: 'And knowledge (al-ʿilm)' is an existent attribute (ṣifa wujūdiyya) subsisting in His Essence (dhātihi taʿālā), relating to a thing with complete encompassment as it is without prior concealment. Thus said al-Kamāl, and it is better than what al-Saʿd and others said, that it is: an existent attribute subsisting in His Essence by which the known object (al-maʿlūm) is disclosed (yunkashifu) as it is; because it is objected to on grounds, among them: that the expression 'disclosure' (inkishāf) suggests prior concealment, because it is the appearance of a thing after its concealment, and that entails prior ignorance (jahl), which is impossible (muḥāl) for Him. And among them: that the expression 'the known object' (al-maʿlūm) entails that the property of being known (ṣifat al-maʿlūmiyya) is established for it before the disclosure, while it is only established for it after it, otherwise its disclosure would be the acquisition of what is already obtained (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil), which is impossible. And among them: that 'the known object' is derived (mushtaqq) from 'knowledge' (al-ʿilm), and the derived term depends on the term from which it is derived, and it is established that the defined (al-muʿarraf) depends on its definition, and in it [the definition] what is dependent upon it has been taken, so it leads to each of them depending on the other, which is a circularity (dawr). And these matters have been answered, but what does not need an answer is better than what needs it. And in their saying: 'relates to a thing... etc.' or 'by which the known object is disclosed... etc.' there is an indication of its relation (taʿalluq) being the eternal actual relation (al-taʿalluq al-tanjīzī al-qadīm), and it is with the thing in actuality eternally, and it has no relation except this, so it has no ancient potential relation (taʿalluq ṣulūḥī qadīm) nor a temporal actual relation (tanjīzī ḥādith), contrary to those who claimed that it has that, because of what would follow of His being described with ignorance, but it relates to a thing before its existence in the manner that it will be, and after its existence in the manner that it was, so the expression 'was' or 'will be' is only according to the known object, not according to knowledge.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage defines the divine attribute of knowledge (al-ʿilm) and defends the preferred formulation against criticisms. The passage begins by giving the definition preferred by al-Kamāl: knowledge is an existent attribute subsisting in God's essence, relating to a thing with complete encompassment as it is, without prior concealment. This is contrasted with the definition of al-Saʿd and others, who defined it as an attribute by which the known object is disclosed. The author then lists three objections to al-Saʿd's definition. First, the term 'disclosure' suggests prior concealment, which would imply prior ignorance in God—impossible. Second, using 'the known object' in the definition implies that the property of being known exists before the disclosure, but it only arises after the disclosure, leading to a regress. Third, 'the known object' is derived from 'knowledge,' so defining knowledge by it involves circularity (dawr) because the definition depends on what is being defined. The passage then notes that these objections have been answered, but a definition needing no answer is preferable. The answer is briefly indicated: the term 'relates' or 'discloses' points to the eternal actual relation (al-taʿalluq al-tanjīzī al-qadīm) of God's knowledge to its objects. This relation is eternal and actual, not potential and then actual. The claim that God has both an ancient potential relation and a temporal actual relation is rejected because it would imply change in God. Instead, God's knowledge relates to a thing before its existence as 'it will be' and after as 'it was,' but this temporal description applies to the known object's state, not to knowledge itself, which is eternal. Thus the definition avoids implying any temporal change in God.

Key Terms
العلم (al-ʿilm)
صفة وجودية (ṣifa wujūdiyya)
الانكشاف (al-inkishāf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does al-Bajuri prefer al-Kamal's definition?
A) It is shorter and more concise.
B) It avoids implying prior concealment and thus ignorance in God.
C) It uses the term 'disclosure' which is more precise.
D) It was written earlier and has more authority.

Key Takeaway
Al-Bajuri prefers al-Kamal's definition because it explicitly negates prior concealment, avoiding any implication of ignorance in God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Al-Bajuri preferred a definition that avoids even the slightest implication of imperfection in God. What does your own mental concept of God's knowledge reflect about your belief in His perfection? Where might you need to purify your understanding?

Three Objections to al-Sa'd's Definition
Al-Bajuri enumerates three objections to al-Sa'd's definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three logical objections raised against al-Sa'd's definition of divine knowledge.

Core Idea
Three objections show how al-Sa'd's definition leads to logical contradictions.

Having stated his preference, al-Bajuri now lists three objections to al-Sa'd's definition. These objections highlight problems with the term 'disclosure' and the use of 'the known object'. Understanding these objections is crucial for appreciating why the preferred definition is superior.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three objections
Read the passage carefully and identify three distinct objections. Each starts with 'minhā' (among them).
2
Analyze the first objection
The first objection concerns the term 'disclosure' (inkishāf). It argues that this term implies prior concealment, which would mean God was ignorant before knowing—impossible.
3
Analyze the second objection
The second objection uses 'the known object' (al-maʿlūm) in the definition. It argues that this presupposes that the property of being known exists before the disclosure, which is circular and leads to 'taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil' (acquiring what is already obtained).
4
Analyze the third objection
The third objection points out that 'the known object' is derived from 'knowledge', so defining knowledge by it creates a circular definition (dawr).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَالْعِلْمُ) هي صفةٌ وجودية قائمة بذاته تعالى تتعلق بالشيء على وجه الإحاطة على ما هو به دون سبق خفاءٍ. كذا قال الكمال)، وهو أَحْسَنُ مما قاله السَّعْدُ (۳) وغيره من أنه: صفةً وجودية قائمة بذاته تعالى يَنْكَشِفُ بها المعلوم على ما هو به؛ لأنه قد اعتُرِضَ عليه بوجوه منها : أن التعبير بالانكشاف يُوهِمُ سَبقَ الخفاء؛ لأنه ظهورُ الشيء بعد خفائه، وذلك يقتضي سَبْقَ الجهل، وهو مُحَالٌ عليه تعالى. ومنها: أن التعبير بالمعلوم يقتضي أن صِفَةً المعلومية ثابتة له قبل الانكشاف مع أنها لا تَثْبُتُ له إلا بعده، وإلا لكان انكشافه تحصيلاً للحاصل، وهو مُحالٌ. ومنها: أن المعلومَ مُشْتَقُ من العلم والمشتق متوقف على المشتق منه، ومن المقرر : أن المعرَّفَ متوقف على تعريفه، وقد أُخِذَ فِيهِ ما هو متوقف عليه، فأدى الأمر إلى أن كلّاً منهما متوقف على الآخر، وهو دَوْرٌ. وأجيب عن هذه الأمور، لكن ما لا يحتاج لجواب أولى مما يحتاجُ له. وفي قولهم: تتعلق بالشيء.... إلخ أو ينكشف بها المعلوم... إلخ إشارة إلى تعلقه تعلقه التنجيزي القديم، وهو بالشيء بالفعل أزلاً، وليس له إلا هذا التعلق، فليس له تَعَلَّق صُلُوحِي قديم ولا تنجيزي حادث، خلافاً لمن زَعَمَ أن له ذلك لما يلزم عليه من اتصافه تعالى بالجهل، لكنه يتعلق بالشيء قبل وجوده على وجه أنه سيكون، وبعد وجوده على وجه أنه كان فالتعبيرُ بكان أو سيكون إنما هو باعتبار المعلوم لا باعتبار العلم.
His saying: 'And knowledge (al-ʿilm)' is an existent attribute (ṣifa wujūdiyya) subsisting in His Essence (dhātihi taʿālā), relating to a thing with complete encompassment as it is without prior concealment. Thus said al-Kamāl, and it is better than what al-Saʿd and others said, that it is: an existent attribute subsisting in His Essence by which the known object (al-maʿlūm) is disclosed (yunkashifu) as it is; because it is objected to on grounds, among them: that the expression 'disclosure' (inkishāf) suggests prior concealment, because it is the appearance of a thing after its concealment, and that entails prior ignorance (jahl), which is impossible (muḥāl) for Him. And among them: that the expression 'the known object' (al-maʿlūm) entails that the property of being known (ṣifat al-maʿlūmiyya) is established for it before the disclosure, while it is only established for it after it, otherwise its disclosure would be the acquisition of what is already obtained (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil), which is impossible. And among them: that 'the known object' is derived (mushtaqq) from 'knowledge' (al-ʿilm), and the derived term depends on the term from which it is derived, and it is established that the defined (al-muʿarraf) depends on its definition, and in it [the definition] what is dependent upon it has been taken, so it leads to each of them depending on the other, which is a circularity (dawr). And these matters have been answered, but what does not need an answer is better than what needs it. And in their saying: 'relates to a thing... etc.' or 'by which the known object is disclosed... etc.' there is an indication of its relation (taʿalluq) being the eternal actual relation (al-taʿalluq al-tanjīzī al-qadīm), and it is with the thing in actuality eternally, and it has no relation except this, so it has no ancient potential relation (taʿalluq ṣulūḥī qadīm) nor a temporal actual relation (tanjīzī ḥādith), contrary to those who claimed that it has that, because of what would follow of His being described with ignorance, but it relates to a thing before its existence in the manner that it will be, and after its existence in the manner that it was, so the expression 'was' or 'will be' is only according to the known object, not according to knowledge.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage defines the divine attribute of knowledge (al-ʿilm) and defends the preferred formulation against criticisms. The passage begins by giving the definition preferred by al-Kamāl: knowledge is an existent attribute subsisting in God's essence, relating to a thing with complete encompassment as it is, without prior concealment. This is contrasted with the definition of al-Saʿd and others, who defined it as an attribute by which the known object is disclosed. The author then lists three objections to al-Saʿd's definition. First, the term 'disclosure' suggests prior concealment, which would imply prior ignorance in God—impossible. Second, using 'the known object' in the definition implies that the property of being known exists before the disclosure, but it only arises after the disclosure, leading to a regress. Third, 'the known object' is derived from 'knowledge,' so defining knowledge by it involves circularity (dawr) because the definition depends on what is being defined. The passage then notes that these objections have been answered, but a definition needing no answer is preferable. The answer is briefly indicated: the term 'relates' or 'discloses' points to the eternal actual relation (al-taʿalluq al-tanjīzī al-qadīm) of God's knowledge to its objects. This relation is eternal and actual, not potential and then actual. The claim that God has both an ancient potential relation and a temporal actual relation is rejected because it would imply change in God. Instead, God's knowledge relates to a thing before its existence as 'it will be' and after as 'it was,' but this temporal description applies to the known object's state, not to knowledge itself, which is eternal. Thus the definition avoids implying any temporal change in God.

Key Terms
الانكشاف (al-inkishāf)
المعلوم (al-maʿlūm)
الدور (al-dawr)
تحصيل الحاصل (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'inkishaf' (disclosure) is a neutral term with no implication of prior concealment.
Correction: Al-Bajuri argues that 'inkishaf' inherently suggests prior concealment, which would imply ignorance in God. Therefore, it is not a neutral term for divine knowledge.

Checkpoint
What is the third objection to al-Sa'd's definition?
A) It is too long and complicated.
B) It creates a circular definition (dawr).
C) It implies God is ignorant.
D) It uses the word 'attribute' incorrectly.

Key Takeaway
Al-Sa'd's definition of knowledge is rejected because it leads to logical absurdities: implying prior ignorance, a regress, and circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The first objection warns that using 'disclosure' implies prior ignorance in God. When you think about God's knowledge, do you ever unconsciously imagine that He 'learns' your prayers or actions after they happen? How does this objection push you to purify your concept of God from any trace of temporal change?
?
The second objection shows that defining knowledge by the known object leads to the absurdity of acquiring what is already possessed. In what areas of your life do you treat God's knowledge as if it could increase or decrease? What specific thought or action can you change today to affirm that God's knowledge is eternally complete and unchanging?
?
The third objection reveals the danger of circular definitions. In your own understanding of Islamic theology, are you careful to base your beliefs on primary sources rather than fall into circular reasoning? How can this lesson's critique of al-Sa'd's definition serve as a reminder to be vigilant about logical consistency in your faith?

The Correct Doctrine: Eternal Actual Relation
Al-Bajuri explains the correct doctrine of divine knowledge and refutes alternative views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Bajuri defends a proper definition of divine knowledge and the doctrine of eternal actual relation.

Core Idea
God's knowledge is eternal and actual; it never was merely potential.

After raising objections, al-Bajuri notes that they have been answered, but a definition needing no answer is best. He then clarifies the correct understanding: God's knowledge has an eternal actual relation (ta'alluq tanjīzī qadīm) to its objects, not a potential relation that becomes actual. This safeguards divine immutability.

Guided Path
1
Note the preference for a definition needing no answer
Al-Bajuri states that a definition which does not require rebuttal is better than one that does. This reinforces his choice of al-Kamal's definition.
2
Understand the 'eternal actual relation'
The phrase 'relates to a thing' or 'by which the known object is disclosed' points to the eternal actual relation (al-ta'alluq al-tanjīzī al-qadīm). This means God's knowledge is always actively knowing everything.
This relation is eternal and unchanging.
3
Reject potential and temporal actual relations
Al-Bajuri denies that God's knowledge has a potential relation (ṣulūḥī) that later becomes actual, or a temporal actual relation. Such views would imply change and ignorance in God.
4
Apply temporal language to the known object, not to knowledge
When we say God knows something 'before' or 'after' its existence, these temporal expressions refer to the known object's state, not to God's knowledge, which is eternal.
This preserves the doctrine of divine immutability.

Correction: Al-Bajuri asserts God's knowledge is eternally actual, not potential. The sense of 'before' and 'after' applies to the known object, not to knowledge. So God always knows everything in the same way.

The Single Eternal Relation of Divine Knowledge
Al-Bajuri refutes potential relation in divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine knowledge has only one type of relation—eternal and actual—and why a potential relation is impossible.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge is eternally actual, not potential.

Al-Bajuri addresses objections about divine knowledge by clarifying its relation to things. He shows that the only true relation is an eternal actual one, and that any idea of a potential relation would imply imperfection in Allah. This passage not only defends the orthodox view but also illustrates how temporal language about knowledge is merely a reflection of the known, not a change in the knower.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
First, read the Arabic carefully, noting the technical terms. Then read the literal translation to grasp the plain meaning.
Pay attention to the terms 'تعلق تنجيزي قديم' and 'تعلق صلوحي'.
2
Identify the main claim
Al-Bajuri states that divine knowledge has only one relation: an eternal actual relation (al-ta'alluq al-tanjīzī al-qadīm). Find this phrase and see how he negates other possible relations.
He says 'ليس له إلا هذا التعلق' – 'it has no relation other than this.'
3
Understand the refutation
The author refutes the view that there could be a potential relation or a temporal actual relation. Note the consequence he mentions: that would imply jahl (ignorance) in Allah.
This is a strong reductio ad absurdum.
4
Analyze the temporal clarification
The passage ends by explaining why we speak of knowledge 'before' and 'after' existence. Notice that this is with respect to the known object, not the knowledge itself.
Thus, divine knowledge is unchanging.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجيب عن هذه الأمور، لكن ما لا يحتاج لجواب أولى مما يحتاج له. وفي قولهم: تتعلق بالشيء.... إلخ أو ينكشف بها المعلوم... إلخ إشارة إلى تعلقه تعلقه التنجيزي القديم، وهو بالشيء بالفعل أزلاً، وليس له إلا هذا التعلق، فليس له تَعَلَّق صُلُوحِي قديم ولا تنجيزي حادث، خلافاً لمن زَعَمَ أن له ذلك لما يلزم عليه من اتصافه تعالى بالجهل، لكنه يتعلق بالشيء قبل وجوده على وجه أنه سيكون، وبعد وجوده على وجه أنه كان فالتعبيرُ بكان أو سيكون إنما هو باعتبار المعلوم لا باعتبار العلم.
And it was answered concerning these matters, but that which does not need an answer is more appropriate than that which needs one. And in their statement: 'it relates to the thing... etc.' or 'by which the known is disclosed... etc.' is an indication of its relation, its eternal actual relation, and it is with the thing actually eternally, and it has no relation other than this, so it has no ancient potential relation nor a temporal actual relation, contrary to the one who claimed that it has that, because of what would necessitate upon him the attribute of ignorance for the Exalted, but it relates to the thing before its existence in the manner that it will be, and after its existence in the manner that it was, so the expression 'was' or 'will be' is only with respect to the known not with respect to knowledge.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal argument about the nature of divine knowledge. It begins by noting that some objections to the definition of knowledge have been answered, but it is better to avoid objections altogether. Then it points out that in the statement 'it relates to the thing... etc.' there is an indication that the relation of divine knowledge is an eternal actual relation (al-ta'alluq al-tanjīzī al-qadīm). This means that from eternity, knowledge is actually related to its object; there is no other type of relation. The author then negates two other possible relations: an ancient potential relation (talluq ṣulūḥī qadīm) and a temporal actual relation (tanjīzī ḥādith). He refutes the view (attributed to Fakhr al-Dīn al-Rāzī) that claims such relations exist, because that would imply ignorance in God. The passage concludes by clarifying that although we talk about knowledge before and after the object's existence—describing the object as 'will be' or 'was'—this is only with respect to the known thing, not with respect to the act of knowing itself, which is eternal and unchanging.

Key Terms
تعلق تنجيزي قديم
تعلق صلوحي
علم
معلوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some mistakenly think divine knowledge has a potential relation before the thing exists and an actual relation after.
Correction: This passage shows that divine knowledge is eternally actual; it does not have a potential or temporal relation. Such a view would imply ignorance in Allah.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, how many types of relation does divine knowledge have?
A) One eternal actual relation
B) Two: potential and actual
C) Three: ancient, potential, and temporal
D) Four: eternal, potential, actual, and temporal

Key Takeaway
Divine knowledge has only one relation, an eternal actual relation, and any notion of potentiality or change in it is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If God's knowledge of your life is unchanging and eternally actual, then every sin you commit is already known to Him from eternity. Does that thought fill you with fear of His justice or hope in His mercy? What will you do today to align your actions with His eternal knowledge?
?
The passage says that expressions like 'was' and 'will be' refer only to the known, not to knowledge. When you think about God knowing your future choices, do you unconsciously imagine His knowledge changing as future becomes present? How can you train your heart to affirm that His knowledge is one unchanging act, and what difference would that make in your trust in Him?

The Story of Ibn al-Shajari: God's Daily Affairs Explained
Anecdote illustrating that God's actions are manifestations of eternal knowledge and will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's daily actions are manifestations of His eternal knowledge and will, not new events.

Core Idea
God's actions are eternal manifestations, not new beginnings.

One of the challenges to the doctrine of uncreated divine attributes is the verse 'Every day He is upon some matter.' This story of a scholar, Ibn al-Shajari, who was asked what God is doing right now, provides a vivid answer: God manifests affairs that He has eternally known and willed. The anecdote, endorsed by the Prophet in a dream, clarifies that God's actions are not novel but are expressions of His eternal decree.

Guided Path
1
Read the anecdote carefully
Read the full Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the dramatic question and the response given in the dream.
The story serves as a practical illustration of a theological point.
2
Identify the key phrase
Focus on the response: 'شؤون يبديها ولا يبتديها'. Understand what each part means: 'yubdiha' (manifests) and 'la yabtadiha' (does not initiate).
This phrase is the core of the answer.
3
Connect to the verse
Relate the explanation to the Quranic verse 'كُلَّ يَوْمٍ هُوَ فِي شَأْنٍ'. See how the story interprets it as manifestation of eternal knowledge and will.
The verse does not imply change in God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فائدة: قام رجلٌ إلى ابن الشجري وهو على كرسيه للوعظ يقرأ تفسير قوله تعالى: كُلَّ يَوْمٍ هُوَ فِي شَأْنٍ ووقف على رأسه فقال: يا هذا، فما يَفْعَلُ ربُّك الآنَ؟ فسَكَتَ وبات مهموماً، فرأى المصطفى، فذكر له ذلك وسأله، فقال له: إن السائل لك الخَضِرُ، وإنه سيعودُ فقل له: شُؤُون يبديها ولا يبتديها، يَخْفِضُ أقواماً ويرفعُ آخرين فأصبح مسروراً، فأتاه وأعاد عليه السؤال، فأجابه بذلك، فقال له: صَلّ على من علمك، وانصرف مُسْرِعاً). والمراد بالشؤون: الأحوال. وقوله: يُبْدِيها، أي: يُظهرُها. وقوله: ولا يبتديها، أي: لا يستأنفُها عِلْماً، فمعنى قوله: كُلَّ يَوْمٍ هُوَ فِي شَأْنٍ: كلّ وقت هو في أمرٍ يُظْهِرُهُ على وفق علمه وإرادته أزلاً. فتدبر.
Benefit: A man stood up to Ibn al-Shajari while he was on his pulpit for preaching, reciting the interpretation of the saying of the Exalted: 'Every day He is upon some matter' (Surah al-Rahman, 29). And he stood at his head and said: 'O this one, what is your Lord doing now?' So he fell silent and spent the night worried. Then he saw the Chosen One (peace be upon him) in a dream, mentioned that to him, and asked him. He said: 'Indeed, the questioner is al-Khidr, and he will return; so say to him: "Affairs He manifests and does not initiate; He lowers some people and raises others."' So he woke up joyful. Then he came and repeated the question, and he answered him with that. Then he said: 'Bless the one who taught you,' and departed quickly. And the intended meaning of 'affairs' (shu'un) is states. And His statement: 'He manifests them' means He shows them. And His statement: 'He does not initiate them' means He does not begin them as knowledge. So the meaning of His saying: 'Every day He is upon some matter' is: every time He is upon an affair that He manifests in accordance with His knowledge and will eternally. So reflect.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is a narrative illustration (fa'ida) that complements the theological discussion. It tells a story about Ibn al-Shajari, a scholar known for eloquence. A man (who turns out to be al-Khidr) asks him what God is doing right now. Ibn al-Shajari is unable to answer and becomes distressed. In a dream, the Prophet instructs him to answer: 'Affairs He manifests and does not initiate.' This means that God’s actions are not new beginnings; they are manifestations of what He has already eternally known and willed. The passage then explains that 'shu'un' means states or conditions. God manifests these states in time, but He does not 'begin' them as new knowledge or will. The verse 'Every day He is upon a matter' is thus interpreted as meaning that at every moment, God brings forth some affair in accordance with His eternal knowledge and will. The story reinforces the earlier point about eternal divine knowledge: God's knowledge does not change; events unfold according to it.

يبديها

علم

The Objects of Divine Knowledge: Necessary, Possible, Impossible
Al-Bajuri defines the objects of divine knowledge as necessary, possible, and impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how divine knowledge relates to the three categories of existents: necessary, possible, and impossible, and that God knows even impossibilities as non-existent while comprehending their reality.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge embraces all things — even impossibilities.

In this passage, al-Bajuri clarifies that God's knowledge is not limited to actual existents. It extends to all rationally possible things as well as impossibilities. This teaches us that nothing is outside the scope of divine omniscience, including things that can never exist.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the complete Arabic passage once, then its literal translation to grasp the flow of the commentary.
Notice the parentheses indicate explanatory inserts typical of a sharh.
2
Identify the three categories
Al-Bajuri lists three objects of divine knowledge: wajibat (necessary), ja'izat (possible), mustahilat (impossible). Underline each term in the text.
These categories correspond to the rational judgments in kalam.
3
Understand how God knows each
For necessary things, God knows His own essence and attributes. For possible things, He knows His creation. For impossible things, He knows them as non-existent but also knows their conceptual reality.
This shows divine knowledge includes both existence and non-existence.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider that God's knowledge is eternal and all-encompassing. Nothing, even impossibilities, is beyond His awareness.
This underscores the absolute perfection of divine omniscience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: الْمُتَعَلَّقُ) أي: تعلُّقاً تنجيزياً قديماً فقط كما علمت. (قوله: بِجَمِيعِ الْوَاجِبَاتِ أي كذاته تعالى وصفاته الشاملة للعلم نفسه، فيعلمُ تعالى بعلمه أن له علماً. (قوله: وَالْجَائِزَاتِ) أي: كَخَلْقِهِ تعالى للأشياء. (وَالْمُسْتَحِيلَاتِ) أي: كشريكه تعالى، فيعلم أنه معدوم، وإنما تعلَّق بالواجبات والجائزات والمستحيلات. (قوله) فَيَعْلَمُ أَنَّهُ مَعْدُومٌ) ويعلم حقيقته بأن يعلم أن معنى الشريك: من يشاركه في الألوهية علماً شبيهاً بعلمنا التصوري لتعلقه بالمفردات.
(His saying: 'al-muta'allaq') i.e., an eternal actual relation only, as you have learned. (His saying: 'with all the necessary things') i.e., like His Essence, Exalted, and His attributes which include knowledge itself, so He knows by His knowledge that He has knowledge. (His saying: 'and the possible things') i.e., like His creation of things. (And the impossible things) i.e., like a partner to Him, Exalted, so He knows that it is non-existent. And it relates to the necessary, possible, and impossible. (His saying) 'so He knows that it is non-existent' and He knows its reality by knowing that the meaning of 'partner' is one who shares in divinity, a knowledge similar to our conceptual knowledge due to its relation to individual concepts.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is a definitional commentary on Sanusi's text. It explains that the term 'al-muta'allaq' refers specifically to the eternal actual relation of divine knowledge, reiterating the earlier point that there is only one kind of relation. Then it lists the three categories of things to which divine knowledge relates: necessary things (wajibat), such as God's own essence and attributes; possible things (ja'izat), such as God's creation; and impossible things (mustahilat), such as a partner to God. For impossible things, God knows them as non-existent, and He also knows their reality in a conceptual way, like our conceptual knowledge of the meaning of a partner. This emphasizes that divine knowledge encompasses all things, whether existent or non-existent, actual or potential, in an eternal manner.

The Story of the Questioner and the Meaning of 'Each Day He is in a Matter'
Al-Bajuri narrates a story illustrating the interpretation of Q. 55:29.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's every act is a manifestation of His eternal knowledge and will, not a new beginning in His knowledge.

Core Idea
God manifests affairs He has eternally known—He does not begin them anew.

This lesson uses a vivid story involving the Prophet's guidance to correct a misunderstanding about God's ongoing actions. The mysterious questioner, later identified as al-Khaḍir, prompts a clarification: God's 'every day matter' refers to events He brings forth according to His eternal knowledge, not changes in His essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the story and its explanation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice how the story sets up a theological puzzle about God's actions.
The questioner's identity as al-Khaḍir adds weight to the answer.
2
Identify the key phrase
Find the phrase 'shu'ūn yubdīhā wa lā yabtadīhā' in the commentary. Understand each word: shu'ūn (affairs), yubdīhā (He manifests), lā yabtadīhā (does not originate anew).
This phrase is the crux of the correct interpretation.
3
Connect to the verse
See how the explanation applies to the verse 'Every day He is in a matter'. The 'matter' is an affair brought forth in accordance with God's eternal knowledge and will.
The verse does not imply change in God, but constant creation.
4
Reflect on the theological point
Consider why this distinction matters: it preserves God's attribute of eternal knowledge and rejects the idea that God experiences new events or changes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فائدة: قام رجلٌ إلى ابن الشَّجَرِيّ وهو على كرسيه للوعظ يقرأ تفسير قوله تعالى: كُلَّ يَوْمٍ هُوَ فِي شَأْنٍ ووقف على رأسه فقال: يا هذا، فما يَفْعَلُ ربُّك الآنَ؟ فسَكَتَ وبات مهموماً، فرأى المصطفى صلى الله عليه وسلم، فذكر له ذلك وسأله، فقال له: إن السائل لك الخَضِرُ، وإنه سيعودُ فقل له: شُؤُونَ يبديها ولا يبتديها، يَخْفِضُ أقواماً ويرفعُ آخرين. فأصبح مسروراً، فأتاه وأعاد عليه السؤال، فأجابه بذلك، فقال له: صَلِّ على من علمك، وانصرف مُسْرِعاً. والمراد بالشؤون: الأحوال. وقوله: يُبْدِيها، أي: يُظْهرُها. وقوله: ولا يبتديها، أي: لا يستأنفُها عِلْماً، فمعنى قوله: كُلَّ يَوْمٍ هُوَ فِي شَأْنٍ: كلّ وقت هو في أمرٍ يُظْهِرُهُ على وفق علمه وإرادته أزلاً. فتدبر.
Benefit: A man stood before Ibn al-Shajarī—while he was on his pulpit preaching, reciting the interpretation of God's saying 'Every day He is in a matter'—and stood at his head and said: 'O this one, what does your Lord do now?' So he fell silent and spent the night worried. Then he saw the Chosen One (peace be upon him) in a dream, mentioned that to him and asked him. He said: 'Indeed the questioner is al-Khaḍir, and he will return, so say to him: Affairs He manifests and does not originate anew; He lowers some people and raises others.' So in the morning he was happy. He came and repeated the question, and he replied with that. He said to him: 'Send blessings upon the one who taught you,' and departed quickly. And what is meant by 'affairs' is states. His saying 'He manifests them' means He brings them forth. His saying 'He does not originate them anew' means He does not begin them in knowledge. So the meaning of His saying 'Every day He is in a matter' is: every time He is in an affair which He brings forth in accordance with His knowledge and will eternally. So ponder.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage presents an anecdote that illustrates the correct interpretation of the Qur'anic verse 'Every day He is in a matter' (Sūrat al-Raḥmān, 29). The story involves Ibn al-Shajarī, a scholar, being asked by a mysterious questioner (later revealed to be al-Khaḍir) about what God is doing at that very moment. The Prophet Muḥammad appears to Ibn al-Shajarī in a dream and provides the answer: 'Shu'ūn yubdīhā wa lā yabtadīhā'—affairs which He manifests and does not originate anew. The explanation clarifies that the word 'shu'ūn' means 'aḥwāl' (states), 'yubdīhā' means He brings them forth (from existence), and 'lā yabtadīhā' means He does not begin them anew in His knowledge, because God's knowledge is eternal and unchanging. Thus the verse means that at every moment God brings into existence events that He has eternally known and willed. The lesson guards against the misunderstanding that God experiences new events or changes in His essence.

شؤون (shu'ūn)
يبديها (yubdīhā)
يبتديها (yabtadīhā)
العلم الأزلي (eternal knowledge)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The passage emphasizes that God manifests affairs according to His eternal knowledge; He does not originate them anew in knowledge, preserving His attribute of being all-knowing from eternity.

C) God changes His mind about events
D) The events are random

Key Takeaway
God's every action is a manifestation of His eternal knowledge and will, not a new event that changes His essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What would change in your heart if you internalized that every 'new' event is only a manifestation of His ancient decree?
?
The Prophet taught Ibn al-Shajarī that God 'does not originate affairs anew'—meaning His knowledge never grows. Does this truth fill you with awe at His perfection, or does it feel distant? How can you train yourself to see every daily circumstance as a mirror of God's eternal, unchanging wisdom rather than a random turn of fate?
?
You have asked, like the questioner, 'What is my Lord doing now?'—perhaps in prayer or distress. Are you willing to hear the answer: 'He is manifesting affairs from His eternal knowledge' without demanding a different outcome? 4 min
God's Knowledge Encompasses All Realities
Al-Bajuri enumerates the three objects of divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's knowledge covers all three rational categories: necessary, possible, and impossible.

Core Idea
God's knowledge knows even impossibles as non-existent realities.

In this passage, Al-Bajuri explains the scope of divine knowledge. He clarifies that God's knowledge relates to all things: necessary (like His essence), possible (like creation), and even impossible (like a partner). This sets the stage for distinguishing knowledge from power and will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation, noticing the three categories mentioned.
Pay attention to the examples given for each category.
2
Identify the three objects
Find the references to wājib (necessary), jā'iz (possible), and mustaḥīl (impossible) in the text.
3
Understand the example for impossibles
Note that God knows a partner is non-existent and also knows its reality (what a partner would be).
This shows knowledge is not limited to existent things.
4
Connect to the commentary
Read the marginal note by al-Ujhūrī about the eternal nature of this relation.
It reinforces that the relation of knowledge is not temporal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: الْمُتَعَلَّقُ) أي: تعلُّقاً تنجيزياً قديماً فقط كما علمت. (قوله: بِجَمِيعِ الْوَاجِبَاتِ) أي كذاته تعالى وصفاته الشاملة للعلم نفسه، فيعلمُ تعالى بعلمه أن له علماً. (قوله: وَالْجَائِزَاتِ) أي: كخَلْقِهِ تعالى للأشياء. (وَالْمُسْتَحِيلَاتِ) أي: كشريكه تعالى، فيعلم أنه معدوم، وإنما تعلَّق بالواجبات والجائزات والمستحيلات حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري بأنه كان وأما تعلق العلم بوجود ذلك الشيء في الوقت المخصوص؛ فهو أزلي لا يوصف بأنه سيكون، ولا يطرأ عليه الوصف بأنه كان. (قوله) فَيَعْلَمُ أَنَّهُ مَعْدُومٌ) ويعلم حقيقته بأن يعلم أن معنى الشريك: من يشاركه في الألوهية علماً شبيهاً بعلمنا التصوري لتعلقه بالمفردات. لأنه ليس من صفات التأثير بخلاف القدرة والإرادة، ولذلك لم تتعلَّقا إلا بالممكن؛ إذ لو تعلقتا بالواجبات؛ لأثرنا فيها الوجود، فيلزم تحصيل الحاصل أو العدم، فيلزم قَلْبُ الحقائق؛ لأن حقيقة الواجب ما لا يَقْبَلُ العَدَمَ، ولو تعلقنا بالمستحيلات؛ لأثرنا فيها الوجود، فيلزم قَلْبُ الحقائق؛ لأن حقيقة المستحيل: ما لا يقبل الوجود أو العدم، فيلزم تحصيل الحاصل فهو بعكس ما قيل في الواجبات. فتأمل.
(His saying: 'That which is related to') meaning: a determinative, eternal relation only, as you have learned. (His saying: 'to all necessary things') meaning like His essence and His attributes including knowledge itself, so He knows by His knowledge that He has knowledge. (His saying: 'and the possible things') meaning like His creating things. (And the impossible things) meaning like a partner to Him, so He knows that it is non-existent. And it is related to necessary, possible, and impossible things—commentary of the scholar Aḥmad al-Ujhūrī—that He was, and as for the relation of knowledge to the existence of that thing at the specific time, it is eternal, not described as 'it will be', nor does the description 'it was' occur to it. (His saying:) 'So He knows that it is non-existent' and He knows its reality by knowing that the meaning of 'partner' is one who shares in divinity with Him, a knowledge similar to our conceptual knowledge because it relates to single items. Because knowledge is not among the attributes of effect contrary to power and will, and therefore they (power and will) only relate to the possible; for if they related to necessary things, they would effect existence in them, necessitating the acquisition of the already acquired or non-existence, necessitating the reversal of realities; for the reality of the necessary is that which does not accept non-existence. And if they related to impossible things, they would effect existence in them, necessitating the reversal of realities; for the reality of the impossible is that which does not accept existence, or [if related to non-existence] it would necessitate the acquisition of the already acquired, so it is the opposite of what was said about necessary things. So reflect.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the objects of God's knowledge (ta'alluq al-ʿilm). It begins by specifying that the relation of knowledge is a determinative (tanzīzī) and eternal relation. Then it enumerates the three categories: necessary things (wājibāt) like God's essence and attributes, possible things (jā'izāt) like His creation, and impossible things (mustaḥīlāt) like a partner to God. For impossible things, God knows they are non-existent and knows their reality (e.g., what a partner would be). The passage then makes a key distinction: knowledge is not an attribute of effect (ta'thīr) unlike power (qudra) and will (irāda). Therefore, power and will only attach to possible things. If they attached to necessary things, they would effect existence, leading to either 'obtaining what is already obtained' (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil) or non-existence, which would reverse the reality of the necessary (which cannot cease to exist). Similarly, if they attached to impossible things, they would effect existence, which would reverse the reality of the impossible (which cannot exist). Thus, power and will are limited to possible things. The lesson underscores the distinction between God's knowledge (comprehensive of all categories) and His power and will (only effective on possibles).

Key Terms
تَعَلُّق
واجب
جائز
مستحيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God does not know impossible things because they do not exist.
Correction: God knows impossible things as non-existent and knows their reality, as the text states.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does God know impossible things like a partner?
A) He knows them as non-existent and knows their reality
B) He does not know them because they are impossible
C) He knows them as possible
D) He knows them as existent

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge encompasses everything: necessary, possible, and impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever, in your heart of hearts, limit God's knowledge by assuming He does not know the full reality of something you consider impossible? How does that diminish your trust in His perfect attribute of knowledge? Today, commit to replacing that assumption with the affirmation that His knowledge encompasses all things, even their inner realities.
?
If God knows even the impossible things in their full reality, what does that mean for how you view the hidden struggles or sins you think are impossible to overcome? Will you bring them before Him, knowing He already understands their true nature and can transform them?
?
Knowing that God's knowledge of your future actions is eternal and not described as 'will be' or 'was', how does that affect the way you approach your choices today? Are you living as if your choices are truly meaningful under His comprehensive knowledge?

Why Power and Will Only Apply to Possibles
Al-Bajuri argues why power and will are limited to possible things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine power and will are restricted to possible things and not to necessary or impossible things.

Core Idea
Power and will would cause logical contradictions if applied to necessary or impossible.

Al-Bajuri now distinguishes knowledge from power and will. While knowledge relates to all, power and will are effective attributes that only apply to possibles. Attaching them to necessary or impossible would lead to absurdities like acquiring what is already possessed (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil) or reversing realities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on power and will
Read the second half of the source, focusing on the argument.
Notice that it starts with 'لأنه ليس من صفات التأثير'.
2
Understand the distinction
Note that knowledge is not an attribute of effect, but power and will are.
3
Analyze the argument for necessary things
What would happen if power acted on necessary beings? It would lead to taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil or non-existence, both contradictory.
The reality of the necessary is that it cannot cease to exist.
4
Analyze the argument for impossible things
If power acted on impossibles, it would make them exist, contradicting their reality (which is that they cannot exist).
The text says it would also lead to qalb al-ḥaqā'iq.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Only possible things are subject to power and will, and this is a rational necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: الْمُتَعَلَّقُ) أي: تعلُّقاً تنجيزياً قديماً فقط كما علمت. (قوله: بِجَمِيعِ الْوَاجِبَاتِ) أي كذاته تعالى وصفاته الشاملة للعلم نفسه، فيعلمُ تعالى بعلمه أن له علماً. (قوله: وَالْجَائِزَاتِ) أي: كخَلْقِهِ تعالى للأشياء. (وَالْمُسْتَحِيلَاتِ) أي: كشريكه تعالى، فيعلم أنه معدوم، وإنما تعلَّق بالواجبات والجائزات والمستحيلات حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري بأنه كان وأما تعلق العلم بوجود ذلك الشيء في الوقت المخصوص؛ فهو أزلي لا يوصف بأنه سيكون، ولا يطرأ عليه الوصف بأنه كان. (قوله) فَيَعْلَمُ أَنَّهُ مَعْدُومٌ) ويعلم حقيقته بأن يعلم أن معنى الشريك: من يشاركه في الألوهية علماً شبيهاً بعلمنا التصوري لتعلقه بالمفردات. لأنه ليس من صفات التأثير بخلاف القدرة والإرادة، ولذلك لم تتعلَّقا إلا بالممكن؛ إذ لو تعلقتا بالواجبات؛ لأثرنا فيها الوجود، فيلزم تحصيل الحاصل أو العدم، فيلزم قَلْبُ الحقائق؛ لأن حقيقة الواجب ما لا يَقْبَلُ العَدَمَ، ولو تعلقنا بالمستحيلات؛ لأثرنا فيها الوجود، فيلزم قَلْبُ الحقائق؛ لأن حقيقة المستحيل: ما لا يقبل الوجود أو العدم، فيلزم تحصيل الحاصل فهو بعكس ما قيل في الواجبات. فتأمل.
(His saying: 'That which is related to') meaning: a determinative, eternal relation only, as you have learned. (His saying: 'to all necessary things') meaning like His essence and His attributes including knowledge itself, so He knows by His knowledge that He has knowledge. (His saying: 'and the possible things') meaning like His creating things. (And the impossible things) meaning like a partner to Him, so He knows that it is non-existent. And it is related to necessary, possible, and impossible things—commentary of the scholar Aḥmad al-Ujhūrī—that He was, and as for the relation of knowledge to the existence of that thing at the specific time, it is eternal, not described as 'it will be', nor does the description 'it was' occur to it. (His saying:) 'So He knows that it is non-existent' and He knows its reality by knowing that the meaning of 'partner' is one who shares in divinity with Him, a knowledge similar to our conceptual knowledge because it relates to single items. Because knowledge is not among the attributes of effect contrary to power and will, and therefore they (power and will) only relate to the possible; for if they related to necessary things, they would effect existence in them, necessitating the acquisition of the already acquired or non-existence, necessitating the reversal of realities; for the reality of the necessary is that which does not accept non-existence. And if they related to impossible things, they would effect existence in them, necessitating the reversal of realities; for the reality of the impossible is that which does not accept existence, or [if related to non-existence] it would necessitate the acquisition of the already acquired, so it is the opposite of what was said about necessary things. So reflect.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the objects of God's knowledge (ta'alluq al-ʿilm). It begins by specifying that the relation of knowledge is a determinative (tanzīzī) and eternal relation. Then it enumerates the three categories: necessary things (wājibāt) like God's essence and attributes, possible things (jā'izāt) like His creation, and impossible things (mustaḥīlāt) like a partner to God. For impossible things, God knows they are non-existent and knows their reality (e.g., what a partner would be). The passage then makes a key distinction: knowledge is not an attribute of effect (ta'thīr) unlike power (qudra) and will (irāda). Therefore, power and will only attach to possible things. If they attached to necessary things, they would effect existence, leading to either 'obtaining what is already obtained' (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil) or non-existence, which would reverse the reality of the necessary (which cannot cease to exist). Similarly, if they attached to impossible things, they would effect existence, which would reverse the reality of the impossible (which cannot exist). Thus, power and will are limited to possible things. The lesson underscores the distinction between God's knowledge (comprehensive of all categories) and His power and will (only effective on possibles).

Key Terms
قدرة
إرادة
تحصيل الحاصل
قلب الحقائق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's power can do anything, including making a necessary being cease to exist or making an impossible thing exist.
Correction: Power and will are effective only on possibles; acting on necessary or impossible leads to logical contradictions (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil or qalb al-ḥaqā'iq).

Checkpoint
What would happen if God's power acted on a necessary being?
A) It would create a new necessary being
B) It would lead to acquiring what is already acquired or non-existence
C) It would have no effect
D) It would destroy the necessary being

Key Takeaway
Divine power and will are logically restricted to possible things to avoid contradictions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that God's power means He can do literally anything, even contradictory things? After reading this, are you willing to accept that logical impossibility is not a limitation on God but a reflection of the nature of reality? How will you reconcile this with your previous understanding?
?
This lesson shows that divine power and will only apply to possible things. Think of a time you prayed for something that was logically impossible. Does this change how you view your prayer? What will you do differently now when you make supplications?
?
If God's power operates within logical boundaries, does that make Him less powerful in your eyes, or does it deepen your awe of His wisdom? How does this affect your trust in the order He has established?

Life as an Existent Attribute Enabling Perception
Al-Bajuri defines life as an attribute of perception and distinguishes it from spirit using miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of life (al-ḥayāh) as an existent attribute that enables perception, its distinction from the spirit, and the miraculous evidence that life can exist in inanimate objects.

Core Idea
Life is a distinct attribute that enables perception, separate from the spirit.

Al-Bajuri now turns to the attribute of life, defining it precisely within the theological framework. He clarifies that life is an existent attribute that makes perception possible, and he distinguishes it from the common notion of the spirit. To illustrate, he cites miraculous instances where God granted life to inanimate objects, showing that life can exist without a spirit.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and identify components
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that life is defined as an existent attribute (ṣifah wujūdiyyah) that renders its possessor capable of perception (idrāk).
The definition lists specific perceptual attributes: knowledge, hearing, sight, and others like power and will.
2
Understand the scope of the definition
Notice that the author considers whether the definition applies to eternal life, temporal life, or both. He states it is most appropriate for eternal life (God's life) but could include temporal life, though not temporal alone.
Context matters: the discussion is about God's attributes.
3
Distinguish life from spirit
Reflect on the key clarification: temporal life is not the spirit. Life can exist without a spirit, as demonstrated by miracles where God granted life to inanimate objects like the tree and pebbles that interacted with the Prophet.
This reinforces that life is a distinct, independent attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: (وَالْحَيَاةُ) هي صِفَةٌ وجودية تُصَحِحُ لمن قامت به الإدراك، أي: أن يتَّصِفَ بصفات الإدراك التي هي العِلْمُ والسَّمْعُ والبَصَرُ، ومثل صفات الإدراك: غيرُها من سائر الصفات كالقدرة والإرادة وهذا التعريفُ يحتمل أن يكون للحياة القديمة فقط، وهو المناسِبُ للمقام، ويحتمل أن يكون للحياة القديمة والحادثة، ولا يَصِحُ أن يكون للحياة الحادثة فقط؛ لأنه خروج عن المقام. واعلم أن الحياة الحادثة غيرُ الروح فليست هي هي؛ إذ قد تُوجَدُ بدونها، فقد خَلَقَ الله تعالى الحياة في كثير من الجمادات مُعْجِزَةً أو كرامة بدون رُوحٍ، كالشَّجَرِ الذي سَلَّمَ على المصطفى صلى الله عليه وسلم، والحَصَى الذي سَبَّحَ في كفه صلى الله عليه وسلم.
(His saying: 'And life') is an existent attribute that renders the one in whom it subsists capable of perception, i.e., that he becomes qualified with the attributes of perception which are knowledge, hearing, and sight, and like the attributes of perception: other than them from the rest of the attributes such as power and will. And this definition likely applies to eternal life alone, and that is appropriate for the context, and it possibly applies to eternal and temporal life, but it cannot apply to temporal life alone because that would be a digression from the context. And know that temporal life is not the spirit; it is not the same as it; for it may exist without it. For God has created life in many inanimate objects as a miracle or a grace without a spirit, like the tree that greeted the Chosen One (peace be upon him) and the pebbles that glorified in his hand (peace be upon him).
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage defines the attribute of life (al-ḥayāh). Life is described as an existent attribute (ṣifah wujūdiyyah) that makes the one in whom it subsists capable of perception (idrāk). Perception includes the attributes of knowledge, hearing, and sight, and similar to these are other attributes like power and will (which also require life). The definition is interpreted as applying primarily to God's eternal life, though it could also apply to created (temporal) life. However, it cannot apply only to temporal life because the context is about God's attributes. A key clarification follows: temporal life (al-ḥayāh al-ḥādithah) is not the same as the spirit (rūḥ). Life can exist in inanimate objects without a spirit, as evidenced by miracles (mu'jizāt) or graces (karāmāt) where God created life in trees and pebbles that then spoke or glorified. This reinforces that life is a distinct attribute that God can bestow independently of a spirit. The examples cited are the tree that greeted the Prophet and the pebbles that glorified God in his hand.

Correction: The text explicitly states that temporal life is not the spirit; life can be created in inanimate objects without a spirit, as shown by miracles.

What will you do today to align your perception with His pleasure?

Definition of the Attribute of Life
Al-Bajuri defines life as an existent attribute enabling perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Bajuri's definition of life as an existent attribute that makes perception valid, and how it differs from the soul.

Core Idea
Life is an existent attribute that enables perception, distinct from the soul.

Al-Bajuri defines the attribute of life (al-hayah) as an existent attribute that renders its bearer capable of perception, including knowledge, hearing, and sight. He then clarifies that this definition primarily applies to God's eternal life, and distinguishes temporal life from the soul, citing miracles where life appears in inanimate objects without a soul.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own wording come through first.
Notice the precise phrasing: 'sifah wujudiyyah tusahhihu liman qamat bihi al-idrak'.
2
Identify the core definition
Find where al-Bajuri states the definition of life. He says it is an existent attribute that makes perception valid for its subject.
Perception includes knowledge, hearing, sight, and other attributes like power and will.
3
Understand the scope of the definition
Al-Bajuri notes that the definition may apply only to God's eternal life, or to both eternal and temporal life. The context suggests it is about eternal life.
He says it is 'not correct for temporal life only' because that would be out of context.
4
Distinguish life from soul
Al-Bajuri explicitly says temporal life is not the soul (ruh). Life can exist without a soul as a miracle, as in the tree that greeted the Prophet and the pebbles that glorified in his hand.
This corrects a common misunderstanding that life and soul are identical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وَالْحَيَاةُ) هي صِفَةٌ وجودية تُصَحِحُ لمن قامت به الإدراك، أي: أن يتَّصِفَ بصفات الإدراك التي هي العِلْمُ والسَّمْعُ والبَصَرُ، ومثل صفات الإدراك: غيرُها من سائر الصفات كالقدرة والإرادة وهذا التعريفُ يحتمل أن يكون للحياة القديمة فقط، وهو المناسِبُ للمقام، ويحتمل أن يكون للحياة القديمة والحادثة، ولا يَصِحُ أن يكون للحياة الحادثة فقط؛ لأنه خروج عن المقام. واعلم أن الحياة الحادثة غيرُ الروح فليست هي هي؛ إذ قد تُوجَدُ بدونها، فقد خَلَقَ الله تعالى الحياة في كثير من الجمادات مُعْجِزَةً أو كرامة بدون رُوحٍ، كالشَّجَرِ الذي سَلَّمَ على المصطفى، والحَصَى الذي سَبَّحَ في كفه.
His saying: (And life) is an existent attribute that corrects (renders valid) for the one in whom it subsists perception, i.e., that he becomes characterized by the attributes of perception which are knowledge, hearing, and sight, and similar to the attributes of perception are the rest of the attributes like power and will. And this definition may be for eternal life only, which is appropriate to the context, and may be for eternal life and temporal life, and it is not correct for temporal life only because it is a departure from the context. And know that temporal life is not the soul, so it is not the same; for it may exist without it, for God Most High has created life in many inanimate objects as a miracle or a miracle without a soul, like the tree that greeted the Chosen One, and the pebbles that glorified in his hand.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage defines the attribute of life (al-hayah) as an existent attribute (sifah wujudiyyah) that makes valid (tusahhihu) for its subject the possession of perception (al-idrak). Perception here includes knowledge, hearing, sight, and similar attributes like power and will. The definition can apply either to God's eternal life or to both eternal and temporal life, but the context (the discussion of God's attributes) makes it specifically about eternal life. The author then clarifies that temporal life is not identical to the soul (al-ruh), for God can create life in inanimate objects as a miracle (mu'jizah) or karama without a soul, citing the examples of a tree greeting the Prophet and pebbles glorifying in his hand.

Hearing and Sight: Divine and Created Definitions
Al-Bajuri defines hearing and sight for God and creatures, with two schools.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how hearing and sight are defined for God (as existent attributes with encompassing relation) versus for creatures (two definitions: philosophers and Ahl al-Sunna).

Core Idea
God's hearing and sight encompass all existence; creatures' senses are physical powers created by God.

This passage from al-Bajuri's commentary distinguishes divine hearing and sight from their created counterparts. For God, they are essential attributes with a unique encompassing relation. For creatures, the passage presents definitions from both philosophers and the Sunni theological school.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Note the flow: first divine definition, then a new section for creatures with two schools.
Pay attention to the symbols (+) and (x) in the philosophers' definition; they are likely diagrams in the original text.
2
Identify the three definitions
The passage gives one definition for God and two for creatures. Underline or note each: divine, philosophers', Ahl al-Sunna.
For creatures, the philosophers define hearing and sight anatomically, while Ahl al-Sunna simplify as powers created by God.
3
Reflect on the divine attribute's uniqueness
Consider why the divine definition emphasizes 'existent attributes' and 'relation additional to knowledge.' What does that imply about God's perception?
The attribute is distinct from knowledge and encompasses all existents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: وَالسَّمْعُ وَالْبَصَرُ هما في حقه تعالى صفتان وجوديتان قائمتان بذاته تعالى تتعلقان بكل موجود على وجه الإحاطة تعلقاً زائداً على تعلق العلم وأما في حقِّ الحوادث؛ فالسمعُ قُوَّةً مُودَعَةٌ في العصب المفروش في مُقَعْرِ الصَّمَاخ. والبصرُ: قُوَّةً مَرْكُوزَةً في العصبتين المتلاقيتين في مُقَدَّمِ الدماغ على وجه التَّقَاطِع الصليبي هكذا (+)، أو على هيئة دالَيْنِ ظَهْرُ كلّ في ظهر الأخرى هكذا: (عد) (x). وهذا تعريفهما عند الحكماء. وأما عند أهل السنة؛ فالسمعُ : قُوَّةً خَلَقَها الله تعالى في الأُذُنين. والبصرُ: قُوَّةً خلقها الله تعالى في العينين.
His saying: And hearing and sight, in the case of Him Most High, are two existent attributes subsisting in His essence, relating to every existent in an encompassing manner, a relation additional to the relation of knowledge. As for the case of temporal things, hearing is a power deposited in the nerve spread in the hollow of the ear canal. And sight is a power implanted in the two nerves meeting in the front of the brain in the form of a crossed intersection like this (+), or in the form of two 'dals' the back of each on the back of the other like this: ('ad) (x). And this is their definition according to the philosophers. But according to Ahl al-Sunna, hearing is a power that God created in the two ears, and sight is a power that God created in the two eyes.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage defines hearing (al-sam') and sight (al-basar). For God, they are two existent attributes subsisting in His essence that relate to every existent comprehensively, and this relation is additional to the relation of knowledge (i.e., they provide a distinct mode of perception). For created beings (al-hawadith), the passage gives two definitions: the philosophers' definition and the Ahl al-Sunna definition. According to the philosophers, hearing is a power lodged in the nerve in the ear canal, and sight is a power in the two optic nerves that cross in the front of the brain (with an illustration). According to Ahl al-Sunna, hearing is a power created by God in the ears, and sight is a power created by God in the eyes, without delving into anatomical specifics.

Key Terms
السمع
البصر
صفة وجودية
الإحاطة
الحكماء
أهل السنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's hearing and sight are identical to His knowledge.
Correction: The text specifies that they relate 'in an encompassing manner, a relation additional to the relation of knowledge,' showing they are distinct modes of perception.

Checkpoint

C) They relate only to possible things
D) They relate only to material things

Key Takeaway
The passage distinguishes divine hearing/sight (attributes with encompassing relation) from created hearing/sight (physical powers), with a note on two schools of definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have ears that hear and eyes that see—limited, physical, dependent. When you consider that God's hearing and sight encompass every existent in a way additional to His knowledge, does your heart tremble at the thought that nothing in your life, even your innermost thoughts, is outside His full perception? How will you live today knowing that every word you speak and every glance you take is heard and seen by Him not as a sound or image, but as an attribute that encompasses all?
?
The philosophers gave detailed anatomical definitions of hearing and sight, while Ahl al-Sunna simply said 'God created it.' Which of these approaches do you naturally gravitate towards? Are you satisfied with that simplicity, or do you feel a pull to understand the mechanisms? In what areas of your faith do you demand explanations that go beyond what Allah has revealed, and does that demand reveal a lack of trust in His word?
?
You believe that God hears and sees everything, but examine your life: do you act as if He does? When you sin in solitude, do you truly feel that you are not hidden from His hearing and sight? This passage teaches that His hearing and sight are not like yours—they are attributes that encompass all existence. If you truly internalized this, what specific habit would you change? Name one, and commit to it starting now.
