Why Hearing Superior to Sight for Creatures
Al-Bajuri argues hearing is better than sight for creatures, refuting a counterargument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Al-Bajuri considers hearing superior to sight for created beings and how he refutes the opposing view.

Core Idea
Hearing unlocks spiritual knowledge; sight offers only worldly benefits.

In this passage, Al-Bajuri weighs two senses—hearing and sight—for creatures. At first glance, sight seems more versatile, perceiving shapes and colors. Yet Al-Bajuri argues that hearing is ultimately superior because it connects us to divine knowledge and intellectual depth. Let's read his reasoning and the refutation of the counterargument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Let the author's argument sink in.
Notice the three-part structure: thesis, antithesis, refutation.
2
Identify the three parts
Find where Al-Bajuri states his view, where he presents the opposing view with its reason, and where he refutes it.
3
Reflect on the example
Consider the example of the deaf person as a discarded stone and the blind person as possessing perfect intellectual knowledge. How does this support hearing's superiority?
The example shows that lacking hearing is more detrimental to spiritual understanding than lacking sight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والسمعُ أفضلُ من البصر في حق الحوادث على الصحيح. وقيل: إن البصر أفضلُ؛ لأنه يُدْرَكُ به الأجسام والألوان والهيئاتُ بخلاف الشمع، فإنه قاصر على الأصوات. ورُدَّ: بأن كثرة هذه المتعلقاتِ فوائد دنيوية لا يُعَوَّلُ عليها، ألا ترى أن من جالَسَ أَصَمَّ ؛ فكأنما جالَسَ حجراً مُلْقى، وأما الأعمى؛ ففي غاية الكمال الفهمي والعلم الذوقي
And hearing is better than sight in the case of temporal things according to the correct view. And it was said: sight is better because by it are perceived bodies, colors, and shapes, unlike hearing, for it is limited to sounds. And it was refuted: that the multitude of these related things are worldly benefits not to be relied upon; do you not see that whoever sits with a deaf person is as if he sat with a discarded stone, while the blind person is at the utmost perfection of intellectual and tasted knowledge.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage presents a debate on whether hearing or sight is superior in created beings. The correct opinion, according to al-Bajuri, is that hearing is better. The opposing view argues that sight is better because it perceives bodies, colors, and shapes, while hearing is limited to sounds. The refutation responds that the multiple objects of sight are merely worldly benefits (fawa'id dunyawiyyah) not to be relied upon. The author supports the superiority of hearing by noting that a deaf person is like a discarded stone in company, while a blind person can attain great intellectual and spiritual understanding (al-'ilm al-dhawqi). Thus, hearing contributes more to spiritual and intellectual perfection than sight.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that sight is superior because it perceives more types of objects (bodies, colors, shapes).
Correction: Al-Bajuri argues that those perceived objects are merely worldly benefits, not to be relied upon for spiritual excellence. Hearing is superior because it enables intellectual and tasted knowledge (al-'ilm al-dhawqi).

Key Takeaway
Hearing is better than sight for creatures because it facilitates spiritual and intellectual knowledge, while sight's objects are only worldly benefits.

Two Types of Relation for Hearing and Sight: Actual and Potential
Al-Bajuri explains the instantaneous and potential eternal relations of hearing and sight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the eternal actual and potential relations of the divine attributes of hearing and sight, and the scholarly disagreement about the potential relation.

Core Idea
Hearing and sight have two eternal relations: actual to God's essence, and potential to future existents—though this latter is disputed.

This passage from al-Bajuri's commentary on Umm al-Barahin explores the precise nature of how the attributes of hearing and sight relate to creation. Al-Bajuri distinguishes between an instantaneous eternal relation to God's own being, and a potential relation to things that will exist. However, as the marginal note discusses, the idea of a potential relation to non-existent entities is controversial among scholars. Let's unpack these technical terms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that al-Bajuri mentions 'three relations' but then lists only two—a possible scribal error.
2
Identify the two types of relation
The passage presents two main categories: the eternal actual relation (al-tacaalluq al-tanjizi al-qadim) and the eternal potential relation (al-tacaalluq al-suluhi al-qadim). See how each is defined.
The actual relation is to God's essence and attributes; the potential is to possible things that will exist.
3
Examine the disagreement
The marginal note from al-Anbabi reports a dispute: some scholars deny an eternal potential relation for hearing and sight because they cannot relate to non-existents. Others say this view is contrary to the well-known position.
The author ends with 'so reflect' (fa-ta'ammal), indicating the issue is not settled.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why this distinction matters for understanding divine attributes. The potential relation allows God's hearing and sight to encompass all future events, while the actual relation emphasizes His immediate knowledge of Himself.
This connects to broader debates about God's knowledge of particulars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي قولهم : تتعلقان بكلّ موجود إشارة إلى تعلقهما الثلاثة: التعلق التنجيزي القديم، وهو تعلقهما أزلاً بذاته تعالى وصفاته، والتعلق الصُّلُوحِيُّ القديم، تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي (قوله: وَالتَّعَلُّقُ الصُّلُوحِيُّ الْقَدِيمُ في بعض شرَّاح المتن: إن الصحيح: أن السمع والبصر ليس لهما تعلق صلوحي قديم؛ لعدم تعلقهما بالممكن المعدوم الذي سَبَقَ في عِلْمٍ الله أنه سيوجد، والقولُ به مبني على تعلقهما بالمعدوم .اهـ. وفي بعض الحواشي: أن تعلقهما تعلقاً صلوحياً قديماً خلافُ المشهور فتأمل
And in their saying: they relate to every existent, there is an indication of their three relations: the eternal actual relation, which is their relating eternally to His essence and attributes, and the eternal potential relation. Marginal notes of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi: (His saying: and the eternal potential relation. In some commentators of the text: the correct view is that hearing and sight do not have an eternal potential relation, due to their not relating to the possible non-existent that has been known in God's knowledge that it will exist, and the statement of it is based on their relation to the non-existent. End quote. And in some marginal notes: that their having an eternal potential relation is contrary to the well-known, so reflect.)
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage discusses the types of relation (tacaalluq) for the divine attributes of hearing and sight. It states that their relation to every existent indicates three (possibly a mistake for 'two') types: the eternal actual relation (al-tacaalluq al-tanjizi al-qadim) which is their eternal relation to God's own essence and attributes, and the eternal potential relation (al-tacaalluq al-suluhi al-qadim) which is their capacity to relate to possible things that will exist. The marginal note by al-Anbabi then notes a disagreement: some commentators say that hearing and sight do not have an eternal potential relation because they do not relate to the non-existent possible (al-mumkin al-ma'dum) before it exists; claiming such a relation is based on the idea that they relate to the non-existent. Other marginal notes say that the view that they have an eternal potential relation is contrary to the well-known opinion (khilaf al-mashhur). The author concludes with 'so reflect' (fa-ta'ammal), leaving the issue open.

Key Terms
التعلق التنجيزي
التعلق الصلاحي
القديم
الممكن المعدوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that hearing and sight relate to all things eternally in the same way, without distinguishing between actual and potential relations.
Correction: The passage shows that the actual relation applies only to God's own essence and attributes, while the potential relation is a capacity for future existents. Moreover, the potential relation is disputed among scholars.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the 'eternal actual relation' (al-tacaalluq al-tanjizi al-qadim) for hearing and sight?
A) Their relation to all possible things that will exist
B) Their eternal relation to God's own essence and attributes
C) Their relation to sounds and colors
D) Their relation to created beings in this world

Key Takeaway
The divine attributes of hearing and sight have an eternal actual relation to God and a disputed eternal potential relation to future existents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that even scholars debate the nature of divine attributes like hearing and sight, does it humble you that God's reality is beyond full human comprehension? How can you use this to increase your reliance on revelation rather than your own reasoning?
?
If you were to choose a side in this debate between the potential relation being affirmed or denied, would you base your choice on theological affiliation or on careful study of the sources and arguments? What would be the consequence of choosing based on partisanship?
?
The potential relation implies that God's hearing and sight, in some sense, anticipate future existents. How does that affect the way you see God's awareness of your future actions? Does it make you more conscious of His presence now, knowing He has always known you?

Objection and Answer on the Phrase 'لا تتعلق بشيء'
Sanusi's commentator explains an objection and answer about the phrasing of the attributes' relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection to the phrase 'لا تتعلق بشيء' and its resolution through the distinction between linguistic and technical meanings of 'شيء'.

Core Idea
The word 'شيء' can mean two things — and the difference saves the creed.

Sanusi's commentator addresses a subtle objection to the phrasing of a key statement about divine attributes. The objection hinges on the possible misinterpretation of the word 'شيء'. This lesson unpacks the objection and the two-part answer that clarifies the intended meaning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the objection and answer structure.
Notice the smooth flow from objection to answer.
2
Identify the objection
Find the reason the objector considers the phrase problematic: because 'شيء' usually means an existent in technical discourse.
3
Understand the two-part answer
The answer offers two interpretations: linguistic (broad) and technical (with priority implication). Distinguish them.
The linguistic meaning covers both existent and non-existent; the technical meaning still works by implication.
4
Reflect on theological precision
Consider why this distinction matters: it prevents misattributing any relation to non-existents to divine attributes.
This maintains the transcendence of Allah's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَهِيَ لَا تَتَعَلَّقُ بِشَيْءٍ) اعتُرِضَ: بأنه كان الأولى حَذْفُ قوله: (بشيء)، أو إبداله بأمر؛ لأنه يُوهِمُ أنها تتعلق بالمعدوم؛ إذ المتبادر منه المعنى الاصطلاحي: وهو الموجود. وأجيب بأن المراد به معناه اللغوي، وهو مُطلَقُ الأمر الشامل للموجود والمعدوم، ويحتمل أن يُرَادَ به المعنى الاصطلاحي: وهو الموجود، ويُفهَمُ منه عدم تعلقها بالمعدوم من باب أولى.
His statement: 'and they (the attributes) do not relate to anything' – it was objected that it would have been better to delete his statement 'to anything' or replace it with 'to an affair' because it implies that they relate to the non-existent, since the immediate understanding from it is the technical meaning which is the existent. And it was answered that what is meant by it is its linguistic meaning, which is the absolute affair encompassing the existent and the non-existent. And it is possible that what is meant by it is the technical meaning, which is the existent, and from it is understood the non-relating to the non-existent by an even stronger priority.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer concerning the phrasing of Sanusi's text. The commentator first presents the objection: the phrase 'لا تتعلق بشيء' should have been omitted or replaced because the term 'شيء' in its technical sense refers to an existent, and thus the phrase could be misinterpreted as implying that the attributes relate to non-existents. The answer is twofold: first, 'شيء' is used here in its linguistic sense, meaning any affair (existent or non-existent), so no problem arises. Second, even if taken in the technical sense, it would still imply by priority that they do not relate to non-existents. The argument follows the author's order: objection, then answer with two alternatives.

Key Terms
شيء
المعدوم
الموجود
المعنى الاصطلاحي
المعنى اللغوي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'شيء' always means an existent thing in Arabic.
Correction: In Arabic, 'شيء' can have a broader linguistic meaning covering any affair, existent or non-existent, as used in this theological context.

Checkpoint
Why was the phrase 'لا تتعلق بشيء' objected to?
A) Because it is redundant.
B) Because it implies the attributes relate to non-existents.
C) Because it contradicts the attributes.
D) Because it is incomplete.

Key Takeaway
The objection to the phrase is resolved by understanding that 'شيء' can be taken in its linguistic sense to include both existent and non-existent, or in its technical sense with a priority implication.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a theological statement because it seemed flawed at first glance, only to later discover a deeper correct interpretation? What does this teach you about humility in seeking knowledge?
?
This passage shows that a single word like 'شيء' can carry multiple valid meanings. How often do you jump to conclusions about the meaning of a text without considering alternative interpretations? Commit to pausing and asking what the intended meaning might be before forming an opinion.
?
The commentator offered two acceptable interpretations for the same phrase. Are you willing to accept that there can be more than one valid explanation for a theological point? How does this affect your attitude towards others who may hold a different valid view?

Divine Hearing and Sight and Their Definitions in Creatures
Sanusi defines hearing and sight as divine attributes and as powers in creatures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how hearing and sight are defined as divine attributes and how they are defined in creatures according to philosophers and Ahl al-Sunna.

Core Idea
Hearing and sight are existential attributes of God and created powers in creatures.

This passage from Sanusi's Umm al-Barahin commentator defines the attributes of hearing and sight. It first establishes their nature in God as encompassing attributes, then explains how they manifest in creatures according to two different schools: the philosophers and Ahl al-Sunna. Understanding these definitions is crucial for grasping the unique nature of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the shift from divine to creaturely definitions.
2
Distinguish divine and creaturely definitions
Identify where the passage talks about God and where it talks about creatures. Note the difference in language: attributes vs powers.
3
Compare philosophers and Ahl al-Sunna
The passage gives two definitions for creatures: one from philosophers (anatomical) and one from Ahl al-Sunna (direct creation). Understand the difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَالسَّمْعُ وَالْبَصَرُ) هما في حقه تعالى صفتان وجوديتان قائمتان بذاته تعالى تتعلقان بكل موجود على وجه الإحاطة تعلقاً زائداً على تعلق العلم وأما في حقِّ الحوادث؛ فالسمعُ قُوَّةً مُودَعَةٌ في العصب المفروش في مُقَعْرِ الصَّمَاخ. والبصرُ: قُوَّةً مَرْكُوزَةً في العصبتين المتلاقيتين في مُقَدَّمِ الدماغ على وجه التَّقَاطِع الصليبي هكذا (+)، أو على هيئة دالَيْنِ ظَهْرُ كلّ في ظهر الأخرى هكذا: (عد) (x). وهذا تعريفهما عند الحكماء. وأما عند أهل السنة؛ فالسمعُ : قُوَّةً خَلَقَها الله تعالى في الأُذُنين. والبصرُ: قُوَّةً خلقها الله تعالى في العينين. والسمعُ أفضلُ من البصر في حق الحوادث على الصحيح. وقيل: إن البصر أفضلُ؛ لأنه يُدرَك به الأجسام والألوان والهيئات بخلاف الشمع، فإنه قاصر على الأصوات. ورُدَّ: بأن كثرة هذه المتعلقاتِ فوائد دنيوية لا يُعَوَّلُ عليها، ألا ترى أن من جالَسَ أَصَمَّ فكأنما جالَسَ حجراً مُلْقى، وأما الأعمى؛ ففي غاية الكمال الفهمي والعلم الذوقي
His statement: 'and hearing and sight' – both are, in His right (mighty and exalted), two existential attributes subsisting by His essence (exalted) relating to every existent with encompassing relation, a relation additional to the relation of knowledge. As for in the right of contingent beings, hearing is a power lodged in the nerve spread in the cavity of the ear. And sight is a power fixed in the two nerves meeting in the front of the brain in the form of a cross-like intersection like this (+), or in the form of two dal's with the back of each against the back of the other like this: (عد) (x). And this is their definition according to the philosophers. And as for according to Ahl al-Sunna, hearing is a power that Allah created in the ears, and sight is a power that Allah created in the eyes. And hearing is better than sight in the right of contingent beings according to the correct view. And it was said that sight is better because by it are perceived bodies, colors, and forms, unlike hearing, for it is limited to sounds. And it was refuted by that the multitude of these objects of perception are worldly benefits not to be relied upon. Do you not see that he who sits with a deaf person is as if he sits with a cast stone, while the blind person is at the utmost of intellectual perfection and experiential knowledge.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage defines the attributes of hearing and sight. It first states that in God they are two existential attributes subsisting in His essence, relating comprehensively to every existent, with a relation additional to that of knowledge. Then it defines them in creatures: hearing as a power in the ear nerve, sight as a power in the optic nerves, describing the anatomical configuration (cross-like intersection or two dals). The definitions are given first according to philosophers, then according to Ahl al-Sunna (as powers created by Allah in the ears and eyes). The passage then discusses which is better in creatures: the correct view is that hearing is better, with a refutation of the claim that sight is better because it perceives more objects. The refutation is that those are worldly benefits not to be relied upon, and a deaf person is like a stone, while a blind person can have intellectual perfection.

قوة

مقعر الصماخ

أهل السنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Hearing and sight in creatures are the same essential attribute as in God.
Correction: In God, they are existential attributes; in creatures, they are created powers.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Hearing and sight in God are comprehensive attributes; in creatures they are powers dependent on physical organs.

The Superiority of Hearing over Sight in Creatures
Sanusi discusses the superiority of hearing over sight in creatures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate among scholars about whether hearing or sight is better in creatures and why hearing is considered superior.

Core Idea
Hearing is superior to sight in creatures because of its spiritual benefits.

After defining hearing and sight in creatures, the text addresses a common debate: which is better? Some argue sight is better because it perceives more, but the correct view according to Ahl al-Sunna is that hearing is superior. This lesson unpacks the arguments and the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the debate passage
Read the Arabic passage that starts with 'والسمع أفضل' and its translation. Note the two opinions presented.
2
Identify the two views and arguments
The passage first states that hearing is better (correct view), then mentions the opposing view that sight is better because it perceives more, and finally gives the refutation.
3
Understand the refutation's focus
The refutation shifts the focus from worldly benefits (sight) to spiritual/intellectual benefits (hearing). This is the key to understanding why hearing is preferred.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَالسَّمْعُ وَالْبَصَرُ) هما في حقه تعالى صفتان وجوديتان قائمتان بذاته تعالى تتعلقان بكل موجود على وجه الإحاطة تعلقاً زائداً على تعلق العلم وأما في حقِّ الحوادث؛ فالسمعُ قُوَّةً مُودَعَةٌ في العصب المفروش في مُقَعْرِ الصَّمَاخ. والبصرُ: قُوَّةً مَرْكُوزَةً في العصبتين المتلاقيتين في مُقَدَّمِ الدماغ على وجه التَّقَاطِع الصليبي هكذا (+)، أو على هيئة دالَيْنِ ظَهْرُ كلّ في ظهر الأخرى هكذا: (عد) (x). وهذا تعريفهما عند الحكماء. وأما عند أهل السنة؛ فالسمعُ : قُوَّةً خَلَقَها الله تعالى في الأُذُنين. والبصرُ: قُوَّةً خلقها الله تعالى في العينين. والسمعُ أفضلُ من البصر في حق الحوادث على الصحيح. وقيل: إن البصر أفضلُ؛ لأنه يُدرَك به الأجسام والألوان والهيئات بخلاف الشمع، فإنه قاصر على الأصوات. ورُدَّ: بأن كثرة هذه المتعلقاتِ فوائد دنيوية لا يُعَوَّلُ عليها، ألا ترى أن من جالَسَ أَصَمَّ فكأنما جالَسَ حجراً مُلْقى، وأما الأعمى؛ ففي غاية الكمال الفهمي والعلم الذوقي
His statement: 'and hearing and sight' – both are, in His right (mighty and exalted), two existential attributes subsisting by His essence (exalted) relating to every existent with encompassing relation, a relation additional to the relation of knowledge. As for in the right of contingent beings, hearing is a power lodged in the nerve spread in the cavity of the ear. And sight is a power fixed in the two nerves meeting in the front of the brain in the form of a cross-like intersection like this (+), or in the form of two dal's with the back of each against the back of the other like this: (عد) (x). And this is their definition according to the philosophers. And as for according to Ahl al-Sunna, hearing is a power that Allah created in the ears, and sight is a power that Allah created in the eyes. And hearing is better than sight in the right of contingent beings according to the correct view. And it was said that sight is better because by it are perceived bodies, colors, and forms, unlike hearing, for it is limited to sounds. And it was refuted by that the multitude of these objects of perception are worldly benefits not to be relied upon. Do you not see that he who sits with a deaf person is as if he sits with a cast stone, while the blind person is at the utmost of intellectual perfection and experiential knowledge.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage defines the attributes of hearing and sight. It first states that in God they are two existential attributes subsisting in His essence, relating comprehensively to every existent, with a relation additional to that of knowledge. Then it defines them in creatures: hearing as a power in the ear nerve, sight as a power in the optic nerves, describing the anatomical configuration (cross-like intersection or two dals). The definitions are given first according to philosophers, then according to Ahl al-Sunna (as powers created by Allah in the ears and eyes). The passage then discusses which is better in creatures: the correct view is that hearing is better, with a refutation of the claim that sight is better because it perceives more objects. The refutation is that those are worldly benefits not to be relied upon, and a deaf person is like a stone, while a blind person can have intellectual perfection.

السمع
البصر
فوائد دنيوية
الكمال الفهمي
العلم الذوقي

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is hearing considered better than sight in creatures?
A) Because it perceives more objects.
B) Because it is associated with intellectual and spiritual perfection.
C) Because it is a divine attribute.
D) Because it is created by Allah.

Key Takeaway
Hearing surpasses sight in creatures because it is linked to higher intellectual and spiritual perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Three Types of Relation for Hearing and Sight
Explains the threefold relation of hearing and sight with a marginal note on the potential relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three types of relation (ancient accidental, ancient potential, and accidental existential) that God's hearing and sight have, including the debated potential relation and the objects they relate to.

Core Idea
God's hearing and sight relate to existents in three distinct ways.

This passage from Sanusi's commentator unpacks the threefold relation of God's attributes of hearing and sight. It distinguishes between an eternal actual relation, a potential relation before creation, and an actual temporal relation after creation. A marginal note alerts us to a disagreement about the potential relation. Finally, it specifies which objects are included and which are excluded.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Get a sense of the author's flow and the technical terms.
Notice the three terms: تعلق تنجيزي قديم, تعلق صلوحي قديم, تعلق تنجيزي حادث.
2
Identify the three types of relation
The passage lists three relations. Identify each one: ancient accidental (to essence and attributes), ancient potential (suitability to relate to contingent before existence), and accidental existential (actual relation after existence).
The potential relation is debated—marginal note gives two views.
3
Understand the debate on potential relation
The marginal note from al-Anbabi reports that some deny an ancient potential relation for hearing and sight because it would require relation to non-existents. Others affirm it. Consider why this matters.
This is a subtle point about how divine attributes relate to contingent things before they exist.
4
Note the objects of relation
The passage says the two attributes relate to all existents (necessary and contingent), including colors and sounds, but not to the 'modes' (akwan: conjunction, disjunction, motion, rest) because these are mental considerations.
The modes are attributes of bodies, not directly perceived.
5
Summarize the distribution of 'al-muta'alliqan'
The commentator explains that the term refers to the three types distributed accordingly. Reflect on how one attribute can have multiple relations.
This shows the precision needed in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي قولهم : تتعلقان بكل موجود إشارة إلى تعلقهما الثلاثة: التعلق التنجيزي القديم، وهو تعلقهما أزلاً بذاته تعالى وصفاته، والتعلق الصُّلُوحِيُّ الْقَدِيمُ، تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي (قوله: وَالتَّعَلُّقُ الصُّلُوحِيُّ الْقَدِيمُ في بعض شرَّاح المتن: إن الصحيح: أن السمع والبصر ليس لهما تعلق صلوحي قديم؛ لعدم تعلقهما بالممكن المعدوم الذي سَبَقَ في عِلْمٍ الله أنه سيوجد، والقولُ به مبني على تعلقهما بالمعدوم .اهـ. وفي بعض الحواشي: أن تعلقهما تعلقاً صلوحياً قديماً خلافُ المشهور فتأمل) وهو صلاحيتهما للتعلُّق بالموجود الجائز قبل وجوده، والتعلق التنجيزي الحادث، وهو تعلقها تنجيزياً بالموجود المذكور بعد وجوده. قوله: الْمُتَعَلَّقَانِ أي: تعلقاً تنجيزياً قديماً، أو صلوحياً قديماً، أو تنجيزياً حادثاً على التوزيع الذي عَلِمْتَه. قوله: بِجَمِيعِ الْمَوْجُودَاتِ أي: واجبها وجائِزها. ودخل في الموجودات: الألوان والأصوات، وأما الأكوان ـ وهي الاجتماع والافتراق، والحركة والسكون ؛ فلا يتعلَّقُ بها سَمْعُهُ تعالى وبَصَرُه؛ لأنها من الأمور الاعتبارية على الصحيح، والمشاهَدُ إنما هو المُتَّصِفُ بها لا هي.
And in their statement: 'they relate to every existent' is an indication of their threefold relation: the ancient accidental relation (al-ta'alluq al-tanjizi al-qadim), which is their relation eternally to His essence and attributes, and the ancient potential relation (al-ta'alluq al-suluhi al-qadim). Notes of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi: (His statement: 'and the ancient potential relation' – in some commentators of the text: the correct view is that hearing and sight do not have an ancient potential relation because they do not relate to the possible non-existent that Allah knew would exist, and the statement about it is based on their relating to the non-existent. End. And in some glosses: that they have an ancient potential relation is contrary to the well-known, so reflect.) And it (the potential relation) is their suitability to relate to the contingent existent before its existence, and the accidental existential relation (al-ta'alluq al-tanjizi al-hadith), which is their relation accidentally to the mentioned existent after its existence. His statement: 'the two related (things)' means relationally in an accidental ancient, or potential ancient, or accidental accidental manner, according to the distribution you know. His statement: 'to all existents' means their necessary and contingent. And included in the existents are colors and sounds. As for the modes (akwan) – which are conjunction and disjunction, motion and rest – His hearing and sight do not relate to them because they are among the mental considerations according to the correct view, and what is witnessed is only the thing qualified by them, not they themselves.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage explains the threefold relation of God's hearing and sight. It first mentions the three types: 1) The ancient accidental relation (ta'alluq tanjizi qadim) which is their eternal relation to God's essence and attributes. 2) The ancient potential relation (ta'alluq suluhi qadim) which is their suitability to relate to contingent beings before they exist. A marginal note from al-Anbabi reports a disagreement: some deny this potential relation because it would require relation to a non-existent, while others affirm it, though it is contrary to the well-known view. 3) The accidental existential relation (ta'alluq tanjizi hadith) which is the actual relation after the being exists. Then the commentator explains that the phrase 'المتعلقان' refers to these three types of relation in a distributive sense. Finally, it specifies the objects: the two attributes relate to all existents (necessary and contingent), including colors and sounds, but not to the 'modes' (akwan: conjunction, disjunction, motion, rest) because those are mere mental considerations; what is perceived is the thing qualified by them.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Hearing and sight have no potential relation because they cannot relate to non-existents.
Correction: While some scholars deny it, the passage presents the potential relation as a valid concept according to others, defined as suitability to relate before existence. The debate shows the complexity of divine attributes.

Checkpoint

C) The actual relation after existence.
D) The relation to colors and sounds.

Key Takeaway
God's hearing and sight relate to all existents (necessary and contingent) including colors and sounds, but not to the modes, and this relation is threefold: ancient accidental, ancient potential, and accidental existential.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
God's hearing and sight relate to you even before you existed, in potential. When you sin, does it ever cross your mind that He already knew you would do it? How does that knowledge—that your action was encompassed by His eternal attributes—change how you approach repentance? Will you let it drive you to more sincere brokenness or to careless indifference?
?
The passage notes that scholars disagreed about the potential relation, and the commentator says 'reflect.' When you encounter deep theological differences, does it humble you or make you dismissive? How can you carry that 'reflect' into your own study of God's attributes, not settling for easy answers?
?
You live your life moving, resting, gathering, separating—these modes are not what God's hearing and sight directly perceive, but rather the reality of what you are. Are you more concerned with the outward motions of worship than with the inner reality that God truly hears and sees? What would change if you acted only for His perception of your heart?

The Divine Attribute of Speech (Kalam)
Definition of divine speech with key distinctions and clarifications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the divine attribute of speech (kalam) as an existential attribute, its relation of indication, and how it is diversified by its objects.

Core Idea
God's speech is one attribute, yet becomes command, prohibition, promise, etc., by relation.

This passage from Sanusi's commentator defines the nature of divine speech. It clarifies that speech is an existential attribute subsisting in God's essence, free from temporal and linguistic imperfections. The key distinction is that speech relates to its objects as indication (dalala), not as disclosure (inkishaf), which is the role of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the rhythm, then the literal translation. Notice the definitions and the marginal notes.
Pay attention to the terms 'تعلق دلالة' and 'تعلق انكشاف'.
2
Identify the definition of kalam
The passage says speech is an existential attribute subsisting in God's essence, free from anteriority, posteriority, and linguistic features like lahn, i'rab, sihha, i'lal. What does this tell us about its nature?
It is not like human speech which has temporal and grammatical properties.
3
Distinguish indication from disclosure
Speech relates to its objects (necessary, contingent, impossible) as indication (dalala) - meaning it signifies them - not as disclosure (inkishaf) which is proper to knowledge. Why is this distinction important?
This prevents conflating speech with knowledge.
4
Understand diversification by relations
Though speech is one attribute, it takes on different forms (command, prohibition, promise) depending on what it relates to. Notice the examples given.
This shows that the attribute is not multiple but its effects are diverse.
5
Review marginal notes
The marginal notes clarify points about hearing/sight not implying deficiency, define 'akwan', and give examples of 'other than that' like hamza, idgham, ghunna.
These notes address potential objections and expand on the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَالْكَلَامُ) هو صفة وجودية قائمة بذاته تعالى، مُنَزَّهَةٌ عن التقدم والتأخُر، واللحن والإعراب والصحة والإعلال، وغير ذلك، فيتعلق بما يتعلق به العلم من الواجبات والجائزات والمستحيلات لكن تعلق دِلالة، لا تَعَلَّقَ انكشاف وهي صِفَةً واحدةً، لكنها تتنوع باعتبار تعلقاتها؛ لأنها إن تعلقت بالأمر؛ كانت أمراً، وإن تعلقت بالنهي؛ كانت نهياً، وإن تعلقت بالوعد؛ كانت وَعْداً، وهكذا. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري :(قوله: وَهُوَ صَلاحِيَّتُهُما ... إلخ) وليس في ذلك إثباتُ صمم ولا عمى؛ لأن الموجودَ الحادث حال عدمه ليس من وظيفتهما، فعدم تعلقهما به حال عدمه لا يؤدي إلى ثبوت الصمم والعمى (قوله: وَأَمَّا الْأكْوَانُ جمع كون وهو الحصول في المكان. (قوله: تَعَلَّقَ دِلَالَةٍ) أي: لنا بمعنى أننا لو اطلعنا عليه لفهمنا منه الواجبات والجائزات والمستحيلات، فالمترتب على الكلام انكشاف هذه الثلاثة لنا، والمترتب على العلم انشكافها الله تعالى. تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي . (قوله: وَغَيْرِ ذَلِكَ) كالمةِ والإدغام والغنة.
His statement: 'and speech' – it is an existential attribute subsisting by His essence, exalted, free from anteriority and posteriority, from grammatical errors (lahn), inflection (i'rab), correctness (sihha), and defect (i'lal), and other than that. It relates to what knowledge relates to: the necessary, contingent, and impossible, but as a relation of indication (dalala), not a relation of disclosure (inkishaf). And it is a single attribute, but it is diversified by virtue of its relations; because if it relates to a command, it is a command; if to a prohibition, it is a prohibition; if to a promise, it is a promise; and so on. Marginal note of the scholar Ahmad al-Ujhuri: His statement: 'and it (the potential relation) is their suitability...' etc. – and that does not entail affirmation of deafness or blindness, because the contingent existent during its non-existence is not within their function, so their non-relation to it during its non-existence does not lead to the affirmation of deafness and blindness. His statement: 'As for the modes (akwan)' – it is a plural of kawn, which is being in a place. His statement: 'a relation of indication' – meaning for us, i.e., if we were to know it, we would understand from it the necessary, contingent, and impossible. So what results from speech is the disclosure of these three to us, while what results from knowledge is their disclosure to Allah. Notes of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi: His statement: 'and other than that' – like the hamza (al-ma? ), idgham, and ghunna.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage defines the divine attribute of speech (kalam). It first states that it is an existential attribute subsisting in God's essence, free from temporal properties (anteriority/posteriority) and from linguistic features like grammatical errors, inflection, correctness, and defect. It relates to the same objects as knowledge (necessary, contingent, impossible) but as a relation of indication (dalala) – meaning it signifies them – not as a relation of disclosure (inkishaf), which is proper to knowledge. The attribute is one, yet diversified by its relations: when it relates to a command, it is a command; to a prohibition, a prohibition; to a promise, a promise; etc. Then a marginal note from al-Ujhuri clarifies a point about hearing and sight: their potential relation does not imply deafness or blindness because they are not expected to relate to non-existent things. Another note defines 'akwan' as 'being in a place' (al-husul fi al-makan). The next note explains that the relation of indication means that if we were to know God's speech, we would understand the categories from it – speech discloses these to us, while knowledge discloses them to Allah. Finally, a note from al-Anbabi gives examples of 'other than that' such as hamza, idgham, and ghunna.

Key Terms
كلام
صفة وجودية
تعلق دلالة
تعلق انكشاف
أمر
نهي
وعد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That divine speech is multiple attributes or that it discloses objects directly like knowledge.
Correction: Speech is one attribute that indicates (dalala) its objects, not disclose them; its diversification is by relation, not essence.

Checkpoint
How does the divine attribute of speech relate to the necessary, contingent, and impossible?
A) As a relation of disclosure (inkishaf).
B) As a relation of indication (dalala).
C) As a relation of creation.
D) As a relation of existence.

Key Takeaway
God's speech is one existential attribute that signifies necessary, contingent, and impossible realities, and it takes on diverse forms like command and prohibition only through its relations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you receive a command from God, do you treat it as the very speech of the One who knows all things, or do you weigh it against your desires? What one command will you obey today out of reverence for His speech?
?
The source teaches that God's speech indicates truth to us, but His knowledge discloses it to Himself. In seeking knowledge, do you prioritize being taught by His speech (the Quran) over your own opinions? How will you adjust your learning habits starting now?
?
If God's speech is one attribute that becomes command, prohibition, and promise, then every divine command carries the same weight. Which command have you been neglecting as 'minor'? Will you commit to fulfilling it this week?

Scope of Divine Hearing and Sight: Excluding Accidental States
Bajuri explains the scope of divine hearing and sight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine hearing and sight do not attach to accidental states like motion and rest, and how this does not imply any deficiency in God.

Core Idea
Divine perception reaches all real existents, not mere mental states.

This lesson clarifies a nuanced point about God's attributes of hearing and sight. While they encompass all created beings and qualities like colors and sounds, they do not directly perceive accidental states (akwan) because these are not real entities but mental considerations. The glosses address potential misunderstandings about divine perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the main passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Note how the author first specifies the scope (all existents) then excludes akwan.
This passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify what is included and excluded
The passage says divine hearing/sight attach to all existents (necessary and contingent), including colors and sounds. They do not attach to akwan: conjunction, separation, motion, rest.
What are akwan? They are relational states in place.
3
Understand why akwan are excluded
Akwan are 'mental considerations' (umur i'tibariyya) — they have no independent reality. What is actually perceived is the entity qualified by them, not the states themselves.
This is the correct view according to the author.
4
Examine the gloss on non-deficiency
The gloss from al-Ajhuri addresses the worry: not perceiving non-existent things does not imply deafness or blindness, because those attributes function only when the object exists.
God's perfection is preserved.
5
Reflect on the key takeaway
Divine attributes are perfect and attach to all real things. Limitations in attachment are due to the nature of the object, not any defect in God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: بِجَمِيعِ الْمَوْجُودَاتِ) أي: واجبها وجائِزها. ودخل في الموجودات: الألوان والأصوات، وأما الأكوان ـ وهي الاجتماع والافتراق، والحركة والسكون؛ فلا يتعلَّقُ بها سَمْعُهُ تعالى وبَصَرُه؛ لأنها من الأمور الاعتبارية على الصحيح، والمشاهَدُ إنما هو المُتَّصِفُ بها لا هي.

حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري
:(قوله: وَهُوَ صَلاحِيَّتُهُما ... إلخ) وليس في ذلك إثباتُ صمم ولا عمى؛ لأن الموجودَ الحادث حال عدمه ليس من وظيفتهما، فعدم تعلقهما به حال عدمه لا يؤدي إلى ثبوت الصمم والعمى.
(قوله: وَأَمَّا الْأكْوَانُ جمع كون وهو الحصول في المكان.)
تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي .
(قوله: وَغَيْرِ ذَلِكَ) كالمةِ والإدغام والغنة.
(His saying: 'with all existents') means the necessary and the contingent. Included in existents are colors and sounds. As for the accidental states (akwan) – which are conjunction and separation, motion and rest – His hearing and sight do not attach to them, because they are among the mentally-posited matters according to the correct view, and what is perceived is only the entity qualified by them, not they themselves.

Gloss of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri: (His saying: 'and it is their potentiality... etc.') In that there is no affirmation of deafness or blindness; because the originated existent in the state of its non-existence is not within their function, so their non-attachment to it in the state of its non-existence does not lead to the affirmation of deafness and blindness.
(His saying: 'and as for al-akwan' – the plural of kawn, which is occurrence in place.)
Annotations of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi: (His saying: 'and other than that') such as idgham and ghunna.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage clarifies the scope of divine hearing and sight. It begins by stating that these attributes attach to all existents, both necessary (wajib) and contingent (ja'iz). Specifically, they perceive colors and sounds. However, the 'akwan' – namely conjunction, separation, motion, and rest – are not perceived directly by these senses because they are mental considerations ('umur i'tibariyya) according to the correct position. What is directly observed is the entity that possesses these states, not the states themselves. The margin from al-Ajhuri addresses a potential misunderstanding: this limited attachment does not imply defects like deafness or blindness in God, because the non-existent originated thing (when it does not exist) is not within the function of these attributes; their non-attachment does not equate to their absence. Al-Anbabi then adds that 'akwan' is the plural of 'kawn', meaning occurrence in place, and that 'other than that' includes phonological phenomena like idgham and ghunna.

Key Terms
السمع
البصر
الأكوان
الموجودات
الأمور الاعتبارية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God does not perceive akwan, He must have a deficiency (deafness or blindness).
Correction: Not perceiving purely mental considerations does not imply a defect, because these attributes only pertain to real existents. The lack of attachment is due to the object's nature, not God's capacity.

Checkpoint
Why do divine hearing and sight not attach to 'akwan' (e.g., motion, rest)?
A) Because they are non-existent
B) Because they are mental considerations, not real entities
C) Because they are too subtle
D) Because God does not perceive them at all

Key Takeaway
Divine hearing and sight attach to all real existents but not to accidental states, which are merely mental considerations, and this does not diminish God's perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are still or in motion, do you truly believe that God sees the substance of your being and not just the surface? How would your actions change today if you internalized that God's hearing and sight are fixed on the essence of your worship, not the fleeting accidents?
?
The lesson teaches that God's perception does not attach to mental constructs like social status or worldly reputation. Are you living for what truly exists in His sight or for shadows that will fade? What one thing can you abandon today that is merely a mental consideration?
?
Since God's hearing and sight encompass all real existents but not accidental states, does reassurance that He perceives your heart's true state move you to be more sincere in your private moments? Will you let this knowledge strip away any pretense in your next prayer?

Defining Divine Speech: One Attribute, Many Attachments
Sanusi defines divine speech and explains its variation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of divine speech as a single eternal attribute that varies in name according to its attachments, and the distinction between the mode of indication and the mode of disclosure.

Core Idea
Divine speech is one attribute; its variety comes from attachments, not change.

In this passage, Sanusi defines divine speech (al-kalam) with precision, stating it is an entitative attribute subsisting in God's essence, free from temporal and linguistic limitations. He then explains how this one attribute relates to the objects of divine knowledge through a mode of indication, not direct disclosure, and how it takes on different names—command, prohibition, promise—based on what it attaches to. This lesson unpacks each element of this definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal English translation. Notice the concise style and the key terms.
Pay attention to the terms 'تعلق دلالة' and 'تعلق انكشاف'.
2
Identify the definition and transcendence
Find how Sanusi defines divine speech: as an entitative attribute, and list what it is free from (temporal sequence, grammatical features, etc.).
The list includes 'التقدم والتأخُر، واللحن والإعراب والصحة والإعلال'.
3
Understand the two modes of attachment
Contrast 'تعلق دلالة' (attachment of indication) with 'تعلق انكشاف' (attachment of disclosure). Reflect on why speech uses indication rather than disclosure.
The gloss by al-Ajhuri explains that indication means we understand the three categories from speech, while knowledge discloses them to God.
4
See how one attribute varies
Notice that despite being one attribute, it is called command, prohibition, promise, etc., depending on its attachment. This is a key point: the attribute itself does not change.
The text says: 'وهي صفة واحدة، لكنها تتنوع باعتبار تعلقاتها'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَالْكَلَامُ) هو صفة وجودية قائمة بذاته تعالى، مُنَزَّهَةٌ عن التقدم والتأخُر، واللحن والإعراب والصحة والإعلال، وغير ذلك، فيتعلق بما يتعلق به العلم من الواجبات والجائزات والمستحيلات لكن تعلق دِلالة، لا تَعَلَّقَ انكشاف وهي صِفَةً واحدةً، لكنها تتنوع باعتبار تعلقاتها؛ لأنها إن تعلقت بالأمر؛ كانت أمراً، وإن تعلقت بالنهي؛ كانت نهياً، وإن تعلقت بالوعد؛ كانت وَعْداً، وهكذا.

حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري
(قوله: تَعَلَّقَ دِلَالَةٍ) أي: لنا بمعنى أننا لو اطلعنا عليه لفهمنا منه الواجبات والجائزات والمستحيلات، فالمترتب على الكلام انكشاف هذه الثلاثة لنا، والمترتب على العلم انشكافها الله تعالى.
تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي .
(قوله: وَغَيْرِ ذَلِكَ) كالمةِ والإدغام والغنة.
(His saying: 'and speech (al-kalam)') is an entitative attribute subsisting in His essence, exalted above anteriority and posteriority, solecism, declension, soundness, defection, and other such things. It attaches to what knowledge attaches to, of the necessary, contingent, and impossible, but in the mode of indication (tata'luq dalala), not the mode of disclosure (tata'luq inshiraf). It is a single attribute, but it varies by virtue of its attachments: if it attaches to a command, it is a command; if it attaches to a prohibition, it is a prohibition; if it attaches to a promise, it is a promise; and so on.

Gloss of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri: (His saying: 'attachment of indication') means for us, i.e., if we were to perceive it, we would understand from it the necessary, contingent, and impossible. So what results from speech is the disclosure of these three to us, while what results from knowledge is their disclosure to God.
Annotations of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi: (His saying: 'and other than that') such as idgham and ghunna.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage defines divine speech (al-kalam) as an entitative attribute (sifa wujudiyya) subsisting in God's essence. It is transcendent: free from temporal sequence (before/after), from grammatical features (lahn, i'rab), from soundness and defection (siha, i'lal), etc. This attribute attaches to the same objects as knowledge: the necessary, contingent, and impossible, but in a mode of 'indication' (tata'luq dalala) – it conveys meaning – not in the mode of 'disclosure' (tata'luq inshiraf) which would reveal the objects directly. Although it is a single attribute, it varies according to its attachments: when it attaches to a command, it becomes command; to a prohibition, prohibition; to a promise, promise; and so on. The gloss by al-Ajhuri specifies that 'attachment of indication' means that we, if we were to perceive this eternal speech, would understand the three categories (necessary, contingent, impossible) from it. Knowledge discloses them to God; speech discloses them to us. Al-Anbabi adds an example of 'other than that' including idgham and ghunna (phonological terms) as features not applicable to divine speech.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's speech is composed of letters and sounds or that it changes over time.
Correction: The text states it is one eternal attribute, free from linguistic features and temporal sequence, and its variation is only in name based on attachments.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, how does divine speech vary into command, prohibition, promise, etc.?
A) It has different parts
B) It attaches to different objects
C) It changes over time
D) It is multiple attributes

Key Takeaway
Divine speech is a single eternal attribute that conveys meaning about necessary, contingent, and impossible things through indication, and takes different names based on what it addresses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a command from God in the Quran, do you treat it as a fleeting instruction or as the eternal speech of the One who never changes? If you truly believe it is one attribute addressing you, would your obedience become more constant? What would you need to change to make that a reality starting today?
?
The passage says God's speech indicates but does not unveil. Are you content to live by what God has indicated through revelation, or do you secretly wish for a direct unveiling? What does that desire reveal about the state of your faith and patience? Describe one area where you have been impatient with God's indications and how you will submit differently this week.
?
Divine speech is one, yet it takes the form of promises and warnings. Which of these do you take more seriously in your daily life—the promise of reward or the threat of punishment? If you are honest, is your motivation based more on fear of the warning or hope in the promise? How would that balance need to shift to reflect the truth that both come from the same single attribute of your Lord?

The Two Meanings of 'Speech': Eternal Attribute and Recited Words
Bajuri clarifies the dual usage of 'speech' and asserts that verbal speech signifies the same meaning as eternal speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the term 'speech' is used both for God's eternal attribute and for the recited words of scripture, and that the recited words indicate the same meaning as the eternal attribute.

Core Idea
The Quran's words signify the same meaning as God's eternal speech.

Before we can explore how the Quran relates to God's attribute of speech, we must clarify a fundamental ambiguity: the word 'speech' itself is used in two distinct senses. This lesson unpacks those two senses and establishes that the recited Quran conveys the very same content as the eternal attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how the author introduces the dual usage of 'speech'.
The passage begins with a parallel structure: 'just as... so too...'
2
Identify the two senses of 'speech'
The first sense is the eternal attribute subsisting in God's essence. The second sense is the recited words we read. Note the example from Aisha.
Aisha's statement is used to show that 'speech' can refer to the written words.
3
Understand the verification (tahqiq)
The author asserts that the Quran indicates what the eternal attribute indicates. He gives the example of the prohibition verse: the meaning understood from the words is the same as would be understood from the attribute.
This is a key point: the signification (madlul) is identical.
4
Notice the nuanced conclusion
The passage concludes that the signification of verbal speech is the signification of internal speech, or at least like it, because they differ in the signifier (words vs. attribute).
This prepares for the next lesson on how the words themselves point to the attribute.
5
Reflect on the core idea
Ask yourself: How can created words have the same meaning as an eternal attribute? This is the mystery of divine speech that the next lesson will clarify further.
The answer lies in the distinction between the meaning and the signifier.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكما يُطلَقُ الكلام على الصفة القديمة القائمة بذاته تعالى؛ يُطْلَقُ على الألفاظ التي نقرؤها، ومنه قول عائشة : مَا بَيْنَ دَفْتَيِ الْمُصْحَفِ كَلَامُ اللهِ تعالى، أي مخلوق له ليس من تأليف المخلوقين، وقد نَصَّ المصنف وغيره على أن الصفةَ القديمة مدلولة لذلك، لكن التحقيق أن القرآن ونحوه كالتوراة يَدلُّ على ما تَدُلُّ عليه الصفةُ القديمة، مثلاً إذا سمعت قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَلَا نَقْرَبُوا الزِنَى ﴾ فهمت منه النهي عن قُرْبان الزنا، ولو أزيل عنك الحجابُ لفَهِمْتَ من الصفة القديمة هذا المعنى، فمدلول الكلام اللفظي هو مدلول الكلام النفسي. وإن شئت قلت: هو مثله لتغايرهما باعتبار الدالّ الألفاظ نعم؛ التي نقرؤها تَدُل على الكلام القديم بطريق الدلالة الالتزامية العرفية؛ لأن كلَّ من له كلامٌ لفظيّ؛ لَزِمَ عُرْفاً أن له كلاماً نفسياً، والمولى له كلامٌ لفظي، بمعنى: أنه خَلَقَهُ في اللوح المحفوظ، فيدلُّ عُزفاً على أن له تعالى كلاماً نفسياً.
والحاصل أن الكلام اللفظي باعتبار دلالته المطابقيَّة يدلُّ على مثل مدلول الكلام القديم كما قاله بعض المتأخرين، وباعتبار دلالته الالتزامية العرفية يدلّ على نَفْسِ الكلام القديم كما قاله السنوسي. أفاده في حاشيته الكبرى".

حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري.
(قوله: تَدلُّ) أي: يدل انتسابها الله على أن له كلاماً نفسياً، فثبوت الكلام النفسي له تعالى معلوم من انتساب ألفاظ القرآن له، لا من نفس الألفاظ كما لا يخفى؛ إذ المعلوم من نفس الألفاظ إنما هو مدلول الصفة القديمة لا نفسها.
تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبايي .
(قوله: وَمِنْهُ قَوْلُ عَائِشَةَ... إلخ) يَظْهَرُ قول عائشة المذكور من قبيل ما أُطلِقَ فيه الكلام على النقوش لا على الألفاظ إلا أن يُتَجَوْزَ.
And just as 'speech' is applied to the eternal attribute subsisting in His essence, it is also applied to the words we recite. From this is the saying of Aisha: 'What is between the two covers of the mushaf is the speech of Allah,' i.e., created by Him, not the composition of creatures. The author and others stated that the eternal attribute is what is signified by that. But the verification is that the Qur'an and similar (e.g., the Torah) indicate what the eternal attribute indicates. For example, if you hear His saying: 'And do not approach zina' (al-Isra:32), you understand from it the prohibition of approaching zina. If the veil were removed from you, you would understand this meaning from the eternal attribute. So the signification of the verbal speech is the signification of the internal speech (al-kalam al-nafsi). If you wish, you can say: it is like it, because they differ in terms of the signifier. Indeed, the words we recite indicate the eternal speech via the way of conventional implicatory indication (al-dalala al-iltizamiyya al-'urfiyya), because everyone who has verbal speech conventionally has internal speech, and the Lord has verbal speech in the sense that He created it in the Preserved Tablet, so it conventionally indicates that He has internal speech.
In conclusion: verbal speech, by virtue of its correspondence indication (dalala mutabiqa), indicates something like the signification of eternal speech, as some later scholars said; and by virtue of its conventional implicatory indication, it indicates the eternal speech itself, as al-Sanusi said. This was informed in his great gloss.

Gloss of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri: (His saying: 'indicates') i.e., its attribution to God indicates that He has internal speech. So the affirmation of internal speech for Him is known from the attribution of the words of the Qur'an to Him, not from the words themselves, as is clear; for what is known from the words themselves is only the signification of the eternal attribute, not the attribute itself.
Annotations of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi: (His saying: 'and from this is the saying of Aisha... etc.') It appears that the mentioned saying of Aisha is of the type in which speech is applied to the script (nuqush) rather than the words, unless one takes it metaphorically.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage discusses the relation between God's eternal attribute of speech (al-kalam al-qadim) and the recited words of scripture. It begins by noting that the term 'speech' is used both for the eternal attribute and for the words we recite. The example of Aisha's statement that the content of the mushaf is 'speech of Allah' is given; the author clarifies that the eternal attribute is what is intended by that. However, the verification (tahqiq) offered is that the Qur'an and similar scriptures indicate (yadullu) what the eternal attribute indicates. For instance, hearing the verse 'do not approach zina' conveys the prohibition from approaching zina; if the veil were removed, one would understand that same meaning from the eternal attribute. Thus the signification of the verbal speech is the same as the signification of the internal speech (al-kalam al-nafsi). Alternatively, one can say it is like it, because although different things (words vs. attribute), the meaning is the same.
Then the passage explains that the words indicate the eternal speech via conventional implicatory indication (al-dalala al-iltizamiyya al-'urfiyya): conventionally, anyone who has verbal speech has internal speech; God has verbal speech in the sense that He created it in the Preserved Tablet, so that indicates that He has internal speech.
The conclusion distinguishes two indications: correspondence indication (dalala mutabiqa) shows that the verbal speech indicates a meaning like that of eternal speech (as some later scholars held), while implicatory indication shows that it indicates the eternal speech itself (as al-Sanusi held).
Al-Ajhuri's gloss clarifies that the attribution of the words to God indicates that He has internal speech; the words themselves (their meaning) only indicate the object of the attribute (the content), not the attribute itself.
Al-Anbabi notes that Aisha's statement seems to apply 'speech' to the actual script (inscriptions) rather than to the spoken words, unless it is taken metaphorically.

الكلام اللفظي
الكلام النفسي
مدلول

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the recited words of the Quran are identical to God's eternal attribute of speech.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the recited words and the eternal attribute share the same meaning (madlul) but are distinct in terms of the signifier (dall). The words are created, whereas the attribute is eternal and subsisting in God's essence.

Checkpoint
According to the verification (tahqiq) in this passage, what does the Quran indicate?
A) It indicates the eternal attribute itself.
B) It indicates what the eternal attribute indicates (the same meaning).
C) It indicates something completely different from the eternal attribute.
D) It indicates only the literal words without meaning.

Key Takeaway
The recited Quran and God's eternal speech share the same meaning, though they are distinct as signifier and signified.

Two Ways the Quran Indicates God's Eternal Speech
Bajuri presents two ways verbal speech indicates eternal speech: correspondence and conventional implicatory, and summarizes the views of later scholars and al-Sanusi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the recited Quran indicates God's eternal speech through both correspondence indication and conventional implicatory indication, and the views of later scholars and al-Sanusi.

Core Idea
The Quran indicates eternal speech by correspondence (same meaning) and by implicatory indication (via convention).

Having established that the recited Quran conveys the same meaning as God's eternal attribute, Bajuri now explains how the words themselves point to the very attribute of speech. He distinguishes two types of indication: correspondence (al-mutabaqa) and conventional implicatory (al-iltizamiyya al-'urfiyya). This lesson unpacks these two relationships and the positions of scholars.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text and its translation. Notice the shift from the previous lesson: now we focus on how the words indicate the attribute itself.
The passage starts with 'n'am' (yes) connecting to the previous thought.
2
Identify the two types of indication
The passage first presents the conventional implicatory indication (dalala iltizamiyya 'urfiyya): because anyone with verbal speech has internal speech, God's having verbal speech implies He has internal speech. Then it presents the correspondence indication (dalala mutabiqa): verbal speech indicates 'like' the meaning of eternal speech.
These two indications are distinct but complementary.
3
Understand the reasoning behind implicatory indication
The reasoning is that conventionally, if someone speaks (has verbal speech), they necessarily have internal speech. Since God created the Quran in the Preserved Tablet, He has verbal speech, so He must have internal speech. This is a logical implication based on custom.
This does not mean God speaks with a voice; it's a metaphysical attribute.
4
Note the summary and scholars' views
The conclusion summarizes: verbal speech via correspondence indicates something like the meaning of eternal speech (according to some later scholars), and via implicatory indication indicates the eternal speech itself (according to al-Sanusi). The glosses clarify that the attribution of the Quran to God indicates internal speech, not the words themselves.
Al-Sanusi's position is that the words point to the attribute itself, not just its meaning.
5
Reflect on the glosses
Al-Ajhuri's gloss emphasizes that it is the attribution of the words to God (not the words themselves) that indicates internal speech. Al-Anbabi notes that Aisha's statement might refer to the script, not the spoken words.
These nuances help prevent over-simplification.

If the words of the Quran are not themselves God's eternal speech but only indicate it, does that make your recitation less powerful—or does it demand a deeper reverence for the attribute they signify? What will you change in your relationship with the Quran starting today?
?
The passage teaches that the attribution of the Quran to God is what grounds the proof of His eternal speech. When you say 'Allah said…', do you fully internalize that you are affirming His eternal attribute, not just the text? How will you ensure your tongue aligns with your heart?

Attachments of Command and Indication of Speech
Sanusi explains the attachments of command/prohibition and the indication of verbal to eternal speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of attachments for divine command and prohibition according to the Ash'aris, and how verbal speech indicates eternal speech through conformity and customary implicative indications.

Core Idea
Verbal speech points to eternal speech in two ways: by meaning and by customary indication.

Sanusi establishes a key distinction: while most divine attributes have a single eternal attachment, command and prohibition have both an eternal potential attachment and a temporal actualizing attachment. He then clarifies the relationship between the recited words of the Qur'an and the eternal attribute of speech, showing how they are connected yet distinct.

Guided Path
1
Understand the exceptional attachments
Read the first sentence about command and prohibition. Notice that unlike other attributes, they have two attachments: one eternal and potential, one temporal and actualizing.
The term 'suluhiyyan' refers to potential or fitness, and 'tanzijiyyan' refers to actualizing or executive.
2
Distinguish verbal and eternal speech
See how the term 'speech' applies both to the eternal attribute and to the recited words. Compare Aisha's statement and the author's clarification.
Verbal speech is created; eternal speech is uncreated and subsists in Allah's essence.
3
Learn the two types of indication
Focus on the two ways verbal speech indicates eternal speech: conformity indication (dalala mutabaqiyya) for similar meaning, and customary implicative indication (dalala iltizamiyya urfiyya) for the attribute itself.
Customary implication relies on the common practice that whoever has verbal speech must have internal speech.
4
Review the conclusion and marginal notes
Read the concluding summary and the marginal notes by al-Ajhuri and al-Anbabi. They clarify that the attribution of words to Allah indicates the eternal attribute, not the words themselves.
Al-Anbabi notes that Aisha's statement is about the written script, not the words, unless metaphorically.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجميع هذه التعلقات تنجيزية قديمة، إلا الأمر والنهي عند الأشاعرة، فلهما تعلقان صلوحيَّان قديمان قبل وجود المكلفين وتنجيزيان حادثان بعد وجودهم. وكما يُطلَقُ الكلام على الصفة القديمة القائمة بذاته تعالى؛ يُطْلَقُ على الألفاظ التي نقرؤها، ومنه قول عائشة : مَا بَيْنَ دَفْتَيِ الْمُصْحَفِ كَلَامُ اللهِ تعالى، أي مخلوق له ليس من تأليف المخلوقين، وقد نَصَّ المصنف وغيره على أن الصفةَ القديمة مدلولة لذلك، لكن التحقيق أن القرآن ونحوه كالتوراة يَدلُّ على ما تَدُلُّ عليه الصفةُ القديمة، مثلاً إذا سمعت قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَلَا نَقْرَبُوا الزِنَى ﴾ فهمت منه النهي عن قُرْبان الزنا، ولو أزيل عنك الحجابُ لفَهِمْتَ من الصفة القديمة هذا المعنى، فمدلول الكلام اللفظي هو مدلول الكلام النفسي. وإن شئت قلت: هو مثله لتغايرهما باعتبار الدالّ الألفاظ نعم؛ التي نقرؤها تَدُل على الكلام القديم بطريق الدلالة الالتزامية العرفية؛ لأن كلَّ من له كلامٌ لفظيّ؛ لَزِمَ عُرْفاً أن له كلاماً نفسياً، والمولى له كلامٌ لفظي، بمعنى: أنه خَلَقَهُ في اللوح المحفوظ، فيدلُّ عُزفاً على أن له تعالى كلاماً نفسياً. والحاصل أن الكلام اللفظي باعتبار دلالته المطابقيَّة يدلُّ على مثل مدلول الكلام القديم كما قاله بعض المتأخرين، وباعتبار دلالته الالتزامية العرفية يدلّ على نَفْسِ الكلام القديم كما قاله السنوسي. أفاده في حاشيته الكبرى. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري. (قوله: تَدلُّ) أي: يدل انتسابها الله على أن له كلاماً نفسياً، فثبوت الكلام النفسي له تعالى معلوم من انتساب ألفاظ القرآن له، لا من نفس الألفاظ كما لا يخفى؛ إذ المعلوم من نفس الألفاظ إنما هو مدلول الصفة القديمة لا نفسها. تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبايي . (قوله: وَمِنْهُ قَوْلُ عَائِشَةَ... إلخ) يَظْهَرُ قول عائشة المذكور من قبيل ما أُطلِقَ فيه الكلام على النقوش لا على الألفاظ إلا أن يُتَجَوْزَ.
All these attachments are actualizing and eternal, except for command and prohibition according to the Ash'aris, for they have two attachments: one potential and eternal before the existence of the obligated, and one actualizing and temporal after their existence. Just as the term 'speech' is applied to the eternal attribute subsisting in His essence, it is applied to the words we recite, and from this is the saying of Aisha: 'What is between the covers of the Mushaf is the speech of Allah', meaning a created thing from Him, not from the authorship of creatures. The author and others stated that the eternal attribute is indicated by that, but the verification is that the Qur'an and similar books like the Torah indicate what the eternal attribute indicates. For example, if you hear the saying of Allah Most High, 'And do not approach adultery,' you understand from it the prohibition of approaching adultery, and if the veil were removed from you, you would understand this meaning from the eternal attribute. So the meaning of verbal speech is the meaning of internal speech. And if you wish you may say: it is like it, because they differ in terms of the signifier. Yes, the words we recite indicate the eternal speech by way of customary implicative indication, because everyone who has verbal speech customarily must have internal speech, and the Lord has verbal speech in the sense that He created it in the Preserved Tablet, so it indicates customarily that He has internal speech. The conclusion: verbal speech, by virtue of its conformity indication, indicates a meaning similar to the meaning of eternal speech, as some later scholars said, and by virtue of its customary implicative indication, indicates the very eternal speech, as Sanusi said. He stated this in his great marginal commentary. Marginal note of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri: (His saying: 'indicate') meaning: its attribution to Allah indicates that He has internal speech, so the existence of internal speech for Him is known from the attribution of the words of the Qur'an to Him, not from the words themselves, as is not hidden; for what is known from the words themselves is only the meaning of the eternal attribute, not the attribute itself. Remarks of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi: (His saying: 'and from this is the saying of Aisha... etc.') It appears that the aforementioned saying of Aisha is of the type in which speech is applied to the written script, not to the words, unless by metaphor.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage defines the two types of attachments for the divine command and prohibition, and explains the relationship between eternal speech (kalam nafsi) and recited words (kalam lafzi). First, it states that all attachments of the attributes are 'tanzijiyya qadima' (actualizing and eternal), except for command and prohibition according to the Ash'aris, which have two attachments: one 'suluhiyyan qadiman' (potential and eternal) before the existence of the obligated, and one 'tanzijiyyan hadithan' (actualizing and temporal) after their existence. Then it explains that the term 'kalam' is applied both to the eternal attribute and to the recited words, citing Aisha's statement that what is between the covers of the Mushaf is the speech of Allah, meaning it is a created thing from Him, not authored by creatures. The author (Sanusi) and others stated that the eternal attribute is indicated by that, but the verification is that the Qur'an and similar books like the Torah indicate what the eternal attribute indicates. For example, when you hear the verse 'And do not approach adultery,' you understand the prohibition of approaching adultery, and if the veil were removed you would understand this meaning from the eternal attribute. So the meaning of verbal speech is the meaning of internal speech. Alternatively, it is like it, because they differ in the signifier. The recited words indicate the eternal speech by customary implicative indication (dalala iltizamiyya 'urfiyya) because whoever has verbal speech customarily has internal speech. Allah has verbal speech in the sense that He created it in the Preserved Tablet, so it indicates customarily that He has internal speech. The conclusion: verbal speech, by its conformity indication (dalala mutabaqiyya), indicates a meaning similar to that of eternal speech, as some later scholars said, and by its customary implicative indication, indicates the very eternal speech, as Sanusi said. The marginal notes by al-Ajhuri and al-Anbabi add clarifications: al-Ajhuri explains that the attribution of the words to Allah indicates that He has internal speech, and the knowledge of that internal speech comes from the attribution, not from the words themselves. al-Anbabi notes that Aisha's statement is an application of the term 'speech' to the written script (nuqush) rather than to the words, unless by metaphor.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the recited words of the Qur'an are identical to the eternal attribute of speech.
Correction: The passage clarifies they are distinct: verbal speech is created, eternal speech is uncreated; verbal speech indicates eternal speech by conformity and customary indication, not by identity.
