The Ash'ari Doctrine on Divine Speech: Refuting Letters and Sounds
Al-Bajuri defends the Ash'ari view of divine speech as not consisting of letters and sounds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari school denies that Allah's speech consists of letters and sounds, and how it refutes two opposing views.

Core Idea
Allah's speech is beyond letters and sounds — a timeless attribute, not a created utterance.

This passage from al-Bajuri's Hashiya clarifies the Ash'ari stance on the nature of divine speech. It first affirms that Allah's speech is not composed of letters or sounds, then confronts two opposing views: that of al-‘Adud al-Iji and a group claiming to follow the Hanabila. Understanding these refutations is essential to grasping the Ash'ari concept of the attribute of speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: first the affirmation, then two refutations.
Pay attention to the technical terms: حرف, صوت, تقدم, تأخر.
2
Identify the Ash'ari position
Locate the statement that divine speech is not a letter nor a sound. This is the well-known view of Ahl al-Sunna.
3
Study the refutation of al-‘Adud
Al-‘Adud claimed that divine speech consists of ancient letters and sounds. The refutation is that this would imply precedence and posteriority in Allah's speech, which is impossible. The answer shows that precedence/posteriority arise from articulation points, which Allah is free from.
Notice al-Bajuri's remark that this view came from the Hashwiyya and is unreliable.
4
Study the refutation of the Hanbali-like group
A group claiming to be Hanabila said letters/sounds are ancient when attributed to Allah and temporal when attributed to creatures. Al-Bajuri calls this false. Al-Ajhuri explains they mistakenly applied the attribute of speech to the Qur'an itself.
Contrast this with the proper Ash'ari distinction between the eternal attribute and the created Qur'an.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حاشية البيجوري على السنوسية (قوله: الَّذِي لَيْسَ بحَرْفٍ وَلَا صَوْتٍ) هذا هو المشهور عند أهل السنة. وقال العَضُدُ: إنه بحروف وأصوات قديمةٍ، ويلزمُ عليه - كما قال المتأخرون - أن كلامه تعالى فيه التقدُّمُ والتأخُرُ. لكن أجيب عن ذلك بأن حروفنا إنما جاءها التقدم والتأخُرُ من اختلاف المخارج، ومَنْ تَنَزَّه عن ذلك تنزه كلامه عن ذلك. وهذا الكلام إنما سَرَى للعضد من الحشوية، فلا يُعَوَّلُ عليه وقال جماعةً نسبوا أنفسهم إلى الحنابلة : إنه بحروف وأصوات، لكن إن نُسِبَتْ إليه تعالى؛ كانت قديمةً، وإن نسبت إلى الحوادث؛ كانت حادثةً ولا يخفى بطلان هذا الكلام. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري (قوله: وَقَالَ جَمَاعَةٌ... إلخ) الظاهر أن هؤلاء حملوا كلام الله المعدود من الصفات على القرآن، فجعلوا حروفه بالنسبة إلى الله قديمةً، وبالنسبة إلينا حادثة.
Al-Bajuri's marginal commentary on the Sanusiyya: (His saying: 'that which is not a letter nor a sound') This is the well-known view of the Ahl al-Sunna. Al-‘Adud said: It is with ancient letters and sounds, and it follows - as the later scholars said - that His speech contains precedence and posteriority. But that was answered by saying that our letters derive precedence and posteriority from the difference of the points of articulation, and whoever is transcendent above that, his speech is transcendent above that. This view came to al-‘Adud from the Hashwiyya, so it is not relied upon. And a group who attributed themselves to the Hanabila said: It is with letters and sounds, but if attributed to Him Most High they are ancient, and if attributed to temporal events they are temporal. The falsity of this statement is not hidden. Marginal note of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri: (His saying: 'and a group said... etc.') It appears that these people applied the speech of Allah counted among the attributes to the Qur'an, and thus made its letters ancient relative to Allah and temporal relative to us.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage refutes two deviant views on the nature of divine speech. First, it affirms the Ash'ari position that Allah's speech is not composed of letters and sounds. It then presents the view of al-‘Adud al-Iji who claimed it is with ancient letters and sounds. The refutation is that this would entail temporal precedence and posteriority in the divine speech, which is impossible. The response to this objection is that precedence and posteriority arise from articulation places, which are absent in Allah. Al-Bajuri notes that this view came to al-‘Adud from the Hashwiyya and is therefore unreliable. Second, it presents the view of a group claiming to follow Hanbali school: that letters and sounds are ancient when attributed to Allah, but temporal when attributed to creatures. Al-Bajuri declares this false. Al-Ajhuri explains that these people mistakenly took the attribute of speech to refer to the Qur'an itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine speech is composed of letters and sounds.
Correction: The Ash'ari school maintains that Allah's speech is an eternal attribute not consisting of letters or sounds, as letters/sounds imply temporality and change.

Checkpoint
What is the flaw in al-‘Adud's view that divine speech consists of ancient letters and sounds?
A) It implies creation
B) It implies precedence and posteriority
C) It implies multiplicity
D) It implies limitations

Key Takeaway
Allah's speech is an eternal non-composite attribute, free from letters and sounds, and any view that assigns letters/sounds to it is refuted.

Speech and Knowledge Share the Same Objects
Al-Bajuri states that the divine attribute of speech relates to the same three categories as knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the divine attribute of speech relates to the same three categories as knowledge (necessary, possible, impossible), but differs in how it relates to them.

Core Idea
Speech and knowledge share the same objects but not the same mode of relation.

In this passage, Al-Bajuri clarifies a subtle point about the divine attribute of speech. Just as knowledge encompasses all things—necessary, possible, and impossible—so too does speech. However, the way speech relates to these objects is distinct from knowledge. This lesson unpacks that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the two parts: first a statement of equality and difference, then a listing of the three categories.
2
Identify the key terms
Focus on 'mutalliq' (related objects) and the three categories: wajibat (necessary), jaizat (possible), mustahilat (impossible).
3
Understand the distinction
The passage says speech equals knowledge in 'what it relates to' but differs in 'the mode of relation.' Ask yourself how they might differ.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: بِمَا يَتَعَلَّقُ بِهِ... إلخ) أشار بذلك إلى أنه مساو للعلم في المتعلق، لكنه يخالفه في التعلّق كما علم مما مر. (قوله: مِنَ الْمُتَعَلَّقَاتِ) - بفتح اللام ، وتلك المتعلقات هي الواجبات والجائزات والمستحيلات.
(His saying: 'by what it relates to... etc.') He indicated by that that it is equal to knowledge in the related object, but it differs from it in the mode of relation as known from what passed. (His saying: 'from the related objects') - with the lam having fatha, and those related objects are the necessary, the possible, and the impossible.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This brief passage clarifies a point about the attribute of speech. It states that the attribute of speech is similar to the attribute of knowledge in terms of the scope of what it relates to: both relate to the three categories of existents: the necessary (wajibat), the possible (jaizat), and the impossible (mustahilat). However, speech differs from knowledge in the mode of relation (ta‘alluq). The passage notes that this was already explained earlier.

The Seven Ma‘nawiyya Attributes and Their Rank
Al-Bajuri explains the rank of ma‘nawiyya attributes and critiques al-Shaktani.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the attributes of ma‘ani and ma‘nawiyya, why al-Shaktani posits a rank difference, and the critique that such gradation is not applicable to Allah's attributes.

Core Idea
All divine attributes are equal in rank; gradation is rejected.

Al-Bajuri here addresses a subtle point about the classification of divine attributes. He introduces two categories: attributes of meaning (ma‘ani) and quidditive attributes (ma‘nawiyya). The word 'thumma' (then) in the text implies a ranking, but he critically examines whether any differentiation can truly apply to Allah's perfect attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification of attributes
Read the passage to see how al-Bajuri distinguishes between ma‘ani (existing and visible) and ma‘nawiyya (established but not visible).
2
Understand the role of 'thumma'
Note that 'thumma' indicates a lower rank for ma‘nawiyya. Al-Shaktani explains this by saying ma‘ani can be seen if the veil is removed, whereas ma‘nawiyya cannot.
This is based on the idea that existence is necessary for visibility.
3
Identify al-Shaktani's argument
Al-Shaktani claims ma‘nawiyya are inferior because they lack existence (wujud) that qualifies for vision (ru’ya).
4
Grasp the critique
Al-Bajuri objects that there is no disparity (tafawut) among Allah's attributes, so the ranking is invalid.
This reflects the principle that all divine attributes are equally necessary and perfect.
5
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider why rejecting gradation in attributes is important for maintaining God's perfection and unity.
The critique aligns with the Ash‘ari view of divine simplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: ثُمَّ سَبْعُ صِفَاتِ... إلخ) معطوف على قوله: (سبع صفات تسمى صفات المعاني وحينئذ فالمعنى: ثم يَجِبُ له تعالى سبع صفات... إلخ، وإنما عطف بـ (ثم)؛ لأن رتبة المعنوية دون رتبة المعاني؛ لأن المعاني صفات موجودة تُمْكِنُ رؤيتها لو أُزِيلَ عنا الحجاب بخلاف المعنوية، فإنها ثابتة فقط، ولا تمكن رؤيتها؛ لأنها لم تنصف بالوجود المصجّح للرؤية. هكذا قال الشكتاني. وفيه نظر؛ لأنه لا تفاوتَ في صفاته تعالى.
(His saying: 'Then seven attributes... etc.') is conjoined to his saying: 'Seven attributes called attributes of ma‘ani.' And therefore the meaning is: Then He, may He be exalted, must have seven attributes... etc. And he conjoined with 'thumma' because the rank of ma‘nawiyya is below the rank of ma‘ani; because the ma‘ani are existing attributes that can be seen if the veil is removed, unlike the ma‘nawiyya, for they are only established and cannot be seen because they are not characterized by existence that qualifies them for vision. This is what al-Shaktani said. And there is consideration in it, because there is no gradation in His attributes.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage explains the phrase 'seven attributes' in the context of divine attributes. It classifies attributes into two types: ma‘ani (attributes of meaning) which are existing and can be seen, and ma‘nawiyya (quidditive attributes) which are merely established and cannot be seen. The word 'thumma' (then) indicates that the rank of ma‘nawiyya is lower. However, a critique is offered: this gradation is problematic because there is no inequality among the attributes of Allah. The passage thus presents al-Shaktani's view and then raises an objection.

Key Terms
صفات المعاني
صفات المعنوية
رتبة
وجود
رؤية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some divine attributes are of a higher rank than others.
Correction: The passage argues that there is no gradation (tafawut) in the attributes of Allah, rejecting al-Shaktani's claim that ma‘nawiyya attributes are inferior because they cannot be seen.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Shaktani say the ma‘nawiyya attributes are of a lower rank than the ma‘ani attributes?
A) Because they are not necessary
B) Because they do not exist
C) Because they cannot be seen
D) Because they are temporal

Key Takeaway
The seven ma‘nawiyya attributes are not inferior to the ma‘ani attributes; all divine attributes are equally perfect and beyond gradation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself treating different aspects of Allah—such as His mercy versus His justice—as if one is more important? How does al-Bajuri's rejection of any gradation challenge that tendency, and what will you change today to ensure you honor all attributes equally?
?
When you think about the afterlife and the vision of Allah (ru’ya), do you subconsciously assume that what can be seen is more real or valuable? How does this lesson correct that assumption, and what concrete shift in your understanding of Allah's perfection does it demand?
?
Al-Bajuri corrects a respected scholar. Is there a belief you hold about Allah that might imply inequality among His attributes, perhaps one you've never examined? Will you commit to spending time in reflection (tafakkur) this week to uproot any hidden gradation in your heart?

Divine Speech Without Letters or Sounds
Al-Bajuri states the Ash'ari position on divine speech and refutes opposing views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari doctrine that divine speech is not composed of letters or sounds, and how its proponents refute opposing views.

Core Idea
God's speech is an eternal attribute, not a sequence of sounds.

This passage from al-Bajuri's Hashiya clarifies the standard Ash'ari position on the nature of divine speech. After stating the Ash'ari view, he addresses two opposing opinions attributed to al-'Adud and a group claiming to follow the Hanabila. The lesson unpacks these positions and the arguments against them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage in Arabic and English
Start by reading the Arabic source carefully, then its literal translation. Notice the condensed style typical of kalam texts.
Pay attention to the key terms: kalam, harf, sawt, qadim, hadith.
2
Identify the three positions
The passage presents the Ash'ari view (not letters/sounds), al-'Adud's view (letters/sounds, ancient), and a Hanabila-influenced group's view (letters/sounds, ancient relative to God, temporal relative to creation).
Each view is followed by a refutation or dismissal.
3
Understand the objection to al-'Adud
The objection is that if speech had letters, it would involve precedence and delay. The response says that in created speech, that comes from articulation points; God is beyond that.
Al-Bajuri dismisses al-'Adud's view as derived from the Hashwiyya.
4
Analyze the Hanabila group's view
They claimed relativity: letters are ancient as God's attribute but temporal as human recitation. The author says this is clearly invalid.
The marginal note explains they confused the attribute of speech with the created Qur'an.
5
Reflect on the core doctrine
The Ash'ari position is that divine speech is a single eternal attribute, not a composite of letters/sounds. The Qur'an is God's speech, but its recited form is a created expression.
This avoids making God subject to temporal sequence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: الَّذِي لَيْسَ بحَرْفٍ وَلَا صَوْتٍ) هذا هو المشهور عند أهل السنة. وقال العَضُدُ: إنه بحروف وأصوات قديمةٍ، ويلزمُ عليه - كما قال المتأخرون - أن كلامه تعالى فيه التقدُّمُ والتأخُرُ. لكن أجيب عن ذلك بأن حروفنا إنما جاءها التقدم والتأخُرُ من اختلاف المخارج، ومَنْ تَنَزَّه عن ذلك تنزه كلامه عن ذلك. وهذا الكلام إنما سَرَى للعضد من الحشوية، فلا يُعَوَّلُ عليه وقال جماعةً نسبوا أنفسهم إلى الحنابلة : إنه بحروف وأصوات، لكن إن نُسِبَتْ إليه تعالى؛ كانت قديمةً، وإن نسبت إلى الحوادث؛ كانت حادثةً ولا يخفى بطلان هذا الكلام. (حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري: قوله: وَقَالَ جَمَاعَةٌ... إلخ) الظاهر أن هؤلاء حملوا كلام الله المعدود من الصفات على القرآن، فجعلوا حروفه بالنسبة إلى الله قديمةً، وبالنسبة إلينا حادثة.
His saying: 'Which is not a letter nor a sound' – this is the well-known (position) among the People of the Sunnah. Al-'Adud said: It is with letters and ancient sounds, and it entails – as later scholars said – that His speech has precedence and delay. But it was answered to that that our letters only acquire precedence and delay from the difference of articulation points, and whoever is transcendent beyond that, his speech is transcendent beyond that. This speech only spread to al-'Adud from the Hashwiyya, so it is not relied upon. And a group who attributed themselves to the Hanabila said: It is with letters and sounds, but if attributed to Him, they are ancient, and if attributed to temporal events, they are temporal – and the invalidity of this speech is not hidden. (Marginal note of al-'Allama Ahmad al-Ajhuri: his saying: 'and a group said...' etc.) Apparently these people carried the speech of God counted among the attributes to the Qur'an, so they made its letters ancient with respect to God and temporal with respect to us.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'ari position on divine speech (kalam Allah) through a series of definitions and refutations. The author begins by defining the Ash'ari view: divine speech is not composed of letters or sounds. He then presents two opposing views. First, al-'Adud's claim that divine speech consists of ancient letters and sounds. The objection to this is that it would imply precedence and delay in God's speech, which is impossible. The answer to this objection is that letters acquire precedence and delay from articulation points, and God is free from such; thus His speech is also free. However, the author dismisses al-'Adud's view as derived from the Hashwiyya and unreliable. Second, a group claiming to follow the Hanabila asserts that divine speech is with letters and sounds but, depending on attribution, can be ancient or temporal. The author declares this view clearly invalid. The marginal note from al-Ajhuri clarifies that this group identifies God's attribute of speech with the Qur'an, making its letters ancient as God's speech and temporal as human recitation. The passage thus reinforces the standard Ash'ari doctrine that divine speech is a single eternal attribute, not composed of letters or sounds.

Key Terms
كلام الله (kalam Allah)
حرف (harf)
صوت (sawt)
قديم (qadim)
حادث (hadith)
حشوية (Hashwiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think divine speech consists of actual letters and sounds that are eternal, similar to created speech.
Correction: The Ash'ari position is that divine speech is an eternal attribute without letters or sounds; letters and sounds are properties of created speech, not applicable to God.

Checkpoint
What is the Ash'ari position on divine speech according to this passage?
A) It consists of letters and sounds.
B) It is neither a letter nor a sound.
C) It is a letter but not a sound.
D) It is a sound but not a letter.

Key Takeaway
Divine speech is an eternal attribute without letters or sounds, unlike created speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever subconsciously pictured God speaking like a human being, with a voice and letters? How does this lesson correct that? What can you do to cultivate awe when you recite the Qur'an, knowing it reflects an eternal attribute?
?
This passage dismisses two views as invalid. Which of those views might your own heart lean towards, and why? What would it cost you to fully accept the Ash'ari position? Will you sincerely probe your own assumptions today?
?
If God's speech has no letters or sounds, then your recitation is a created act pointing to the uncreated. Does your heart feel the difference when you recite? Pledge to pause before your next recitation and consciously affirm: 'This is not like God's speech, but it conveys it.'

The Scope of Divine Speech Equals That of Knowledge
Al-Bajuri explains the scope of divine speech relative to knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine speech relates to the same three categories of things as divine knowledge, but in a different mode.

Core Idea
Divine speech speaks of all that God knows: the necessary, the possible, and the impossible.

Al-Bajuri clarifies a fundamental point about the attribute of divine speech (kalam). While it may seem that speech only involves commands and prohibitions, its scope is actually identical to that of divine knowledge. This passage explains that speech relates to everything that knowledge relates to—the necessary, the possible, and the impossible—though the nature of the relation differs.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Notice the technical terms used: muta'allaq, ta'alluq, wajib, ja'iz, mustahil.
The passage is a commentary on an earlier text, so it begins with a parenthetical quote.
2
Identify the three categories of objects
The passage lists the objects of divine speech as 'al-wajibat, al-ja'izat, al-mustahilat'—the necessary, possible, and impossible. These are the same as the objects of divine knowledge.
These three categories cover all that can be conceived.
3
Understand the distinction between object and relation
Although speech and knowledge share the same objects (muta'allaq), they differ in the mode of relation (ta'alluq). Knowledge is cognitive, speech is expressive. Appreciate this subtle point.
The Arabic word 'ta'alluq' indicates how an attribute relates to its object.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: بِمَا يَتَعَلَّقُ بِهِ... إلخ) أشار بذلك إلى أنه مساو للعلم في المتعلق، لكنه يخالفه في التعلّق كما علم مما مر. (قوله: مِنَ الْمُتَعَلَّقَاتِ) - بفتح اللام ، وتلك المتعلقات والجائزات والمستحيلات. هي الواجبات
His saying: 'by what it is related to... etc.' He indicated by that that it is equal to knowledge in the thing-related-to, but it differs from it in the relation, as known from what has passed. His saying: 'from the things-related-to' – with fat-ha on the lam – and those things-related-to are the necessary things, the possible things, and the impossible things. They are the obligatory matters.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage defines the scope of the attribute of divine speech (kalam) in relation to the attribute of knowledge ('ilm). The author explains that the phrase 'by what it is related to' indicates that speech shares the same set of objects (mut'allaqat) as knowledge. These objects are the three categories: the necessarily existent (wajib), the possibly existent (ja'iz), and the impossibly existent (mustahil). However, speech differs from knowledge in the mode of relation (ta'alluq): knowledge is a cognitive relation, while speech is an expressive or communicative relation. The author also notes that the word 'al-muta'allaqat' is pronounced with fatha on the lam, which is a grammatical point. The passage thus clarifies that God's speech encompasses everything that is known, but in a distinct manner.

Key Terms
تعلق (ta'alluq)
متعلق (muta'allaq)
واجب (wajib)
جائز (ja'iz)
مستحيل (mustahil)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think divine speech only relates to commands and prohibitions, or only to things that actually exist.
Correction: Divine speech actually relates to all things: necessary (God's essence and attributes), possible (creations), and impossible (like a second god). It encompasses all that is conceivable.

Checkpoint
To what objects does divine speech relate according to this passage?
A) Only necessary things.
B) Only possible things.
C) The necessary, possible, and impossible.
D) Only the impossible.

Key Takeaway
Divine speech covers all that God knows, but as communication rather than cognition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If God's speech includes even the impossible, then nothing is beyond His expression. Is there any area of your life that you think God cannot address or understand? How will you bring that area before Him in prayer?
?
The lesson says divine speech is equal in scope to knowledge but differs in relation. God's knowledge and speech are distinct yet harmonious aspects. Does your own speech reflect what you truly know, or do you often say things without understanding? How can you align your speech with your knowledge of truth?

Why 'Thumma' Is Used for the Seven Attributes
Al-Bajuri explains the reason for using 'thumma' when listing the seven attributes of meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the conjunction 'thumma' (then) is used when listing the seven attributes of meaning (ma'ani) and their derivative attributes (ma'nawiyya), and why the preferred explanation is based on conceptual priority rather than ontological superiority.

Core Idea
'Thumma' indicates conceptual order, not lesser rank.

In this passage, al-Bajuri explains the use of the particle 'thumma' when presenting the seven attributes of meaning and the subsequent derived attributes. He first presents a view that suggests a lower rank for the derived attributes, but then critiques it and offers a more precise explanation based on the order of intellection. This lesson unpacks his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic text once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the use of 'thumma'.
2
Identify the first explanation
Al-Bajuri initially presents al-Shaknani's view that 'thumma' indicates a lower rank for the ma'nawiyya because they are not visible.
This explanation is based on ontological difference.
3
Examine the critique
The author finds this problematic because there is no distinction in Allah's attributes. He also rejects al-Qarafi's claim of superiority.
The critique upholds the equality of attributes.
4
Understand the preferred explanation
The better explanation is that 'thumma' indicates an order in intellection: you cannot conceive of the state of being powerful without first conceiving of power.
This preserves the equality while explaining the sequence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: ثُمَّ سَبْعُ صِفَاتِ... إلخ) معطوف على قوله: (سبع صفات تسمى صفات المعاني وحينئذ فالمعنى: ثم يَجِبُ له تعالى سبع صفات... إلخ، وإنما عطف بـ (ثم)؛ لأن رتبة المعنوية دون رتبة المعاني؛ لأن المعاني صفات موجودة تُمْكِنُ رؤيتها لو أُزِيلَ عنا الحجاب بخلاف المعنوية، فإنها ثابتة فقط، ولا تمكن رؤيتها؛ لأنها لم تنصف بالوجود المصجّح للرؤية. هكذا قال الشكتاني". وفيه نظر؛ لأنه لا تفاوُتَ في صفاته تعالى. وقولُ القَرَافِيّ بأفضلية بعض الصفات الوجودية على بعض؛ مردود، وحينئذ فالأولى أن يقال: إنما عَطَفَ بـ ( ثم) لترتب المعنوية على المعاني في التعقل؛ إذ لا يُعْقَلُ الكونُ قادراً إلا بعد تعقل القدرة، ولا يعقل الكونُ مريداً إلا بعد تعقل الإرادة وهكذا.
His saying: 'Then seven attributes... etc.' is coordinated with his saying: 'seven attributes called attributes of meanings.' And so the meaning is: Then it is necessary for Him, exalted, seven attributes... etc. And it is coordinated with 'thumma' only because the rank of the ma'nawiyya is lower than the rank of the ma'ani; because the ma'ani are existing attributes that can be seen if the veil were removed from us, unlike the ma'nawiyya, for they are only fixed and cannot be seen because they were not characterized by existence that makes sight valid. Thus said al-Shaknani. But there is consideration in it, because there is no distinction in His attributes, exalted. And the saying of al-Qarafi about the superiority of some existential attributes over others is rejected. And so the better is to say: He coordinated with 'thumma' only for the ordering of the ma'nawiyya after the ma'ani in intellection; for the state of being powerful is not intellected except after intellection of power, and the state of being willing is not intellected except after intellection of will, and so on.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage explains the use of the conjunction 'thumma' (then) when listing the seven attributes of meaning (sifat al-ma'ani) followed by the derived attributes (sifat ma'nawiyya). The author first cites al-Shaknani's view: 'thumma' indicates a lower rank of ma'nawiyya because ma'ani are existent and potentially visible, while ma'nawiyya are merely fixed and not visible. This view is criticized because there is no distinction in God's attributes. Qarafi's claim that some existential attributes are superior is also rejected. The preferred explanation is that 'thumma' indicates an order in intellection (ta'aqqul): one cannot conceive of God being powerful (kawn qadir) without first conceiving of power (qudra), and similarly for will, etc. Thus the conjunction reflects a conceptual priority, not ontological superiority.

Key Terms
صفات المعاني
صفات معنوية
ثم
تعقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'thumma' indicates that the ma'nawiyya (derived attributes) are ontologically inferior or lesser in rank compared to the ma'ani (attributes of meaning).
Correction: The passage clarifies that all attributes are equal in perfection; 'thumma' only indicates a conceptual order in the mind, not any real distinction in God's essence.

Checkpoint
According to the preferred explanation in the passage, why is 'thumma' used?
A) Because ma'nawiyya are lower in rank.
B) Because ma'nawiyya are not visible.
C) To indicate a conceptual order in intellection.
D) Because ma'ani are superior in existence.

Key Takeaway
The use of 'thumma' when listing the seven attributes does not imply any superiority or inferiority among God's attributes; rather, it reflects the conceptual order in which the mind must grasp them.

How can you train your heart to hold all His attributes with equal reverence? Will you today make a list of all seven qualities and actively affirm each as perfect?
?
The correct understanding of 'thumma' requires you to conceive of power before being powerful. In your own life, do you seek to understand God's power (qudra) before acting as if He is the All-Powerful? Or do you rush to claim His attributes without first meditating on their reality? What step will you take this week to deepen your conceptual knowledge of just one of His attributes?
?
Al-Shaknani was a great scholar, yet this lesson shows that even he held a view that needed correction. Are you humble enough to admit when your understanding of God's perfection is flawed? What false notion about God's attributes have you held onto that you need to abandon today—not in theory, but in the way you actually think of Him?

Refuting Superiority of Attributes: The Function of 'Thumma'
Al-Bajuri refutes al-Qarafi and clarifies the function of 'thumma'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Qarafi's claim of superiority among God's attributes is rejected and how the conjunction 'thumma' in the attribute list indicates a mental order, not superiority.

Core Idea
The conjunction 'thumma' in attributes indicates logical order, not superiority.

In this passage, al-Bajuri addresses a common misunderstanding about the order of God's essential attributes. Some scholars, like al-Qarafi, thought that the conjunction 'thumma' (then) implied that some attributes are superior to others. Al-Bajuri firmly rejects this view and provides the correct interpretation: 'thumma' indicates a mental ordering, where the attributive states (like being powerful) are conceived only after the underlying meanings (like power) are understood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice that al-Bajuri first rejects a claim, then provides the correct explanation.
The passage is concise but packed with meaning.
2
Identify the refutation
Find the part where al-Bajuri rejects al-Qarafi's view. What is the claim being rejected?
The rejected claim is about 'afdaliyya' (superiority) among existential attributes.
3
Understand the function of 'thumma'
Al-Bajuri explains that 'thumma' indicates mental ordering. He gives examples: the state of being powerful is conceived after power, etc. See how this ordering is logical, not hierarchical.
The key phrase is 'li-tarattub al-ma'nawiyya 'alā al-ma'ānī fī al-ta'aqqul'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولُ القَرَافِيّ بأفضلية بعض الصفات الوجودية على بعض؛ مردود، وحينئذ فالأولى أن يقال: إنما عَطَفَ بـ ( ثم) لترتب المعنوية على المعاني في التعقل؛ إذ لا يُعْقَلُ الكونُ قادراً إلا بعد تعقُل القدرة، ولا يعقل الكونُ مريداً إلا بعد تعقل الإرادة وهكذا.
And the statement of al-Qarafi about the superiority of some existential attributes over others is rejected. And then it is more appropriate to say: He only conjoined with (thumma) for the ordering of the attributive states (al-ma'nawiyya) after the meanings (al-ma'ani) in conceptualization; for the state of being powerful (al-kawn qādiran) is not conceived except after conceiving power (al-qudra), and the state of being willing (al-kawn murīdan) is not conceived except after conceiving will (al-irāda), and so on.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage is a refutation and correction. Al-Bajuri first rejects the view of Imam al-Qarafi, who claimed that some of God's existential attributes (ṣifāt wujūdiyya) are superior to others. He then provides the correct interpretation: the conjunction 'thumma' (then) in the order of the seven essential attributes is not for superiority but for mental ordering. The attributive states (al-ma'nawiyya) such as God's being powerful (kawnuhu qādiran) and being willing (kawnuhu murīdan) are understood only after the underlying meanings (al-ma'ānī) of power and will are conceived. Thus, the order is logical, not hierarchical.

الصفات الوجودية
ثم
ترتب المعنوية على المعاني

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the conjunction 'thumma' implies temporal sequence or superiority among God's attributes.
Correction: Al-Bajuri clarifies that 'thumma' indicates only mental order: the attributive states are conceived after the underlying meanings, not temporally or hierarchically.

Checkpoint

A) Temporal sequence
B) Superiority of some attributes
C) Mental order of attributive states after meanings
D) A stylistic device

Key Takeaway
The conjunction 'thumma' in the order of God's attributes indicates a logical mental ordering, not superiority or temporal sequence.

Mutual Entailment of Attributive Attributes and Their Definitions
Al-Bajuri explains mutual entailment and defines attributive attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the seven attributive attributes are mutually entailed with the seven essential attributes and how each attributive attribute is defined as an intermediate between existence and non-existence.

Core Idea
Attributive attributes are not separate; they are mutually entailed with essential attributes.

In this passage, al-Bajuri clarifies the intricate relationship between the essential attributes (power, will, etc.) and their attributive counterparts (being powerful, being willing). He affirms mutual entailment but also notes a one-sided aspect when considering causality. Finally, he defines each attributive attribute as a state concomitant with its essential attribute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim of mutual entailment
Read the opening sentence where al-Bajuri states that the attributive attributes are concomitant with the seven essential attributes. Notice that he says this implies mutual entailment from both sides.
The phrase 'al-talāzum min al-jānibayn' means entailment from both sides.
2
Understand the nuance of causality
Al-Bajuri then notes that if we consider the attributive attributes as effects (maʿlūlāt) and the essential attributes as causes (ʿilal), the entailment becomes one-sided: the attributive attributes are necessary concomitants of the essential attributes.
This is because an effect is necessary for its cause.
3
Study the definition of each attributive attribute
Read the definition of 'being powerful' as an intermediate (wāsiṭa) between existence and non-existence and as a concomitant of power. Understand that similarly, 'being willing' is defined relative to will, and the same applies to the rest.
The term 'wāsiṭa bayn al-mawjūd wa al-maʿdūm' is a technical term meaning a state that is neither fully existent nor non-existent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حاشية البيجوري على السنوسية (قوله: وَهِيَ مُلَازِمَةٌ لِلسَّبْعِ الأولى مقتضاه أن التلازم من الجانبين، وهو كذلك، وإن كان مقتضى جَعْلِهم لها معلولةً، وجعلهم السبع الأولى عِلَلاً: أن المعنوية هي اللازمة فقط؛ لأن المعلول لازم لعلته. للقدرة. (قوله: وَهِيَ كَوْنُهُ تَعَالَى قَادِراً هو واسِطَةٌ بين الموجود والمعدوم ملازمةٌ للقدرة. (وقوله: وَمُريداً) أي: وكونه تعالى مريداً ، وهو واسطة بين الموجود والمعدوم ملازمة للإرادة، وهكذا يُقال في الباقي.
Al-Bajuri's gloss on Umm al-Barāhīn: (His saying: 'And they are concomitant with the seven first') its implication is that the entailment is from both sides, and it is so, even though the implication of their making them caused (maʿlūla) and making the seven first causes (ʿilal) is that the attributive attributes are only the necessary (al-lāzima) because the caused (maʿlūl) is necessary for its cause. For power. (His saying: 'And they are His being powerful (kawnuhu taʿālā qādiran) is an intermediate (wāsiṭa) between the existent and the non-existent, concomitant (mulāzima) with power.' (And his saying: 'and willing') i.e., His being willing, and it is an intermediate between the existent and the non-existent, concomitant with will, and likewise is said for the rest.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage explains the relationship between the seven essential attributes (al-sabʿ al-ūlā: power, will, knowledge, etc.) and the seven attributive attributes (al-ṣifāt al-maʿnawiyya: being powerful, willing, knowing, etc.). Al-Bajuri states that they are mutually entail (al-tazāzum min al-jānibayn), meaning each implies the other. However, if we consider the attributive attributes as effects (maʿlūlāt) and the essential attributes as causes (ʿilal), then the attribution is one-sided: the attributive attributes are necessary concomitants (lāzima) of the essential attributes, because an effect is necessary for its cause. The gloss 'lil-qudra' ('for power') seems to be a marginal note specifying that this applies to power. Then he defines each attributive attribute: 'being powerful' (kawnuhu qādiran) is an intermediate state (wāsiṭa) between existence and non-existence, and it is concomitant with the attribute of power. Similarly, 'being willing' is concomitant with will, and so on for the rest of the attributes. This underscores that the attributive attributes are not separate from the essential attributes but are their necessary manifestations.

Key Terms
ملازمة
التلازم من الجانبين
معلولة
علل
واسطة بين الموجود والمعدوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the attributive attributes are separate from essential attributes.
Correction: This passage shows they are mutually entailed or consequent; the attributive attributes are not independent but are necessary results of the essential attributes.

Checkpoint
According to al-Bajuri, what is the relationship between the attributive attributes and the essential attributes?
A) They are completely independent
B) They entail each other from both sides
C) The essential attributes are caused by the attributive
D) There is no relationship

Key Takeaway
The attributive attributes are not independent; they are the necessary concomitants of the essential attributes and are defined as intermediate states between existence and non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is Powerful,' do you think of that as a separate description or as a necessary consequence of His essence? How does that affect the certainty with which you rely on His power in your daily life?
?
The passage describes attributive attributes as 'intermediate between existence and non-existence.' Can you think of a personal experience where you felt a quality of Allah was 'intermediate'—not fully grasped yet real? How might that awareness change your prayer?
?
If the seven essential attributes are causes of the attributive attributes, then your knowledge of them is built on a chain. What does this entailment demand of you in terms of trusting not just one attribute but the entire interconnected reality of Allah's nature?

Introduction to the Twenty Impossible Attributes
Al-Bajuri introduces the twenty impossible attributes and explains their dependence on the theory of 'ahwal'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theoretical basis for the classification of twenty impossible attributes and the role of 'ahwal' in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
The twenty attributes depend on affirming the reality of 'ahwal'.

This passage introduces the second section of what is obligatory to know about Allah: the impossible attributes. Al-Bajuri clarifies that only twenty are detailed and explains that this enumeration rests on a specific theological framework, namely the affirmation of states (ahwal). Let's read the passage to see how he sets up this foundational concept.

Guided Path
1
Identify the context
Read the first part of the passage where Al-Bajuri explains that this is the second section of what is obligatory for the responsible person to know, and that the author only details some of the impossible attributes—specifically twenty.
2
Understand the phrase 'in His right'
Notice how Al-Bajuri clarifies that 'in His right' means 'upon His essence'. This is a key terminological point.
This phrase appears in many theological contexts.
3
Grasp the theoretical basis
Focus on the last part where Al-Bajuri states that the enumeration of twenty attributes is built on the position affirming 'ahwal' and the fourfold division of things, as opposed to the position negating 'ahwal'.
This shows that attribute classification is not arbitrary but rooted in deep theological methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: وَمِمَّا يَسْتَحِيلُ فِي حَقِّهِ تَعَالَى... إلخ) هذا هو القسم الثاني مما يَجِبُ على المكلف معرفته، وهو ما يستحيلُ في حقه تعالى لكن المصنف لم يُبين جميع ما يستحيل في حقه تعالى، بل بَعْضَهُ، وهو المستحيل على سبيل التفصيل، وهو العشرون الآتية كما أشار لذلك بقوله: (ومما يستحيل... إلخ)، وقد تقدَّم توضيح ذلك. فتنته. (قوله: فِي حَقِّهِ تَعَالَى) أي: على ذاته تعالى، فـ (في) بمعنى: على، وحق بمعنى الذات كما مرَّ نظيره. (قوله: عِشْرُونَ صِفَةً قد علمت أن هذا مبني على القول بثبوت الأحوال المبني على الطريقة القائلة بأن الأشياء أربعة أقسام موجودات ومعدومات وأحوال وأمور اعتبارية، لا على القول بنفي الأحوال المبني على الطريقة القائلة بأن الأشياء ثلاثة أقسام فقط كما تقدم بيانُه.
His statement: 'And from what is impossible in His right, exalted is He... etc.' This is the second section of what is obligatory for the responsible person to know, which is what is impossible in His right, exalted is He, but the author did not explain all that is impossible in His right, exalted is He, rather some of it, which is the impossible in detail, and it is the twenty that follow, as he indicated by his statement: 'And from what is impossible... etc.' and its clarification has already preceded. So be attentive. His statement: 'In His right, exalted is He' means upon His essence, exalted is He, so 'fi' means 'upon', and 'right' means 'essence' as its equivalent has passed. His statement: 'Twenty attributes you have known that this is based on the position affirming the states (ahwal), based on the method that things are four divisions: existents, non-existents, states, and mental considerations, not on the position negating the states, based on the method that things are only three divisions, as its explanation has preceded.'
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage is an introductory definition and classification. Al-Bajuri explains that the section on impossible attributes is the second obligatory topic for the responsible person. He notes that the author (Sanusi) only details some of the impossible attributes—specifically twenty. The commentary then clarifies the phrase 'in His right' means 'upon His essence'. Finally, he explains that the enumeration of twenty attributes depends on accepting the reality of 'ahwal' (states), which follows the view that things are four categories (existents, non-existents, states, and mental considerations), as opposed to the view that things are only three categories (excluding states). This theological framework underpins the attribute classification.

Key Terms
مستحيل
أحوال
موجودات
معدومات
أمور اعتبارية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The twenty attributes are based on a simple list without theological background.
Correction: Al-Bajuri clarifies that the enumeration relies on the acceptance of states (ahwal), a key theological position with a specific ontology of things.

Checkpoint
According to al-Bajuri, on what theoretical basis is the enumeration of twenty impossible attributes built?
A) The negation of 'ahwal' and the threefold division of things
B) The affirmation of 'ahwal' and the fourfold division of things
C) The acceptance of only existents and non-existents
D) The rejection of all classifications

Key Takeaway
The enumeration of the twenty impossible attributes is built on the theological acceptance of states (ahwal).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

The Twenty Impossible Attributes as Opposites: Linguistic vs. Technical
Al-Bajuri explains why the twenty impossible attributes are called opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the twenty impossible attributes are called 'opposites' in a broad linguistic sense and how they differ from strict technical opposites.

Core Idea
‘Opposite’ here means absolute contradiction, not the technical rule of two existents with utmost difference.

Al-Bajuri begins by explaining the author’s use of the term 'opposites' for the twenty impossible attributes. He shows that the author chose a broad, linguistic meaning to avoid implying that God’s attributes are temporal. This lesson unpacks that reasoning and previews the various logical relationships among the attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Note the key terms like 'ضد' and 'نقيض' and how they are used.
2
Identify the main claim
Al-Bajuri argues that 'opposite' here is used in its linguistic sense, not the technical sense. Find the sentence where he states this explicitly.
Look for the phrase 'المراد بالضد هنا: المعنى اللغوي'.
3
Understand the technical definition
The passage gives the technical definition of two opposites: two existent things with utmost difference, not coexisting but both absentable. Compare this with the linguistic sense.
Example: blackness and whiteness.
4
Note the variety among the twenty
Al-Bajuri mentions that among the twenty, some are true opposites, some are contradictories, etc. This sets up a more detailed classification later.
This is a preview, not a full breakdown.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: وَهِيَ أَضْدَادُ الْعِشْرِينَ الْأُولَى أي: الأَوَّل ضد للأول، والثاني للثاني، وهكذا على الترتيب المتقدّم في الواجبات وأطلق المصنف الأضداد على المقابل لصفاته تعالى، ولم يَعْكِس؛ لأن صفاته تعالى قديمة، فلا تكون ضدّاً لغيرها. هكذا يُؤْخَذُ من كلام الشيخ يس. ويُحِثَ فيه: بأن التضاد: نسبة من الجانبين فكلُّ منهما ضِدُّ للآخر، ولا يلزمُ من ذلك كَوْنُ صفاته تعالى حادثةً؛ لأن الضدَّ كما يُطْلَقُ على الحادث؛ يُطْلَقُ على القديم والمراد بالضد هنا: المعنى اللغوي، وهو مُطْلَقُ المُنَافي، وإلا؛ فليست هذه العشرون كلُّها أضداداً للعشرين الأولى بالمعنى الاصطلاحي؛ لأن الضدين في الاصطلاح هما الأمران الوجوديَّان اللذان بينهما غايةُ الخلاف، لا يجتمعان، وقد يرتفعان كالسواد والبياض وليست هذه العشرون كلُّها كذلك، بل بعضُها ضد، وبعضها نقيض، وبعضُها مُسَاءٍ للنقيض، وبعضُها أخصُّ من النقيض كما ستقف عليه إن شاء الله تعالى.
His statement: 'And they are opposites of the first twenty' meaning: the first is opposite to the first, the second to the second, and so on according to the order that preceded in the necessary attributes. And the author used 'opposites' for what corresponds to His attributes, exalted is He, and did not reverse, because His attributes, exalted is He, are eternal, so they cannot be opposite to other than them. Thus it is taken from the words of Sheikh Yas. And it is urged therein: that opposition is a relation from both sides, so each of them is opposite to the other, and that does not necessitate that His attributes, exalted is He, be temporal, because 'opposite' as applied to the temporal is also applied to the eternal. And what is meant by 'opposite' here is the linguistic meaning, which is absolute contradiction; otherwise, these twenty are not all opposites to the first twenty in the technical sense, because the two opposites in terminology are the two existent things between which there is utmost difference, they do not coexist, and they may both be absent, like blackness and whiteness. And these twenty are not all like that, rather some of them are opposites, some are contradictories, some are similar to the contradictory, and some are more specific than the contradictory, as you will come to know if God wills.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is a terminological classification. Al-Bajuri explains the author's statement that the twenty impossible attributes are 'opposites' (أضداد) of the first twenty necessary attributes. He clarifies that 'opposite' here is used in a broad linguistic sense, meaning any contradictory attribute, not the strict technical sense of two co-existent things that cannot meet and may both be absent. He reasons that using the technical sense would imply God's attributes are temporal, which is false. Instead, the term 'opposite' is applied to both eternal and temporal. He then notes that in technical terminology, opposites are existent things with utmost difference (like black and white). He concludes that among these twenty, some are true opposites, some are contradictories, some are similar to contradictories, and some are more specific than contradictories, promising further detail.

Key Terms
ضد
نقيض
معنى لغوي
معنى اصطلاحي
غاية الخلاف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The twenty impossible attributes are all technically opposite to the twenty necessary attributes.
Correction: Only some are technical opposites; others are contradictories or other logical relations. The term 'opposite' here is used in a broad linguistic sense.

Checkpoint
Why did the author use the linguistic meaning of 'opposite' rather than the technical meaning?
A) To avoid implying that God's attributes are temporal
B) Because the technical meaning only applies to created things
C) Because the author preferred the linguistic meaning
D) Because the twenty are all non-existents

Key Takeaway
The term 'opposite' for the impossible attributes is used linguistically to mean absolute contradiction, not the strict technical sense, and among the twenty, various logical relationships exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's attributes, do you sometimes slip into assuming that human language captures them perfectly? How does this lesson's distinction between linguistic and technical meaning challenge that assumption? What will you do to guard against sloppy thinking about the Divine?
?
The rebuttal in this passage protects the eternity of Allah's attributes. Have you ever felt uncomfortable that theological terms might imply something unworthy of Allah? How will you from today insist on precise language when speaking about your Lord?
?
This lesson shows that not all 'opposites' are the same. In your own understanding of tawheed, are there areas where you have lumped different concepts together in a way that blurs important distinctions? Commit to studying one such area this week.

The Ten Types of Likeness to Created Things
Sanusi enumerates ten types of likeness to created things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ten types of likeness (mumāthala) to created things that are impossible for Allah, and the logical relation between likeness and otherness (mukhālafa).

Core Idea
Likeness to creation is impossible for Allah in ten distinct ways.

Sanusi begins by explaining that the term 'likeness' (mumāthala) extends to bodies and accidents, and that it stands in opposition to 'otherness' (mukhālafa) to created things. He then lists ten specific ways in which likeness to originated things would be impossible for Allah, forming a foundational taxonomy in Ash‘ari theology. This lesson presents that enumeration and explains each type.

Guided Path
1
Understand the logical opposition
Read the opening lines explaining how mumāthala and mukhālafa are opposites. Note that mukhālafa is the negation of mumāthala, so they are true negations of each other.
This mirrors earlier logic in the text.
2
Learn the ten types
Go through each of the ten listed types of likeness: being a body, accident, direction, etc. Notice the order and what each type entails.
These are all impossible for Allah.
3
Reflect on the purpose
Why does the author list these ten? Consider that they cover all possible ways a creature could be similar to its Creator, and negating them fully protects tawḥīd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَالْمُمَاثَلَةُ لِلْحَوَادِثِ) أي: الشاملة للأَجْرَام والأعراض أخذاً مما بعده، والتقابل بينها وبين المخالفة للحوادث من التقابل بين الشيء والمساوي لنقيضه على نَسَقٍ ما قبله؛ لأن نقيض المخالفة للحوادث : لا مخالفة للحوادث، وهي عينُ المماثلة للحوادث. واعلم أنَّ أنواع المماثلة عَشَرَةُ: الأول: أن يكون جزماً، الثاني: أن يكون عَرَضاً يقوم بالجزم، والثالث: أن يكون في جهةٍ، الرابع: أن يكون له هو جهةً، الخامس أن يكون في مكان السادس أن يكون في زمان، السابع: أن يكون مَحَلّاً للحوادث، الثامن: أن يكون متّصفاً بالصَّغَر التاسع أن يكون متصفاً بالكبر، العاشر : أن يكون متصفاً بالأغراض في الأفعال أو الأحكام وقد ذكرها المصنف على هذا الترتيب. فتدبر. (قوله: بأَنْ يَكُونَ... إلخ) هذا تصوير للمماثلة للحوادث بأنواعها العشرة المذكورة. (قوله: (جزماً) هو ما مَلَاً ،فراغاً، سواء كان مركباً أو مفرداً، بخلاف الجسم، فإنه يختص بالمركب. والصحيحُ : أن مُعْتَقِدَ الجِسْمِيَّةِ لا يَكْفُرُ إِلا إن قال: إنه جِسْم كالأجسام، فالمكفِّرُ في الحقيقة إنما هو التشبيه. (قوله: أَيْ: تَأْخُذُ ذَاتُهُ الْعَلِيَّةُ ... إلخ) تفسير لمدخول (أن) باللازم؛ لأنه يلزمُ من كونه جزماً أخذُهُ قدراً من الفراغ واستفيد من كلامه: أنه يجوز إطلاق الذات عليه تعالى وهو الصحيح. وقيل: لا يجوز ذلك. وقيل بالوقف. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري :(قوله: تَضويرٌ لِلْمُمَاثَلَةِ) أي: بيان لها ببيان أسبابها، فالباء في قوله بأن يكون جزماً) سببية لا تصويرية كما هو ظاهر. (قوله: تَفْسِيرٌ لِمَدْخُولِ أَنْ بِاللَّازِم الظاهر أنه تفسير حقيقي؛ لأن الكون جرماً هو الجرمية، وحقيقة الجرمية: أخذ الذات قدراً من الفراغ.
His saying: 'and likeness (al-mumāthala) to originated things' means encompassing bodies and accidents, taken from what follows. The opposition between it and otherness (al-mukhālafa) to originated things is of the type of opposition between a thing and the equivalent of its negation, in the pattern of what preceded, because the negation of otherness to originated things is 'no otherness to originated things', and it is identical to likeness to originated things. Know that the types of likeness are ten: first: that He be a body (jazm), second: that He be an accident subsisting in a body, third: that He be in a direction, fourth: that He be a direction, fifth: that He be in a place, sixth: that He be in a time, seventh: that He be a locus for accidents, eighth: that He be described as small, ninth: that He be described as large, tenth: that He be described as having purposes in actions or judgments. The author has mentioned them in this order. So reflect. His saying: 'by His being... etc.' This is a depiction of likeness to originated things by its ten mentioned types. His saying: 'a body (jazman)' is that which fills a void, whether compound or simple, unlike the body (jism), which is specific to compound. The correct view is that the believer in corporeality (al-jismiyya) does not disbelieve unless he says that He is a body like bodies; indeed, the cause of disbelief in reality is comparison (al-tashbīh). His saying: 'i.e., His exalted essence takes... etc.' is an interpretation of the object of 'an' (that) by the necessary consequence, because from His being a body necessarily follows that His essence takes up a measure of void. And it is understood from his statement that it is permissible to apply the term 'essence' (al-dhāt) to Him, exalted, and this is correct. Some say it is not permissible. Some say to stop. Commentary of al-ʿAllāma Aḥmad al-Ujhūrī: His saying: 'a depiction of likeness' means an explanation of it by stating its causes. So the 'ba' (ب) in his statement 'by His being a body' is causal, not depictive, as is apparent. His saying: 'an interpretation of the object of 'an' by the necessary consequence'—apparently it is a real interpretation, because being a body is corporeality (al-jarmiyya), and the reality of corporeality is the essence taking up a measure of void.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage defines and enumerates the ten types of likeness (mumathala) to created things that are impossible for God. It begins by noting that the opposition between likeness and otherness (mukhālafa) to created things is of the same logical type as earlier: equivalence. Then it lists ten types: being a body, being an accident, being in a direction, being a direction, being in a place, being in a time, being a locus for accidents, being qualified as small, being qualified as large, and being qualified by purposes. Then al-Bajuri comments on the first type (being a body): a body (jazm) is what fills a void, whether simple or compound, whereas jism is specifically compound. He then states the correct position on takfir: a person who believes God is a body (jismiyya) is not a disbeliever unless he explicitly says God is a body like other bodies, because the actual cause of disbelief is tashbih (direct comparison). Then he explains that the phrase 'takes His essence...' is an interpretation by necessary consequence: being a body necessarily entails occupying space. The commentary by al-Ajhuri clarifies that 'depiction' means explanation by causes, and the 'ba' is causal. He also says that the interpretation is real because being a body is essentially the same as occupying space.

Key Terms
مماثلة (mumāthala)
مخالفة (mukhālafa)
جزم (jazm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many types of likeness to created things are enumerated?
A) 8
B) 9
C) 10
D) 7

Key Takeaway
There are ten distinct ways in which likeness to created things is impossible for Allah, covering all dimensions of similarity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of Allah hearing your prayers, do you unconsciously imagine Him in a certain direction or place? Which of the ten types of likeness is most subtle in your own mind, and what practical step will you take today to purify your concept of Allah from it?
?
The tenth type warns against attributing purposes in a human sense. Are you ever tempted to question or assume you fully know Allah's wisdom behind His decrees? How does this lesson call you to submit to His transcendence even when you don't understand?
?
If someone accused you of having a flawed understanding of Allah's oneness, would your defense be rooted in the precise negation of these ten likenesses? Write down one way you will teach this categorization to someone else to protect them from subtle shirk.

The Ruling on Divine Corporeality and the Boundary of Takfir
Al-Bajuri comments on the first type of likeness and the ruling on takfir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between believing God is a body (jismiyya) and explicitly comparing Him to bodies (tashbīh), and why only the latter constitutes disbelief.

Core Idea
Believing God is a body is not kufr unless one says He is like other bodies.

In this lesson, al-Bajuri comments on the first type of likeness—being a body (jazm). He clarifies the distinction between the terms jazm and jism, and then addresses a crucial theological question: when does belief in divine corporeality become disbelief? He explains that the mere belief that God is a body does not necessarily entail tashbīh (comparison), and that only explicit comparison (like bodies) leads to kufr. This lesson also touches on the permissibility of using the term 'essence' (dhāt) for Allah.

Guided Path
1
Understand jazm vs jism
Al-Bajuri defines jazm as anything that fills a void, whether compound or simple, while jism is specifically compound. Note that the first type of likeness is being a jazm.
This distinction is important for the takfir ruling.
2
Learn the takfir ruling
Read the statement: 'the correct view is that the believer in corporeality does not disbelieve unless he says that He is a body like bodies.' Understand why: the cause of disbelief is tashbīh, not mere corporeality belief.
This guards against excessive takfir.
3
Reflect on the term 'essence'
Al-Bajuri mentions that it is permissible to apply the term 'essence' (dhāt) to Allah, contrary to some views. Consider how this relates to negative theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَالْمُمَاثَلَةُ لِلْحَوَادِثِ) أي: الشاملة للأَجْرَام والأعراض أخذاً مما بعده، والتقابل بينها وبين المخالفة للحوادث من التقابل بين الشيء والمساوي لنقيضه على نَسَقٍ ما قبله؛ لأن نقيض المخالفة للحوادث : لا مخالفة للحوادث، وهي عينُ المماثلة للحوادث. واعلم أنَّ أنواع المماثلة عَشَرَةُ: الأول: أن يكون جزماً، الثاني: أن يكون عَرَضاً يقوم بالجزم، والثالث: أن يكون في جهةٍ، الرابع: أن يكون له هو جهةً، الخامس أن يكون في مكان السادس أن يكون في زمان، السابع: أن يكون مَحَلّاً للحوادث، الثامن: أن يكون متّصفاً بالصَّغَر التاسع أن يكون متصفاً بالكبر، العاشر : أن يكون متصفاً بالأغراض في الأفعال أو الأحكام وقد ذكرها المصنف على هذا الترتيب. فتدبر. (قوله: بأَنْ يَكُونَ... إلخ) هذا تصوير للمماثلة للحوادث بأنواعها العشرة المذكورة. (قوله: (جزماً) هو ما مَلَاً ،فراغاً، سواء كان مركباً أو مفرداً، بخلاف الجسم، فإنه يختص بالمركب. والصحيحُ : أن مُعْتَقِدَ الجِسْمِيَّةِ لا يَكْفُرُ إِلا إن قال: إنه جِسْم كالأجسام، فالمكفِّرُ في الحقيقة إنما هو التشبيه. (قوله: أَيْ: تَأْخُذُ ذَاتُهُ الْعَلِيَّةُ ... إلخ) تفسير لمدخول (أن) باللازم؛ لأنه يلزمُ من كونه جزماً أخذُهُ قدراً من الفراغ واستفيد من كلامه: أنه يجوز إطلاق الذات عليه تعالى وهو الصحيح. وقيل: لا يجوز ذلك. وقيل بالوقف. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري :(قوله: تَضويرٌ لِلْمُمَاثَلَةِ) أي: بيان لها ببيان أسبابها، فالباء في قوله بأن يكون جزماً) سببية لا تصويرية كما هو ظاهر. (قوله: تَفْسِيرٌ لِمَدْخُولِ أَنْ بِاللَّازِم الظاهر أنه تفسير حقيقي؛ لأن الكون جرماً هو الجرمية، وحقيقة الجرمية: أخذ الذات قدراً من الفراغ.
His saying: 'and likeness (al-mumāthala) to originated things' means encompassing bodies and accidents, taken from what follows. The opposition between it and otherness (al-mukhālafa) to originated things is of the type of opposition between a thing and the equivalent of its negation, in the pattern of what preceded, because the negation of otherness to originated things is 'no otherness to originated things', and it is identical to likeness to originated things. Know that the types of likeness are ten: first: that He be a body (jazm), second: that He be an accident subsisting in a body, third: that He be in a direction, fourth: that He be a direction, fifth: that He be in a place, sixth: that He be in a time, seventh: that He be a locus for accidents, eighth: that He be described as small, ninth: that He be described as large, tenth: that He be described as having purposes in actions or judgments. The author has mentioned them in this order. So reflect. His saying: 'by His being... etc.' This is a depiction of likeness to originated things by its ten mentioned types. His saying: 'a body (jazman)' is that which fills a void, whether compound or simple, unlike the body (jism), which is specific to compound. The correct view is that the believer in corporeality (al-jismiyya) does not disbelieve unless he says that He is a body like bodies; indeed, the cause of disbelief in reality is comparison (al-tashbīh). His saying: 'i.e., His exalted essence takes... etc.' is an interpretation of the object of 'an' (that) by the necessary consequence, because from His being a body necessarily follows that His essence takes up a measure of void. And it is understood from his statement that it is permissible to apply the term 'essence' (al-dhāt) to Him, exalted, and this is correct. Some say it is not permissible. Some say to stop. Commentary of al-ʿAllāma Aḥmad al-Ujhūrī: His saying: 'a depiction of likeness' means an explanation of it by stating its causes. So the 'ba' (ب) in his statement 'by His being a body' is causal, not depictive, as is apparent. His saying: 'an interpretation of the object of 'an' by the necessary consequence'—apparently it is a real interpretation, because being a body is corporeality (al-jarmiyya), and the reality of corporeality is the essence taking up a measure of void.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage defines and enumerates the ten types of likeness (mumathala) to created things that are impossible for God. It begins by noting that the opposition between likeness and otherness (mukhālafa) to created things is of the same logical type as earlier: equivalence. Then it lists ten types: being a body, being an accident, being in a direction, being a direction, being in a place, being in a time, being a locus for accidents, being qualified as small, being qualified as large, and being qualified by purposes. Then al-Bajuri comments on the first type (being a body): a body (jazm) is what fills a void, whether simple or compound, whereas jism is specifically compound. He then states the correct position on takfir: a person who believes God is a body (jismiyya) is not a disbeliever unless he explicitly says God is a body like other bodies, because the actual cause of disbelief is tashbih (direct comparison). Then he explains that the phrase 'takes His essence...' is an interpretation by necessary consequence: being a body necessarily entails occupying space. The commentary by al-Ajhuri clarifies that 'depiction' means explanation by causes, and the 'ba' is causal. He also says that the interpretation is real because being a body is essentially the same as occupying space.

Key Terms
جزم (jazm)
جسم (jism)
تشبيه (tashbīh)
ذات (dhāt)
جرمية (jarmiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any belief that Allah is a body constitutes disbelief (kufr).
Correction: Al-Bajuri states that the believer in corporeality does not disbelieve unless he explicitly compares God to bodies (saying 'like bodies'). The cause of disbelief is tashbīh, not mere belief in corporeality.

Checkpoint
According to al-Bajuri, under what condition does a person who believes in divine corporeality (الجسمية) become a disbeliever?
A) If they say God is a body in any sense
B) If they say God is a body like other bodies
C) If they say God is not a body
D) If they deny divine attributes

Key Takeaway
Believing that Allah is a body does not automatically lead to kufr; disbelief occurs only when one explicitly compares Allah to created bodies (tashbīh).

Impossibility of Allah being described with accidents
Sanusi negates temporal accidents from Allah's essence with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Allah cannot be described with temporal accidents and how this principle is fundamental to the Sunni doctrine of divine attributes.

Core Idea
Allah's attributes are eternal; He cannot acquire new ones.

In this concise passage, Sanusi lays down an essential principle of Sunni theology: Allah's essence is not subject to temporal change. He cannot be described with accidents—new, originated qualities that come into being. To illustrate, he gives examples of a new power, new will, or new knowledge. This doctrine safeguards the belief in Allah's perfection and eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the original Arabic and its English translation. Notice how the text uses the term 'ḥawādith' (accidents) and gives specific examples.
2
Understand the concept of 'ḥawādith'
Reflect on what it means for something to be an accident—a quality that arises in time. Contrast this with essential attributes of Allah.
In kalam, 'ḥawādith' refer to temporal events or attributes that are created.
3
Apply the principle
Ask yourself: why can't Allah have a new knowledge? Because His knowledge is eternal and perfect; any new knowledge would imply deficiency or change.
This connects to the broader doctrine that Allah's attributes are not distinct from His essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أَوْ تَتَّصِفَ ذَاتُهُ الْعَلِيَّةُ بِالْحَوَادِثِ) أي: كأن تتصف بقدرة حادثة، أو إرادة حادثة، أو علم حادث إلى غير ذلك.
Or that His exalted essence be qualified with accidents – i.e., such as being qualified by a new power, or a new will, or a new knowledge, and so on.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage negates from Allah that His essence be described with temporal accidents (hawadith). It gives examples: a new (i.e., originated) power, a new will, a new knowledge. Since Allah is eternal and His attributes are eternal, it is impossible for Him to acquire any new attribute that did not exist before. This is a fundamental principle in Sunni kalam: Allah's attributes are eternal, not created in time.

Key Terms
اتصاف
ذات علية
حوادث
قدرة حادثة
إرادة حادثة
علم حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Allah can acquire new attributes or knowledge over time.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that Allah cannot be described with any temporal accident, including new knowledge, new power, or new will. All of Allah's attributes are eternal and unchanging.

Checkpoint

A) Eternal power
B) New knowledge
C) Essential will
D) Attribute of life

Key Takeaway
Allah cannot be described with any temporal accident, as His attributes are eternal and unchanging.

Allah's Essence Does Not Receive Temporal Events
Sanusi enumerates examples of temporal events impossible for Allah's essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Allah's essence cannot be described with temporal events and how this protects divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Allah's attributes are eternal; they cannot be created or acquired.

In this brief but powerful passage, Sanusi clarifies a cornerstone of Islamic theology: that Allah's essence is not a locus for temporal, created attributes. He lists examples like temporal power, will, and knowledge to emphasize that whatever originates cannot attach to the Eternal. This guards against any suggestion that Allah acquires new attributes over time.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully, noting the key terms.
Pay attention to the examples Sanusi gives.
2

These examples illustrate what 'ḥawādith' means.
3
Reflect on the theological implication

This connects to the concept of divine immutability.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: أَوْ تَتَّصِفَ ذَاتُهُ الْعَلِيَّةُ بِالْحَوَادِثِ) أي: كأن تتصف بقدرة حادثة، أو إرادة حادثة، أو علم حادث إلى غير ذلك.

Commentary
This passage is a concise statement explaining the impossibility of Allah's essence being described with temporal events (ḥawādith). The phrase 'temporal events' refers to accidents or attributes that come into existence, such as a power that is created (ḥāditha), a will that is created, or a knowledge that is created. The implication is that Allah's essence is eternal and does not become a locus for things that originate; His attributes are all eternal, not temporally originated. This guards against any notion that Allah could acquire new attributes after having lacked them.
