(1) من سورة الزمر: ٧.
Text of Umm al-Barahin by Abu Abdillah al-Sanusi: Not the aforementioned bringing into existence. And the opposition between them is of the opposition of privation and disposition; because aversion (karāha) is the absence of will (irāda) as the author said. And in the speech there is ellipsis at the beginning and end, and the estimation is: and bringing into existence something of the world or annihilating it while having aversion to its existence or non-existence. And that was only opposed to will because the emergence of something from the world from it negates the generality of its attachment, and another [reason] is the emergence of the entire world from it. So this opposes will in terms of the generality of its attachment, not in terms of its essence, contrary to bringing into existence by way of causation (ta‘līl) or by nature (tab‘), for it is opposed to it in terms of its essence. And there is no difference between good and evil as the author’s speech includes it, contrary to the Mu‘tazila who held that He, exalted, does not will evils and abominations, and they argued that willing evil is evil, and willing the abominable is abominable, and that forbidding what is willed and commanding what is not willed is foolishness, and that punishment for what is willed is injustice, and Allah is exalted above all of that. And it was refuted that this is only considered evil or abominable or foolishness or injustice relative to the contingent, not to Him, exalted; because He is not asked about what He does, and the wisdom of His command or prohibition is the manifestation of the test: whether the servant obeys or not? Commentary of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri: (His statement: 'the disposition') it is the firmly rooted attribute. (His statement: 'and more appropriate') i.e., in negating the attachment of will, not in negating its generality; because he negates it from its root. (His statement: 'contrary to bringing into existence by way of causation... etc.') That which opposes the essence of will is bringing the entire world into existence by way of causation or nature, and the author’s speech is about bringing something of it into existence by that way, and it only opposes attachment like the preceding one. The answer is that the one who asserts causation or nature applies it to the entire world, not generality in some without others, so its opposition to will is from the perspective of reality, not from the perspective of the author’s speech. Notes of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi: It is bringing into existence something of the world or annihilating it with aversion as the author said, therefore he turned to this construction. But the point of the comparison is his statement: 'with aversion to it' and what is coordinated with it. Yes, it would have been more suitable for him to say: 'and his aversion to something he brought into existence, or annihilated, or his heedlessness, or his forgetfulness of that... etc.' unless it is said: this is what the commentator intended. Consider. (His statement: 'and another') i.e., more worthy and prior, and it is a fronted predicate, and what follows it is a delayed subject. (The rest of the text including page 134: Commentary of al-Bajuri on al-Sanusia) And there does not apply to the school of Ahl al-Sunna the saying of the Exalted: {And He is not pleased for His servants with unbelief} (Q. 39:7); because will (irāda) is other than pleasure (riḍā), and adherence to the verse is based on their synonymy, which is false. In summary, it follows upon the school of the Mu‘tazila that most of what occurs in existence is not according to His will, Exalted. And it has been narrated that some imams of Ahl al-Sunna attended a debate with some Mu‘tazila. When the Mu‘tazili sat, he said: 'Glory be to the One who is free from indecency.' The Sunni said: 'Glory be to the One in whose kingdom nothing occurs except what He wills.' The Mu‘tazili said: 'Does our Lord will to be disobeyed?' The Sunni said: 'Is our Lord disobeyed by force?' The Mu‘tazili said: 'Do you think, if He prevents me from guidance and decrees upon me ruin, has He done good to me or evil?' He said: 'If He prevents you from what is yours, He has done evil; and if He prevents you from what is His, then He singles out for His mercy whom He wills.' So the Mu‘tazili was silenced from the debate. Commentary of al-Ajhuri: (His statement: 'and He is not pleased') perhaps what is meant by pleasure (riḍā) is refraining from taking to task, i.e., He does not refrain from taking to task for unbelief, rather He takes to task for it with eternal punishment. (His statement: 'it follows upon the school... etc.') because they made will follow command, so He wills only what He commands, so forbidden things, disliked things, and permissible things are not willed by Him, exalted, and there is no doubt that these are more than commanded things. (His statement: 'from indecency') i.e., from willing it. (His statement: 'by force') what is meant by force is the absence of will so that the contrast between the Sunni’s and the Mu‘tazili’s speech appears. Notes of al-Anbabi: (His statement: 'Do you think, if He prevents me from guidance... etc.') His intention is that it is not valid to say that He has done good to me or evil except in this situation, so it is determined that He did not decree ruin upon me, and it did not occur by His will so that it could be said that He did good or evil. Reflect.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument on the attribute of divine will (irāda), focusing on its scope and relation to good and evil. It begins by explaining a point from the matn about opposition between concepts, probably related to a prior statement. It clarifies that the opposition between bringing into existence with aversion (karāha) and simple bringing into existence is of the type of opposition between privation and disposition (ʿadam wa al-malaka), because aversion is defined as absence of will. The text then notes an ellipsis, supplying the implied meaning: bringing into existence or annihilating something with aversion to its existence or non-existence. This negates the generality of will's attachment, because if something occurs against God's will, it violates the principle that all things occur by His will. However, the negation here is of the generality of attachment, not of the essence of will itself. In contrast, bringing into existence by causation (taʿlīl) or by nature (ṭabʿ) would negate will's very essence, because it implies that things happen independently of God's will.

Next, the text affirms that God's will includes both good and evil, contrary to the Muʿtazila. The Muʿtazila argued that God does not will evil or abominations because willing evil is evil, willing the abominable is abominable, forbidding what is willed and commanding what is not willed is foolishness, and punishing for what is willed is injustice. The Sunni response (al-rudd) is that these judgments (evil, abominable, foolish, unjust) are relative to contingent beings, not to God, because God is not questioned about what He does. The wisdom behind His commands and prohibitions is to test whether the servant obeys or not.

The commentary by al-Ajhuri clarifies definitions: al-malaka is a firmly rooted attribute; aḥrā (more appropriate) means in negating attachment, not generality; and the distinction about causation or nature: applying them to the entire world opposes will in reality, not just in the author's speech.

Then al-Anbabi notes that the author's construction is appropriate, though it would be more precise to include 'aversion to something He brought into existence, or annihilated, or His heedlessness, or His forgetfulness' but this may be implied.

Page 134 continues with a response to a potential objection from Q. 39:7, where Allah says He is not pleased with unbelief. The Sunni position is that will (irāda) and pleasure (riḍā) are distinct; the verse refers to pleasure, not will. The Muʿtazila are then shown that if will were restricted to what He commands, then most of what occurs (forbidden, disliked, permissible actions) would be outside His will, which is problematic.

Finally, a narrated debate illustrates the Sunni position: when the Muʿtazili tried to argue that God does not will disobedience, the Sunni countered that disobedience occurs by His will, but not by force (i.e., not without His will). The Muʿtazili then questioned about being prevented from guidance, and the Sunni answered that if God prevents you from what is rightfully yours, that would be evil, but everything belongs to God, so He may do as He wills. The Muʿtazili was silenced.

The commentaries further explain: the verse about pleasure means God does not leave unbelief without punishment; the Muʿtazila make will follow command, leading to most events not being willed; 'force' in the debate means absence of will; and al-Anbabi explains that the Muʿtazili's final argument is invalid because the scenario presupposes that God's prevention and decree are by His will, so the question of good or evil is irrelevant.

Key Terms
الإرادة (irāda)
الكراهة (karāha)
التقابل (taqābul)
العدم والملكة (ʿadam wa al-malaka)
التعلق (taʿalluq)
التعليل (taʿlīl)
الطبع (ṭabʿ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Opposition between will and aversion is like the opposition between existence and non-existence or heat and cold.
Correction: The passage states it is the opposition of privation and disposition, meaning aversion is simply the absence of will, not an opposite attribute.

Checkpoint
What type of opposition exists between the divine will (irāda) and aversion (karāha) according to Sanusi?
A) Opposition of existence and non-existence
B) Opposition of privation and disposition
C) Opposition of contradiction
D) Opposition of correlatives

Key Takeaway
Aversion is the absence of will, so the opposition is one of privation, not equality; this protects the principle that all things occur by God's will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your heart feels aversion to something God has decreed. According to this lesson, that aversion is merely the absence of will. But is your heart truly indifferent, or does it actively resist? What would it mean to acknowledge that your aversion is a lack, not a positive force, and to turn that lack into submission?
?
The lesson says that bringing into existence by nature negates the essence of will. In your daily life, do you attribute events to 'nature' or 'luck' rather than to God's will? How does this lesson expose a hidden shirk in your thoughts? Will you consciously remind yourself that every detail is by His will alone?
?
Sanusi distinguishes between negating the generality of will and negating its essence. When you sin, do you act as though God's will is not comprehensive—that your sin happens outside His will? How does this lesson force you to either accept His will fully or deny it entirely? Repent and realign your heart to His universal will.

Divine Attributes

God's Will Encompasses Good and Evil: Refuting the Mu'tazila
Sanusi refutes the Mu'tazila arguments that God does not will evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sunni theology affirms that God wills both good and evil, and how the Mu'tazila arguments are refuted by distinguishing between relative and absolute judgments.

Core Idea
God wills evil, but that does not make Him evil because He is beyond such relative attributions.

Having established the scope of divine will, Sanusi now tackles the Mu'tazila claim that God cannot will evil. He presents their four proofs and then gives the Sunni response. This is a crucial point of difference between the two schools, resting on a profound understanding of divine transcendence (tanzih).

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila arguments
The passage lists four reasons why the Mu'tazila thought God cannot will evil. Read them carefully and see the logic behind each.
The arguments are: willing evil is evil, willing abominable is abominable, forbidding what is willed is foolish, punishing for what is willed is unjust.
2
Understand the Sunni refutation
The refutation is that these judgments only apply to contingent beings, not to God. God is not subject to such evaluations.
Key phrase: 'because He is not asked about what He does'.
3
Connect to the earlier lesson
Notice how the earlier discussion about opposition prepares the ground: God can will something and still not be pleased with it.

(قوله: وَأَخرَى) أي: أحقُّ وأولى، وهو خبر مقدَّم، وما بعده مبتداً مؤخَّرٌ.

حاشية البيجوري على السنوسية
ولا يَرِدُ على مذهب أهل السنة قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَا يَرْضَى لِعِبَادِهِ الكفر ) ( ) ؛ لأن الإرادة غيرُ الرّضَا، والتمسك بالآية مبني على ترادفهما، وهو باطل. وبالجملة، فيلزم على مذهب المعتزلة: أن أكثر ما يقع في الوجود على غير مراده تعالى، وقد حُكِي أن بعض أئمة أهل السنة حَضَرَ مع بعض المعتزلة للمناظرة، فلما جلس المعتزلي قال سبحان من تَنَزَّه عن الفحشاء، فقال السُّني: سبحان من لا يَقَعُ في مُلْكِهِ إلا ما يشاء، فقال المعتزلي: أيشاءُ ربُّنا أن يُعْصَى؟ فقال السُّنّي: أيُعْصَى ربنا قَهْراً؟ فقال المعتزلي: أرأيتَ أن مَنَعَنِي الهُدَى ، وقَضَى عليَّ بالرَّدَى، أحسنَ إِليَّ أم أسا؟ فقال: إن مَنَعَكَ ما هو لك فقد أسا، وإن مَنَعَكَ ما هو له؛ فيختص برحمته من يشا. فانقطع المعتزلي عن المناظرة.
حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري
(قوله: وَلا يَرْضَى لعل المراد بالرضا ترك المؤاخذة أي لا يترك المؤاخذة بالكفر، بل يؤاخذ عليه بالعذاب المخلد.
(قوله : فَيَلْزَمُ عَلَى مَذْهَبِ... إلخ) لأنهم جعلوا الإرادة تابعة للأمر، فلا يريد إلا ما أمر به فتكون المحرمات والمكروهات والمباحات غير مرادة له تعالى، ولا شك أن هذه أكثر من المأمورات
(قوله: عَنِ الْفَحْشَاءِ) أي: عن إرادتها.
(قوله: (قهراً المراد بالقهر عدم الإرادة حتى تظهر المقابلة بين كلام السني والمعتزلي.
تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي
(قوله: أَرَأَيْتَ إِنْ مَنَعَنِي الْهُدَى... إلخ) مقصودُهُ : أنه لا يَصِحُ أن يقال: إنه أَحْسَنَ إليَّ أو أساء إلا في هذه الحالة، فيتعين أنه لم يَقْضِ علي بالرَّدَى، ولم يقع بإرادته حتى يقال: إنه أحسن أم أساء. تدبر.
.V
(1) من سورة الزمر: ٧.
Text of Umm al-Barahin by Abu Abdillah al-Sanusi: Not the aforementioned bringing into existence. And the opposition between them is of the opposition of privation and disposition; because aversion (karāha) is the absence of will (irāda) as the author said. And in the speech there is ellipsis at the beginning and end, and the estimation is: and bringing into existence something of the world or annihilating it while having aversion to its existence or non-existence. And that was only opposed to will because the emergence of something from the world from it negates the generality of its attachment, and another [reason] is the emergence of the entire world from it. So this opposes will in terms of the generality of its attachment, not in terms of its essence, contrary to bringing into existence by way of causation (ta‘līl) or by nature (tab‘), for it is opposed to it in terms of its essence. And there is no difference between good and evil as the author’s speech includes it, contrary to the Mu‘tazila who held that He, exalted, does not will evils and abominations, and they argued that willing evil is evil, and willing the abominable is abominable, and that forbidding what is willed and commanding what is not willed is foolishness, and that punishment for what is willed is injustice, and Allah is exalted above all of that. And it was refuted that this is only considered evil or abominable or foolishness or injustice relative to the contingent, not to Him, exalted; because He is not asked about what He does, and the wisdom of His command or prohibition is the manifestation of the test: whether the servant obeys or not? Commentary of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri: (His statement: 'the disposition') it is the firmly rooted attribute. (His statement: 'and more appropriate') i.e., in negating the attachment of will, not in negating its generality; because he negates it from its root. (His statement: 'contrary to bringing into existence by way of causation... etc.') That which opposes the essence of will is bringing the entire world into existence by way of causation or nature, and the author’s speech is about bringing something of it into existence by that way, and it only opposes attachment like the preceding one. The answer is that the one who asserts causation or nature applies it to the entire world, not generality in some without others, so its opposition to will is from the perspective of reality, not from the perspective of the author’s speech. Notes of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi: It is bringing into existence something of the world or annihilating it with aversion as the author said, therefore he turned to this construction. But the point of the comparison is his statement: 'with aversion to it' and what is coordinated with it. Yes, it would have been more suitable for him to say: 'and his aversion to something he brought into existence, or annihilated, or his heedlessness, or his forgetfulness of that... etc.' unless it is said: this is what the commentator intended. Consider. (His statement: 'and another') i.e., more worthy and prior, and it is a fronted predicate, and what follows it is a delayed subject. (The rest of the text including page 134: Commentary of al-Bajuri on al-Sanusia) And there does not apply to the school of Ahl al-Sunna the saying of the Exalted: {And He is not pleased for His servants with unbelief} (Q. 39:7); because will (irāda) is other than pleasure (riḍā), and adherence to the verse is based on their synonymy, which is false. In summary, it follows upon the school of the Mu‘tazila that most of what occurs in existence is not according to His will, Exalted. And it has been narrated that some imams of Ahl al-Sunna attended a debate with some Mu‘tazila. When the Mu‘tazili sat, he said: 'Glory be to the One who is free from indecency.' The Sunni said: 'Glory be to the One in whose kingdom nothing occurs except what He wills.' The Mu‘tazili said: 'Does our Lord will to be disobeyed?' The Sunni said: 'Is our Lord disobeyed by force?' The Mu‘tazili said: 'Do you think, if He prevents me from guidance and decrees upon me ruin, has He done good to me or evil?' He said: 'If He prevents you from what is yours, He has done evil; and if He prevents you from what is His, then He singles out for His mercy whom He wills.' So the Mu‘tazili was silenced from the debate. Commentary of al-Ajhuri: (His statement: 'and He is not pleased') perhaps what is meant by pleasure (riḍā) is refraining from taking to task, i.e., He does not refrain from taking to task for unbelief, rather He takes to task for it with eternal punishment. (His statement: 'it follows upon the school... etc.') because they made will follow command, so He wills only what He commands, so forbidden things, disliked things, and permissible things are not willed by Him, exalted, and there is no doubt that these are more than commanded things. (His statement: 'from indecency') i.e., from willing it. (His statement: 'by force') what is meant by force is the absence of will so that the contrast between the Sunni’s and the Mu‘tazili’s speech appears. Notes of al-Anbabi: (His statement: 'Do you think, if He prevents me from guidance... etc.') His intention is that it is not valid to say that He has done good to me or evil except in this situation, so it is determined that He did not decree ruin upon me, and it did not occur by His will so that it could be said that He did good or evil. Reflect.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument on the attribute of divine will (irāda), focusing on its scope and relation to good and evil. It begins by explaining a point from the matn about opposition between concepts, probably related to a prior statement. It clarifies that the opposition between bringing into existence with aversion (karāha) and simple bringing into existence is of the type of opposition between privation and disposition (ʿadam wa al-malaka), because aversion is defined as absence of will. The text then notes an ellipsis, supplying the implied meaning: bringing into existence or annihilating something with aversion to its existence or non-existence. This negates the generality of will's attachment, because if something occurs against God's will, it violates the principle that all things occur by His will. However, the negation here is of the generality of attachment, not of the essence of will itself. In contrast, bringing into existence by causation (taʿlīl) or by nature (ṭabʿ) would negate will's very essence, because it implies that things happen independently of God's will.

Next, the text affirms that God's will includes both good and evil, contrary to the Muʿtazila. The Muʿtazila argued that God does not will evil or abominations because willing evil is evil, willing the abominable is abominable, forbidding what is willed and commanding what is not willed is foolishness, and punishing for what is willed is injustice. The Sunni response (al-rudd) is that these judgments (evil, abominable, foolish, unjust) are relative to contingent beings, not to God, because God is not questioned about what He does. The wisdom behind His commands and prohibitions is to test whether the servant obeys or not.

The commentary by al-Ajhuri clarifies definitions: al-malaka is a firmly rooted attribute; aḥrā (more appropriate) means in negating attachment, not generality; and the distinction about causation or nature: applying them to the entire world opposes will in reality, not just in the author's speech.

Then al-Anbabi notes that the author's construction is appropriate, though it would be more precise to include 'aversion to something He brought into existence, or annihilated, or His heedlessness, or His forgetfulness' but this may be implied.

Page 134 continues with a response to a potential objection from Q. 39:7, where Allah says He is not pleased with unbelief. The Sunni position is that will (irāda) and pleasure (riḍā) are distinct; the verse refers to pleasure, not will. The Muʿtazila are then shown that if will were restricted to what He commands, then most of what occurs (forbidden, disliked, permissible actions) would be outside His will, which is problematic.

Finally, a narrated debate illustrates the Sunni position: when the Muʿtazili tried to argue that God does not will disobedience, the Sunni countered that disobedience occurs by His will, but not by force (i.e., not without His will). The Muʿtazili then questioned about being prevented from guidance, and the Sunni answered that if God prevents you from what is rightfully yours, that would be evil, but everything belongs to God, so He may do as He wills. The Muʿtazili was silenced.

The commentaries further explain: the verse about pleasure means God does not leave unbelief without punishment; the Muʿtazila make will follow command, leading to most events not being willed; 'force' in the debate means absence of will; and al-Anbabi explains that the Muʿtazili's final argument is invalid because the scenario presupposes that God's prevention and decree are by His will, so the question of good or evil is irrelevant.

الحادث (ḥādith)
الامتحان (imtiḥān)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God wills evil, then He must be evil or unjust.
Correction: Sunni theology teaches that these attributions are relative; God's will is beyond such judgments.

Checkpoint
What is the core Sunni response to the Mu'tazila argument that 'willing evil is evil'?
A) God does not actually will evil.
B) Evil is not truly evil from God's perspective.
C) Such judgments are relative to the contingent, not to God.
D) God's will only applies to good actions.

Key Takeaway
God wills both good and evil, but moral judgments like 'evil' and 'unjust' do not apply to God because He is the standard, not subject to standards.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When something evil happens in your life, do you ever find yourself accusing God of injustice? How does the truth that God is not subject to the standard of justice—He is the standard—change your reaction? Will you commit to never questioning His decree again?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that willing evil is evil. Have you ever subconsciously judged God by human moral standards? What practical step will you take to train your heart to accept that God's will is beyond human categorization?
?
God wills both good and evil, and He holds you accountable. This test of obedience is the purpose of His commands. Are you living as if you are being tested, or do you act as though your actions are outside His will? What will you change today to align with this reality?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
8 min
Distinguishing Will from Pleasure: The Debate with Mu'tazila
Sanusi distinguishes will from pleasure and recounts a debate that refutes Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the crucial distinction between God's will (irāda) and His pleasure (riḍā), and see how a famous debate illustrates the Sunni position.

Core Idea
God can will something without being pleased with it; the two attributes are distinct.

Sanusi now addresses a potential objection from the Qur'an: Allah says He is not pleased with unbelief. If He wills it, wouldn't that contradict His displeasure? The answer is that will and pleasure are different. He then recounts a striking debate that powerfully illustrates the Sunni position on divine sovereignty.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the Qur'anic verse
The verse (Q. 39:7) says Allah is not pleased with unbelief. Read the passage and see how the Sunni scholar explains it.
The key is that pleasure (riḍā) here means refraining from punishment, not a synonym for will.
2
Understand the distinction
The passage explicitly states that will and pleasure are not synonymous. This is why the verse does not contradict the Sunni position.
3
Read the debate
Follow the exchange between the Sunni and the Mu'tazili. Notice how each statement undercuts the other's assumptions.
The Mu'tazili is silenced by the end.
4
Reflect on the implications
This debate shows the practical consequences of the Mu'tazila view: most of what happens would not be willed by God.

متن أم البراهين لأبي عبد الله السنوسي
لا الإيجادُ المذكورُ. والتقابل بينهما من تقابل العدم والملكة؛ لأن الكراهة : عدم الإرادة كما قاله المصنف. وفي الكلام حذف أوّلاً وآخراً، والتقدير: وإيجاد شيءٍ من العالم أو إعدامه مع كراهته لوجوده أو عدمه، وإنما كان ذلك منافياً للإرادة؛ لأن خروج شيء من العالم عنها يَنْفِي عموم تعلقها، وأخرى خروج جميع العالم عنها، فمنافاة هذا للإرادة من حيث عموم تعلقها، لا من حيث ذاتها، بخلاف الإيجاد بالتعليل أو بالطبع فإنه مُنَافٍ لها من حيث ذاتها.
ولا فَرْقَ بين الخير والشر كما شَمِلَهُ كلامُ المصنف، خلافاً للمعتزلة حيث ذهبوا إلى أنه تعالى لا يريد الشرور والقبائح واحتجوا بأن إرادة الشرِ شَرٌّ، وإرادة القبيح قبيحةً، وبأن النَّهْيَ عما يُرَادُ والأمر بما لا يُرَاد سَفَة، وبأن العقاب على ما أريد ظُلم، والله مُنَزَّة عن ذلك كلّه. ورُدَّ بأن ذلك إنما يُعَدُّ شرّاً أو قبيحاً أو سَفَها أو ظُلْماً بالنسبة إلى الحادث لا إليه تعالى؛ لأنه لا يُسْأَلُ عما يفعل، وحكمة أمره أو نهيه ظهورُ الامتحان هل يطيعُ العبد أو لا؟

But the point of the comparison is his statement: 'with aversion to it' and what is coordinated with it. Yes, it would have been more suitable for him to say: 'and his aversion to something he brought into existence, or annihilated, or his heedlessness, or his forgetfulness of that... etc.' unless it is said: this is what the commentator intended. Consider. (His statement: 'and another') i.e., more worthy and prior, and it is a fronted predicate, and what follows it is a delayed subject. (The rest of the text including page 134: Commentary of al-Bajuri on al-Sanusia) And there does not apply to the school of Ahl al-Sunna the saying of the Exalted: {And He is not pleased for His servants with unbelief} (Q. 39:7); because will (irāda) is other than pleasure (riḍā), and adherence to the verse is based on their synonymy, which is false. In summary, it follows upon the school of the Mu‘tazila that most of what occurs in existence is not according to His will, Exalted. And it has been narrated that some imams of Ahl al-Sunna attended a debate with some Mu‘tazila. When the Mu‘tazili sat, he said: 'Glory be to the One who is free from indecency.' The Sunni said: 'Glory be to the One in whose kingdom nothing occurs except what He wills.' The Mu‘tazili said: 'Does our Lord will to be disobeyed?' The Sunni said: 'Is our Lord disobeyed by force?' The Mu‘tazili said: 'Do you think, if He prevents me from guidance and decrees upon me ruin, has He done good to me or evil?' He said: 'If He prevents you from what is yours, He has done evil; and if He prevents you from what is His, then He singles out for His mercy whom He wills.' So the Mu‘tazili was silenced from the debate. Commentary of al-Ajhuri: (His statement: 'and He is not pleased') perhaps what is meant by pleasure (riḍā) is refraining from taking to task, i.e., He does not refrain from taking to task for unbelief, rather He takes to task for it with eternal punishment. (His statement: 'it follows upon the school... etc.') because they made will follow command, so He wills only what He commands, so forbidden things, disliked things, and permissible things are not willed by Him, exalted, and there is no doubt that these are more than commanded things. (His statement: 'from indecency') i.e., from willing it. (His statement: 'by force') what is meant by force is the absence of will so that the contrast between the Sunni’s and the Mu‘tazili’s speech appears. Notes of al-Anbabi: (His statement: 'Do you think, if He prevents me from guidance... etc.') His intention is that it is not valid to say that He has done good to me or evil except in this situation, so it is determined that He did not decree ruin upon me, and it did not occur by His will so that it could be said that He did good or evil. Reflect.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument on the attribute of divine will (irāda), focusing on its scope and relation to good and evil. It begins by explaining a point from the matn about opposition between concepts, probably related to a prior statement. It clarifies that the opposition between bringing into existence with aversion (karāha) and simple bringing into existence is of the type of opposition between privation and disposition (ʿadam wa al-malaka), because aversion is defined as absence of will. The text then notes an ellipsis, supplying the implied meaning: bringing into existence or annihilating something with aversion to its existence or non-existence. This negates the generality of will's attachment, because if something occurs against God's will, it violates the principle that all things occur by His will. However, the negation here is of the generality of attachment, not of the essence of will itself. In contrast, bringing into existence by causation (taʿlīl) or by nature (ṭabʿ) would negate will's very essence, because it implies that things happen independently of God's will.

Next, the text affirms that God's will includes both good and evil, contrary to the Muʿtazila. The Muʿtazila argued that God does not will evil or abominations because willing evil is evil, willing the abominable is abominable, forbidding what is willed and commanding what is not willed is foolishness, and punishing for what is willed is injustice. The Sunni response (al-rudd) is that these judgments (evil, abominable, foolish, unjust) are relative to contingent beings, not to God, because God is not questioned about what He does. The wisdom behind His commands and prohibitions is to test whether the servant obeys or not.

The commentary by al-Ajhuri clarifies definitions: al-malaka is a firmly rooted attribute; aḥrā (more appropriate) means in negating attachment, not generality; and the distinction about causation or nature: applying them to the entire world opposes will in reality, not just in the author's speech.

Then al-Anbabi notes that the author's construction is appropriate, though it would be more precise to include 'aversion to something He brought into existence, or annihilated, or His heedlessness, or His forgetfulness' but this may be implied.

Page 134 continues with a response to a potential objection from Q. 39:7, where Allah says He is not pleased with unbelief. The Sunni position is that will (irāda) and pleasure (riḍā) are distinct; the verse refers to pleasure, not will. The Muʿtazila are then shown that if will were restricted to what He commands, then most of what occurs (forbidden, disliked, permissible actions) would be outside His will, which is problematic.

Finally, a narrated debate illustrates the Sunni position: when the Muʿtazili tried to argue that God does not will disobedience, the Sunni countered that disobedience occurs by His will, but not by force (i.e., not without His will). The Muʿtazili then questioned about being prevented from guidance, and the Sunni answered that if God prevents you from what is rightfully yours, that would be evil, but everything belongs to God, so He may do as He wills. The Muʿtazili was silenced.

The commentaries further explain: the verse about pleasure means God does not leave unbelief without punishment; the Muʿtazila make will follow command, leading to most events not being willed; 'force' in the debate means absence of will; and al-Anbabi explains that the Muʿtazili's final argument is invalid because the scenario presupposes that God's prevention and decree are by His will, so the question of good or evil is irrelevant.

Key Terms
الرضا (riḍā)
الامتحان (imtiḥān)
القهري (qahrī)
الملك (mulk)
الهدى (hudā)
الردى (radā)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God wills something, He must be pleased with it.
Correction: Will and pleasure are distinct; God can will something (like unbelief) without being pleased with it.

Checkpoint
According to the Sunni position, why does Q. 39:7 ('And He is not pleased for His servants with unbelief') not contradict that God wills unbelief?
A) Because the verse is abrogated.
B) Because will and pleasure are distinct attributes.
C) Because unbelief is not actually willed by God.
D) Because the verse only applies to believers.

Key Takeaway
God's will is absolute; His pleasure is conditional. Their separation allows for God to will even what He dislikes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you truly believe that it happened only because God willed it, even though He is not pleased with it? How does that realization affect your humility before Him and your reliance on His mercy?
?
If everything you have—including your ability to obey—belongs to God and could be withdrawn at any moment, what kind of gratitude and dependence should fill your heart right now? Is that what you actually feel?
?
The Sunni debater silenced the Mu'tazili with the truth that God owes nothing to anyone. Does your heart accept this truth without resentment, or do you still harbor a sense of entitlement toward God's favor?

Distinguishing Divine Will from Divine Pleasure
Sanusi rebuts Mu'tazili objection using the will/pleasure distinction and a debate anecdote.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'aris distinguish between divine will (irada) and divine pleasure (rida) to refute the Mu'tazili objection from the Quranic verse, and how this distinction is illustrated in a historical debate.

Core Idea
Allah may will what He does not approve; will and pleasure are not the same.

The Mu'tazilites argued that since Allah does not approve of disbelief, He cannot will it. The Ash'ari response hinges on a crucial distinction: will (irada) and pleasure (rida) are not synonymous. In this passage, Sanusi presents this distinction and recounts a famous debate where a Sunni imam silenced a Mu'tazili opponent. Let's explore this rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the shift from logical argument to narrative debate.
2
Identify the key distinction
Find where Sanusi distinguishes will (irada) from pleasure (rida). This is the core of the rebuttal.
The verse is used by Mu'tazila as evidence, but Sanusi shows it does not apply.
3
Analyze the Mu'tazili argument and the Sunni counter
Understand the Mu'tazili position: that will and approval are the same, so if Allah does not approve of disbelief, He cannot will it. Then see how Sanusi counters by separating the two concepts, leading to the conclusion that much of what happens is not willed according to them (which is problematic).
4
Examine the debate narrative
Follow the exchange between the Sunni imam and the Mu'tazili. Notice how each question and answer builds on the distinction. Focus on the final question about 'preventing what is yours' and the decisive answer.
The debate illustrates the practical implications of the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يَرِدُ على مذهب أهل السنة قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَا يَرْضَى لِعِبَادِهِ الكفر﴾؛ لأن الإرادة غيرُ الرّضَا، والتمسك بالآية مبني على ترادفهما، وهو باطل. وبالجملة، فيلزم على مذهب المعتزلة: أن أكثر ما يقع في الوجود على غير مراده تعالى، وقد حُكِي أن بعض أئمة أهل السنة حَضَرَ مع بعض المعتزلة للمناظرة، فلما جلس المعتزلي قال سبحان من تَنَزَّه عن الفحشاء، فقال السُّني: سبحان من لا يَقَعُ في مُلْكِهِ إلا ما يشاء، فقال المعتزلي: أيشاءُ ربُّنا أن يُعْصَى؟ فقال السُّنّي: أيُعْصَى ربنا قَهْراً؟ فقال المعتزلي: أرأيتَ أن مَنَعَنِي الهُدَى، وقَضَى عليَّ بالرَّدَى، أحسنَ إِليَّ أم أسا؟ فقال: إن مَنَعَكَ ما هو لك فقد أسا، وإن مَنَعَكَ ما هو له؛ فيختص برحمته من يشا. فانقطع المعتزلي عن المناظرة.
And it is not an objection against the school of Ahl al-Sunna the saying of the Exalted: 'And He does not approve for His servants disbelief' because will (irada) is other than pleasure (rida), and clinging to the verse is based on their synonymy, which is false. And in summary, it follows upon the Mu'tazilite school that most of what occurs in existence is contrary to His will, exalted. And it has been narrated that one of the imams of Ahl al-Sunna attended a debate with a Mu'tazilite. When the Mu'tazilite sat down, he said: 'Glory be to Him who is free from indecency.' The Sunni said: 'Glory be to Him in whose domain nothing occurs except what He wills.' The Mu'tazilite said: 'Does our Lord will to be disobeyed?' The Sunni said: 'Is our Lord disobeyed by force?' The Mu'tazilite said: 'Do you see, if He prevents me from guidance and decrees upon me ruin, has He done good to me or evil?' He said: 'If He prevented what is yours, then He has done evil; but if He prevented what is His, then He singles out for His mercy whom He wills.' So the Mu'tazilite was silenced from debate.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is a rebuttal of a Mu'tazili objection against the Ash'ari position on divine will. The objection cites the verse 'And He does not approve for His servants disbelief' (Quran 39:7) to argue that Allah does not will disbelief. Sanusi responds by distinguishing between will (irada) and pleasure (rida), stating that they are not synonymous. Thus, Allah may will disbelief without approving of it. Furthermore, Sanusi points out that the Mu'tazili position, which makes will follow command, implies that most occurrences in existence (sins, permissible acts) are not willed by Allah, which is problematic. He then narrates a debate where a Sunni imam outmatches a Mu'tazili. The Mu'tazili first exclaims that Allah is free from indecency, to which the Sunni replies that nothing occurs in His dominion except what He wills. The Mu'tazili then asks if Allah wills disobedience, and the Sunni counters by asking if Allah is disobeyed by force (i.e., against His will). The Mu'tazili then raises a moral argument: if God prevents guidance and decrees ruin, has He done good or evil? The Sunni's final response is crucial: if God prevented what is 'yours' (something you are owed), that would be evil; but since everything is God's, He may grant mercy to whom He wills. This shows that the Mu'tazili's assumption of moral obligation on God is false. The Mu'tazili is thereby silenced.

Key Terms
إرادة (irada)
رضا (rida)
مذهب أهل السنة (madhhab ahl al-sunna)
معتزلة (mu'tazila)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ash'aris believe Allah approves of evil actions.
Correction: No, Ash'aris distinguish between will and approval. Allah may will something without approving it, so He does not approve of evil even though He wills its occurrence.

Checkpoint
What is the key distinction that Sanusi uses to refute the Mu'tazili objection from the verse 'And He does not approve for His servants disbelief'?
A) Will and pleasure are synonymous
B) Will and pleasure are distinct
C) The verse is abrogated
D) Disbelief is not willed by Allah

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari rebuttal against Mu'tazilites on divine will rests on the distinction between will (irada) and pleasure (rida), showing that Allah may will what He does not approve.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever secretly think, 'If Allah truly willed this, He wouldn't have let it happen'? How does the distinction between will and pleasure reshape your understanding of your own failures—and your repentance?
?
The Mu'tazilite asked if he deserved good treatment from God. His feeling: 'Has Allah been unjust to me by not guiding me?' Do you ever hold resentment toward Allah for trials or lack of blessings, assuming you are owed something? What does the Sunni's answer—'If He prevented what is His'—reveal about the danger of entitlement before your Creator?
?
Allah wills your existence and actions, including your sins, yet He does not approve of sin. Will you let this truth drive you to despair or to humility? Can you still love and obey a Lord who wills things He hates—and does that not make His pleasure a greater gift than you could ever earn?

The Meaning of Divine Pleasure (Rida)
Al-Ajhuri defines 'pleasure' as leaving off accountability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the term 'pleasure' (rida) in theological debates can refer to leaving off accountability rather than emotional approval.

Core Idea
Divine pleasure is not approval—it is leaving off punishment.

When the Qur'an states that Allah is not pleased with disbelief, it does not mean He has an emotional state that is disapproving. Sunni theologians reinterpret 'pleasure' in this context as 'leaving off accountability' to avoid anthropomorphism and to clarify that punishment is justly deserved. In this lesson, we examine al-Ajhuri's concise gloss on this key term.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style of al-Ajhuri's gloss.
2
Identify the new definition of rida
Al-Ajhuri says 'perhaps what is meant by pleasure (rida) is leaving off accountability (tark al-mu'akhadha).' This is the key point.
3
Reflect on why this definition matters
This interpretation avoids anthropomorphism and preserves divine transcendence. It also shows that Allah's non-approval is active (punishment) rather than passive (displeasure).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري
(قوله: وَلا يَرْضَى لعل المراد بالرضا ترك المؤاخذة أي لا يترك المؤاخذة بالكفر، بل يؤاخذ عليه بالعذاب المخلد.
(قوله : فَيَلْزَمُ عَلَى مَذْهَبِ... إلخ) لأنهم جعلوا الإرادة تابعة للأمر، فلا يريد إلا ما أمر به فتكون المحرمات والمكروهات والمباحات غير مرادة له تعالى، ولا شك أن هذه أكثر من المأمورات
(قوله: عَنِ الْفَحْشَاءِ) أي: عن إرادتها.
(قوله: (قهراً المراد بالقهر عدم الإرادة حتى تظهر المقابلة بين كلام السني والمعتزلي.
تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي
(قوله: أَرَأَيْتَ إِنْ مَنَعَنِي الْهُدَى... إلخ) مقصودُهُ : أنه لا يَصِحُ أن يقال: إنه أَحْسَنَ إليَّ أو أساء إلا في هذه الحالة، فيتعين أنه لم يَقْضِ علي بالرَّدَى، ولم يقع بإرادته حتى يقال: إنه أحسن أم أساء. تدبر.
Hashiya of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri: His saying: 'And He does not approve' – perhaps what is meant by pleasure (rida) is leaving off accountability, i.e., He does not leave off accountability for disbelief, but rather He punishes for it with eternal torment. His saying: 'So it follows upon the school...' – because they made will (irada) dependent on command, so He only wills what He commands; thus prohibitions, disliked things, and permissible things are not willed by Him, and there is no doubt that these are more than commanded things. His saying: 'from indecency' – i.e., from willing it. His saying: 'by force' – what is meant by force is the absence of will, so that the contrast between the Sunni's and Mu'tazilite's statements becomes clear. Taqrirat of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi: His saying: 'Do you see, if He prevents me from guidance...' – his intent is that it is not correct to say 'He did good to me' or 'He did evil' except in this situation; so it is determined that He did not decree ruin upon me, and it did not occur by His will such that it could be said He did good or evil. Reflect.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage presents the glosses of al-Ajhuri and al-Anbabi on specific phrases from the previous debate. Al-Ajhuri first explains the word 'pleasure' (rida) in the verse: he proposes that it means 'leaving off accountability,' so Allah does not leave off punishing disbelief, but punishes with eternal torment. This clarifies that divine disapproval does not negate will. Next, al-Ajhuri comments on 'so it follows upon the school...' explaining that the Mu'tazila made will (irada) dependent on command (amr), thus only willing what He commands; consequently, prohibited, disliked, and permissible acts are not willed by Allah, which is clearly false since such acts are more numerous. Then he glosses 'from indecency' as 'from willing it,' showing that Allah's transcendence is from willing indecency, not from its occurrence. He also explains 'by force' as 'absence of will,' so the Sunni's response highlights that disobedience occurs with Allah's will, not by force. Al-Anbabi then comments on the Mu'tazili's final question. He states that the Mu'tazili's question about good or evil is invalid because the premise assumes that God has an obligation to give guidance; in reality, everything is God's, so His prevention is not evil. He concludes that it is determined that God did not decree ruin (rada) nor did it occur by His will, so the question is misguided. The lesson ends with 'reflect' (tadabbar).

Key Terms
رضا (rida)
مؤاخذة (mu'akhadha)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'pleasure' (rida) means Allah loves or approves of something.
Correction: In this debate, rida is interpreted as 'leaving off accountability,' not emotional approval.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ajhuri, what does 'pleasure' (rida) mean in the context of the verse?
A) Divine love
B) Leaving off accountability
C) Will
D) Command

Key Takeaway
Divine pleasure in theological debate can mean leaving off accountability, not emotional approval.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah's 'pleasure' means leaving off accountability, how does that change the way you view your own sins? Are you counting on His mere 'disapproval' without expecting real consequences?
?
When you hear that Allah does not approve of disbelief, do you feel a sense of urgency to align your life with His command? Or do you treat it as a passive statement?
?
This lesson defines divine rida in a way that emphasizes accountability. How does this affect your understanding of repentance? Do you truly seek to leave off actions that incur His mu'akhadha?

The Mu'tazili Position on Divine Will and Its Refutation
Al-Ajhuri explains and refutes the Mu'tazili equation of will with command.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili doctrine that will follows command, why it leads to absurd conclusions, and how Sunni theologians respond.

Core Idea
If Allah wills only what He commands, then most of creation is unwilled—an absurdity.

The Mu'tazila argued that divine will (irada) is tied to command (amr): Allah wills what He commands and nothing else. This seems logical but leads to a problematic conclusion: that acts like prohibitions, disliked things, and permissible actions are not willed by Allah. Since such acts are numerous, this would imply that most of human actions occur outside divine will—contradicting Islam's emphasis on divine sovereignty. In this lesson, we explore al-Ajhuri's gloss on this point and the clarifying terminology that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about the Mu'tazili position
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how the Mu'tazili position is presented as a logical deduction.
2
Understand the reasoning: will dependent on command
The Mu'tazila made will dependent on command, so they only accept that what Allah commands is willed.
3
Note the conclusion: only commanded acts are willed
This excludes prohibitions, disliked, and permissible acts from divine will.
4
Consider the refutation: prohibitions etc. are more numerous
Al-Ajhuri points out that these uncommanded acts are far more numerous, making the position absurd.
5
Examine the glosses on 'from indecency' and 'by force'
These clarifications define key terms and contrast Sunni and Mu'tazili views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(قوله : فَيَلْزَمُ عَلَى مَذْهَبِ... إلخ) لأنهم جعلوا الإرادة تابعة للأمر، فلا يريد إلا ما أمر به فتكون المحرمات والمكروهات والمباحات غير مرادة له تعالى، ولا شك أن هذه أكثر من المأمورات

This clarifies that divine disapproval does not negate will. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself thinking that certain events—especially tragedies or sins—are somehow outside Allah's will? What does this lesson's refutation of the Mu'tazili view say about that assumption? Will you now consciously affirm Allah's will in every circumstance, even when it challenges your understanding?
?
If Allah wills everything, including the evil that people do, does that make Him unjust? How does this lesson's distinction between will and command resolve that tension? Before moving on, take a moment to sincerely declare: 'I accept that nothing happens except by Allah's will,' and ask yourself what changes in your heart as you say it.
?
The Mu'tazili position seems logical but leads to absurdity. When you encounter a theological argument that feels rational but contradicts established teaching, what is your default response? Will you commit to testing all reason against revelation, even when it feels uncomfortable?

Refuting a False Premise: God's Obligation to Guide
Al-Anbabi exposes the false premise in the Mu'tazili argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Anbabi's refutation of the Mu'tazili question about God's guidance and the principle that God has no obligation to guide anyone.

Core Idea
God is not obligated to guide; His prevention is not evil.

The Mu'tazili debater asks: if God prevents me from guidance, has He done good or evil to me? This question assumes that God has a duty to guide everyone. Al-Anbabi points out that this premise is false. Allah is the owner of all, and His acts are not subject to such moral evaluation. He is free to give guidance or withhold it without being called 'good' or 'evil.' By examining this incisive comment, we see the Sunni insistence on divine sovereignty.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Anbabi's gloss on the Mu'tazili question
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the context: the Mu'tazili is trying to corner the Sunni.
2
Identify the hidden premise: God must guide
The question only makes sense if God owes guidance. Premise is false.
3
Understand why the premise is false
God is the owner of all; He has no obligations to His creation. His actions are not subject to human moral evaluation.
4
See the conclusion: God did not decree ruin, and the question is invalid
Al-Anbabi says 'it is determined that He did not decree ruin upon me, and it did not occur by His will such that it could be said He did good or evil.'
5
Reflect on the broader principle
This underscores divine sovereignty and the rejection of any notion of obligation on God.
The word 'tadabbar' (reflect) encourages contemplation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري
(قوله: وَلا يَرْضَى لعل المراد بالرضا ترك المؤاخذة أي لا يترك المؤاخذة بالكفر، بل يؤاخذ عليه بالعذاب المخلد.
(قوله : فَيَلْزَمُ عَلَى مَذْهَبِ... إلخ) لأنهم جعلوا الإرادة تابعة للأمر، فلا يريد إلا ما أمر به فتكون المحرمات والمكروهات والمباحات غير مرادة له تعالى، ولا شك أن هذه أكثر من المأمورات
(قوله: عَنِ الْفَحْشَاءِ) أي: عن إرادتها.
(قوله: (قهراً المراد بالقهر عدم الإرادة حتى تظهر المقابلة بين كلام السني والمعتزلي.
تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي
(قوله: أَرَأَيْتَ إِنْ مَنَعَنِي الْهُدَى... إلخ) مقصودُهُ : أنه لا يَصِحُ أن يقال: إنه أَحْسَنَ إليَّ أو أساء إلا في هذه الحالة، فيتعين أنه لم يَقْضِ علي بالرَّدَى، ولم يقع بإرادته حتى يقال: إنه أحسن أم أساء. تدبر.
Hashiya of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri: His saying: 'And He does not approve' – perhaps what is meant by pleasure (rida) is leaving off accountability, i.e., He does not leave off accountability for disbelief, but rather He punishes for it with eternal torment. His saying: 'So it follows upon the school...' – because they made will (irada) dependent on command, so He only wills what He commands; thus prohibitions, disliked things, and permissible things are not willed by Him, and there is no doubt that these are more than commanded things. His saying: 'from indecency' – i.e., from willing it. His saying: 'by force' – what is meant by force is the absence of will, so that the contrast between the Sunni's and Mu'tazilite's statements becomes clear. Taqrirat of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi: His saying: 'Do you see, if He prevents me from guidance...' – his intent is that it is not correct to say 'He did good to me' or 'He did evil' except in this situation; so it is determined that He did not decree ruin upon me, and it did not occur by His will such that it could be said He did good or evil. Reflect.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage presents the glosses of al-Ajhuri and al-Anbabi on specific phrases from the previous debate. Al-Ajhuri first explains the word 'pleasure' (rida) in the verse: he proposes that it means 'leaving off accountability,' so Allah does not leave off punishing disbelief, but punishes with eternal torment. This clarifies that divine disapproval does not negate will. Next, al-Ajhuri comments on 'so it follows upon the school...' explaining that the Mu'tazila made will (irada) dependent on command (amr), thus only willing what He commands; consequently, prohibited, disliked, and permissible acts are not willed by Allah, which is clearly false since such acts are more numerous. Then he glosses 'from indecency' as 'from willing it,' showing that Allah's transcendence is from willing indecency, not from its occurrence. He also explains 'by force' as 'absence of will,' so the Sunni's response highlights that disobedience occurs with Allah's will, not by force. Al-Anbabi then comments on the Mu'tazili's final question. He states that the Mu'tazili's question about good or evil is invalid because the premise assumes that God has an obligation to give guidance; in reality, everything is God's, so His prevention is not evil. He concludes that it is determined that God did not decree ruin (rada) nor did it occur by His will, so the question is misguided. The lesson ends with 'reflect' (tadabbar).

Key Terms
هدى (huda)
رَدَى (rada)
إرادة (irada)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God must guide everyone, and if He doesn't, He does evil.
Correction: God is not obligated to guide; His acts are beyond such moral categories.

Checkpoint
According to al-Anbabi, why is the Mu'tazili's question about good and evil invalid?
A) Because God always does good
B) Because God has no obligation to guide, so prevention is not good or evil
C) Because God's will is not involved
D) Because the question is rhetorical

Key Takeaway
God is not obligated to guide anyone, so the Mu'tazili question about good or evil in guidance is based on a false premise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that God owes you guidance because of your efforts or religious practices? How does al-Anbabi's refutation strip away that entitlement, and what does that reveal about your reliance on His mercy?
?
When you see someone who is misguided, do you find yourself silently judging them or even questioning God’s justice? How will you remind yourself that God is not obligated to guide anyone, and that His actions are beyond human moral categories?
?
This lesson argues that God's prevention of guidance cannot be called evil. Think of a time you felt wronged by God's decree. Does this truth change how you process that pain? What concrete thought will you hold onto when such feelings arise?

Definition of Karaha and Refutation of Mu'tazili Error
Definition of karaha and refutation of Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that karaha (dislike) in divine context means absence of will, and why the Mu'tazili equation of command and will is mistaken.

Core Idea
Karaha is not hatred but absence of will.

The passage opens by clarifying a key divine attribute term: karaha. The author explicitly defines it as 'absence of will' to prevent confusion with its legal meaning. He also uses this definition to correct a major theological error of the Mu'tazila regarding the relationship between God's command and His will.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic sentence defining karaha as 'the absence of His will for it'. Note the author's caution against misunderstanding.
The legal meaning of karaha is a request to leave, which is different.
2
Understand the Mu'tazili error
Identify why the Mu'tazila claimed that what is disliked (makruh) is not willed, based on their equation of command and will.
The author refutes by showing no necessary link between command and will.
3
Reflect on the four possibilities
Notice the four cases: command with/without will, will with/without command. This disproves the Mu'tazili claim.
This was clarified earlier in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: أَيْ: عَدَمِ إِرَادَتِهِ لَهُ تَعَالَى إنما أتى المصنف بذلك ـ مع أن التفسير ليس من وظيفة المتون ؛ لئلا يُتَوَهَّمَ أن المراد بالكراهة معناها الشرعي: وهو طَلَبُ تَرْكِ الشيء طلباً غير جازم.
لا يُقال: إن المقامَ يقتضي تفسيرها بما ذُكِرَ فلا حاجة للتنصيص عليه؛ لأنا نقول : المصنف لاحَظَ الاحتياط، وأيضاً قصد التنبية على خطأ المعتزلة في قولهم: إن الإرادة على وفق الأمر وبناؤهم على ذلك أن المكروة شرعاً ليس بمراد، ووجه خطئهم في ذلك: أنه لا ملازَمَةَ بين الأمر ،والإرادة، فقد يأمُرُ ولا يُرِيدُ، وقد يُرِيدُ ولا يأمُرُ، كما أنه قد يريدُ ويأمرُ ، وقد لا يريد ولا يأمرُ كما تقدم توضيحه.
قوله: أَوْ مَعَ الدُّهُولِ أَوِ الْغَفْلَةِ معطوف على قوله: (مع كراهته، وكذا قوله: (أو بالتعليل أو بالطبع، وعَطْفُ ذلك على الكراهة بالمعنى المذكور من عطف الخاص على العام لدخوله فيها.
فإن قيل: إذا كانت هذه الأمور داخلةً في الكراهة بذلك المعنى؛ كان مستغنى عنها، فلا حاجة إلى ذكرها أجيب بأنه إنما ذكرها المصنف ـ مع كونها مستغنى عنها ؛ لأن المقصود في هذا العلم: ذِكْرُ العقائد على وجه التفصيل؛ لأن خَطَرَ الجهل فيه عظيم، فلا يُكْتَفَى فيه بعامّ عن خاص، ولا بملزوم عن لازم.
واعلم أنه اختلف فقيل : الذُّهُولُ والغَفْلَةُ متساويان. وقيل: الغفلة أعم من الذهول؛ لأن الذهول: هو عَدَمُ العلم بالشيء مع تقدم العلم به، والغفلة: عدم العلم بالشيء مطلقاً، وهذا هو ما ظهر للمؤلف.
حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري
(قوله: أَنَّ الْمَكْرُوهَ شَرْعاً) وكذا المحرم والمباح.
تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي .
(قوله: مِنْ عَطْفِ الْخَاصِّ عَلَى الْعَامِّ فيه أنه لا يكون ب(أو)، إلا أن تُجْعَلَ بمعنى الواو. :(قوله: وَهَذَا مَا ظَهَرَ لِلْمُؤلّف) راجع للقول الثاني، فحينئذٍ ينبغي حَمْلُ المتن عليه.
His saying: 'i.e., the absence of His will for it, Exalted' – the author only brought this, despite interpretation not being the function of matns, lest it be supposed that what is meant by 'karaha' (dislike) is its legal meaning: which is the request to leave a thing in a non-assertive manner. It cannot be said that the context requires its interpretation as mentioned so there is no need to specify it; because we say: the author observed caution, and also intended to alert to the error of the Mu'tazila in their saying that will is in accordance with command, and their building upon that that what is legally disliked (makruh) is not willed. The face of their error in that: there is no necessary connection between command and will. He may command and not will, and may will and not command, just as He may will and command, and may not will and not command, as has been clarified previously. His saying: 'or with heedlessness (dhuhul) or forgetfulness (ghafla)' is conjoined to his saying 'with His dislike,' and likewise his saying 'or by cause or by nature,' and the conjoining of that to dislike in the mentioned sense is a conjunction of the specific to the general because of its inclusion in it. If it is said: since these things are included in dislike in that sense, they are rendered superfluous, so there is no need to mention them. The answer is that the author only mentioned them – despite being superfluous – because the purpose in this science is the mention of beliefs in detail; for the danger of ignorance in it is great, so one is not satisfied with a general over a specific, nor with a necessary over a necessary. And know that there is disagreement: it is said that dhuhul and ghafla are synonymous. And it is said that ghafla is more general than dhuhul; because dhuhul is lack of knowledge of a thing with prior knowledge of it, and ghafla is lack of knowledge of a thing absolutely. And this is what appeared to the author. Hashiya of al-Allama Ahmad al-Ajhuri: His saying: 'that which is legally disliked (makruh') – and likewise the prohibited (haram) and permissible (mubah). Taqrirat of al-Allama Muhammad al-Anbabi: His saying: 'from the conjunction of the specific to the general' – there is a problem with using 'aw' (or) for that, unless it is made to mean 'waw' (and). His saying: 'and this is what appeared to the author' refers to the second opinion; therefore, it is appropriate to interpret the matn according to it.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage is a detailed definition and explanation of the term 'karaha' (dislike) in the context of divine attributes. It explains that the author explicitly defines karaha as 'absence of His will,' anticipating possible misunderstanding that it might mean the legal sense (request to leave). He does this to caution against the Mu'tazili error of equating will with command. Then it discusses the inclusion of 'with dhuhul or ghafla' as specific instances of karaha. An objection is raised that if they are included, they are superfluous. The answer is that in theological matters, detailed mention is necessary due to the gravity of ignorance. Then it reports a disagreement on the relationship between dhuhul and ghafla: some say they are synonymous; others say ghafla is more general, and dhuhul is forgetfulness after knowing. The author opts for the second. Finally, al-Ajhuri expands that makruh, haram, and mubah are all not willed (according to the Ash'ari view). Al-Anbabi comments on the grammatical issue of using 'or' for the specific and general, suggesting it means 'and,' and notes that the matn should be understood according to the second opinion.

Key Terms
كراهة (karaha)
إرادة (irada)
أمر (amr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Karaha means God dislikes something in a human emotional sense, or that it always refers to the legal definition.
Correction: In divine attribute context, karaha simply means absence of will, not human-like dislike or legal request.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the definition of karaha (dislike) attributed to Allah?
A) The request to leave a thing non-assertively
B) The absence of His will for a thing
C) Divine hatred
D) The absence of His command

Key Takeaway
Karaha in divine context means absence of will, and there is no necessary link between command and will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a command of God that feels difficult, do you ever secretly doubt that God truly wills it for you? How does this lesson challenge that assumption, and what would it look like to obey even when you cannot see the will behind it?
?
Think of a time you desired something that was not commanded or even prohibited. How does the truth that God may will without commanding (like death) reshape your understanding of His sovereignty and your own desires?
?
The Mu'tazila demanded a neat logical system where God's will always matches His command. Are there areas in your own faith where you insist on tidy explanations, leaving no room for divine mystery? What would it cost you to trust God's wisdom even when you cannot connect the dots?

Inclusion of Dhuhul and Ghafla and Their Distinction
Defense of detailed mention and distinction of dhuhul/ghafla.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why dhuhul (heedlessness) and ghafla (forgetfulness) are specifically mentioned as cases of karaha, and the scholarly disagreement on their relationship.

Core Idea
Specific instances of karaha are detailed because theological ignorance is dangerous.

The passage now explains why the author added 'with dhuhul or ghafla' and similar phrases despite them already being included in the general definition of karaha. It defends detailed exposition in theology. Then it addresses a disagreement about whether dhuhul and ghafla are synonyms or if ghafla is more general.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objection: if dhuhul/ghafla are already included in karaha, why mention them separately?
This is a common logical concern.
2
Understand the answer
The answer: in theology, detail is necessary because ignorance is grave; general cannot substitute for specific.
The author cites 'purpose of this science'.
3
Analyze the disagreement on dhuhul vs. ghafla
Two opinions: (1) synonymous, (2) ghafla is broader (absence of knowledge) while dhuhul is specific (absence after prior knowledge).
The author prefers the second opinion.
4
Reflect on marginal comments
Al-Ajhuri and al-Anbabi add that makruh, haram, mubah are all not willed, and clarify the grammar of 'aw' (or) meaning 'and'.
These reinforce the matn interpretation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dhuhul and ghafla are always synonyms and interchangeable.
Correction: According to the preferred view, ghafla is more general (absence of knowledge) and dhuhul is a subset (forgetfulness after knowing).

Key Takeaway
Theological details are spelled out despite redundancy; dhuhul is a kind of ghafla (forgetfulness after knowledge) and ghafla is broader.

Impossibility of Ignorance for God: Compound and Simple Ignorance
Sanusi defines the two types of ignorance and states both are impossible for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of ignorance and why both are impossible for God, affirming His perfect knowledge.

Core Idea
God cannot be ignorant, whether by false belief or by lack of knowledge.

Sanusi now turns to a negative attribute: ignorance. He distinguishes two forms—compound ignorance (believing what is false) and simple ignorance (lacking knowledge). Both are impossible for God, because His knowledge is perfect and all-encompassing. This brief passage clarifies what we mean when we say God is not ignorant.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once. Notice how Sanusi defines each term concisely. Then read the translation to ensure understanding.
The phrase 'كَذَا يَسْتَحِيلُ عَلَيْهِ' connects to the previous discussion on the impossibility of certain attributes.
2
Understand the two types of ignorance
Identify the definitions: compound ignorance is believing something contrary to reality; simple ignorance is the absence of knowledge. Reflect on the difference.
Compound ignorance is a positive belief that is false; simple ignorance is a privation.
3
Grasp the ruling: both are impossible for God
Sanusi states both types are impossible for God. This is because God's knowledge is perfect—He cannot have false beliefs nor lack any knowledge.
This upholds the divine attribute of omniscience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَكَذَا يَسْتَحِيلُ عَلَيْهِ تَعَالَى الْجَهْلُ) أي: سواء كان مركباً: وهو اعتقاد الشيء على خلاف ما هو عليه، أو بسيطاً وهو عَدَمُ العلم بالشيء
(His saying: 'And likewise ignorance is impossible for Him, exalted is He') i.e., whether it is compound: which is believing something contrary to what it is, or simple: which is absence of knowledge of something.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage states that ignorance is impossible for God and defines two types. Compound ignorance is believing something contrary to reality, while simple ignorance is simply lacking knowledge. Both are impossible for God, affirming His perfect knowledge.

Key Terms

جهل بسيط
استحالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that simple ignorance is not blameworthy, so it might be possible for God because it is just a lack.
Correction: The passage explicitly states both types are impossible for God. Even the absence of knowledge contradicts His perfect omniscience.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is compound ignorance (الجهل المركب)?
A) Not knowing something.
B) Believing something contrary to what it actually is.
C) Doubting something.
D) Ignoring something.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge is perfect, so both false belief (compound ignorance) and lack of knowledge (simple ignorance) are impossible for Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God cannot be ignorant—neither by false belief nor by lack of knowledge—then what does it mean for you when you try to hide your sins from Him? Are you acting as if He is ignorant of what you do? How will you change that today?
?
Consider a time when you insisted on a belief without verifying it from revelation. Did you realize that compound ignorance (believing something contrary to reality) is impossible for God, yet you as a creature are susceptible to it? How does that contrast with God's perfection, and what step will you take to correct any false belief?
?
The passage defines simple ignorance as 'absence of knowledge.' If you neglect to learn what God has commanded, are you not choosing a state that is impossible for God? What commitment will you make this week to seek knowledge that you have been avoiding?

Death, Deafness, Blindness, Muteness: Existential Attributes According to Ahl al-Sunna
Al-Bajuri explains the Sunni view that death, deafness, blindness, muteness are existential attributes, with evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni and Mu'tazili views on death, deafness, blindness, and muteness, and the Quranic evidence for the Sunni position.

Core Idea
For Ahl al-Sunna, death is a created existent, not a mere absence.

This lesson examines a key theological dispute: are privative attributes like death real entities or mere absences? Al-Bajuri presents the Sunni position that they are existential entities opposed to their positive counterparts, and contrasts it with the Mu'tazili view that they are privations. The Quranic verse 'He created death and life' serves as evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note its structure
Read the Arabic passage once, noticing that each of the four attributes (death, deafness, blindness, muteness) is treated in the same repetitive pattern. Then read the translation.
2
Understand the Sunni definition for death
Focus on death: Ahl al-Sunna say it is an existential entity (amr wujudi) that opposes life, while the Mu'tazila say it is the absence of life. Notice the contrast type: opposites vs. privation/possession.
3
Examine the Quranic proof and the Mu'tazili response
The Sunni evidence is from Quran 67:2, 'He created death and life', because creation applies to existents. The Mu'tazila respond by interpreting 'creation' as 'decree' (taqdir), which can apply to non-existents.
4
Apply the same pattern to deafness, blindness, muteness
Notice that the same structure is repeated exactly for deafness/hearing, blindness/sight, and muteness/speech. Ahl al-Sunna consider each existential, Mu'tazila each privative.
5
Reflect on why this distinction matters
This dispute affects how we understand God's creation and attributes. For Ahl al-Sunna, even privations like death are created by Allah, affirming His absolute sovereignty.
This connects to broader themes in kalam about divine attributes and creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَالْمَوْتُ) هو أمرٌ وجودِيٌّ يُضَادُّ الحياةَ عند أهل السنة. وأما عند المعتزلة : فهو عَدَمُ الحياة عما من شأنه أن يكون حيّاً. والتقابل بينه وبين الحياة من تقابل الصِّدِّين على الأول، ومن تقابل العَدَم والملكة على الثاني. ويدلُّ للأول قوله تعالى: خَلَقَ الْمَوْتَ وَالْحَيَوَةَ ) (۱) لأن الخلق إنما يتعلق بالأمر الوجودي. وأجيب من جهة القائلين بالثاني بأن المراد بالخلق التقديرُ ، وهو كما يكون للوجودي يكون للعدمي. (قوله: وَالصَّمَمُ) هو أمرٌ وجودي يُضَادُّ السمع عند أهل السنة. وأما عند المعتزلة : فهو عَدَمُ السمع عما من شأنه أن يكون سميعاً. والتقابل بينه وبين السمع من تقابل الضدين على الأول، ومن تقابل العدم والملكة على الثاني. (قوله: (وَالْعَمَى) هو أمرٌ وجودي يضادُّ البصر عند أهل السنة. وأما عند المعتزلة : فهو عَدَمُ البصر عما من شأنه أن يكون بصيراً. والتقابل بينه وبين البصر من تقابل الضدين على الأول، ومن تقابل العدم والملكة على الثاني. (قوله: (وَالْبَكَمُ هو أمرٌ وجودي يضادُّ الكلام عند أهل السنة. وأما عند المعتزلة: فهو عَدَمُ الكلام عما من شأنه أن يكون متكلّماً، والتقابل بينه وبين الكلام من تقابل الضدين على الأول، ومن تقابل العدم والملكة على الثاني.
(His saying: 'and death') is an existential entity that opposes life according to Ahl al-Sunna. As for the Mu'tazila, it is the absence of life from that which is capable of being living. The contrast between it and life is a contrast of opposites on the first view, and a contrast of privation and possession on the second. And the first view is supported by His saying, exalted: 'He created death and life' (Surah al-Mulk, 2) because creation is only related to an existential entity. And it was answered from the proponents of the second view that by 'creation' is meant 'decree', and that is applicable to the existential as well as the non-existential. (His saying: 'and deafness') is an existential entity that opposes hearing according to Ahl al-Sunna. As for the Mu'tazila, it is the absence of hearing from that which is capable of being hearing. The contrast between it and hearing is a contrast of opposites on the first view, and a contrast of privation and possession on the second. (His saying: 'and blindness') is an existential entity that opposes sight according to Ahl al-Sunna. As for the Mu'tazila, it is the absence of sight from that which is capable of being seeing. The contrast between it and sight is a contrast of opposites on the first view, and a contrast of privation and possession on the second. (His saying: 'and muteness') is an existential entity that opposes speech according to Ahl al-Sunna. As for the Mu'tazila, it is the absence of speech from that which is capable of being speaking. The contrast between it and speech is a contrast of opposites on the first view, and a contrast of privation and possession on the second.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is a series of definitions and doctrinal comparisons regarding four attributes: death (mawt), deafness (samam), blindness (ama), and muteness (bakam). For each, Al-Bajuri states the position of Ahl al-Sunna (the Sunni school) and the Mu'tazila. According to Ahl al-Sunna, each of these is an existential entity (amr wujudi) that is the opposite (didd) of its corresponding positive attribute: death opposes life, deafness opposes hearing, blindness opposes sight, muteness opposes speech. According to the Mu'tazila, they are purely privations (adam) of those positive attributes. Thus, the relationship is one of opposition (tadadd) on the Sunni view, and privation/posession (adam wa mulka) on the Mu'tazili view. The passage then provides evidence for the Sunni view from the Quranic verse 'He created death and life' (Surah al-Mulk:2), arguing that creation applies to existential things. It also presents the Mu'tazili response that 'creation' here means 'decree' (taqdir), which can apply to non-existents as well. The same pattern is repeated for deafness, blindness, and muteness.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Death is merely the absence of life (Mu'tazili view).
Correction: Ahl al-Sunna hold that death is an existential entity created by Allah, as evidenced by the Quranic verse 'He created death and life'.

Checkpoint
According to Ahl al-Sunna, is death an existential attribute or a privation?
A) Existential attribute.
B) Privation of life.
C) Both existential and privation.
D) Neither existential nor privation.

Key Takeaway
Ahl al-Sunna affirm that death, deafness, blindness, and muteness are real created entities that oppose their positive counterparts, as supported by the Quranic statement that God created death and life.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If death is a created existent that opposes life, how does that reshape your perspective on your own mortality? Recognizing that death is a real creation of God, are you actively preparing for it, or do you live as if it is merely an end to life? What concrete step will you take this week to remember death as a created reality?
?
Deafness, blindness, and muteness are described as existential opposites to hearing, sight, and speech. In what areas of your spiritual life are you 'deaf' to God's commands, 'blind' to His signs, or 'mute' in proclaiming His truth? Knowing that these are real deficiencies created by God as tests or punishments, how will you plead to Him to remove them from your heart, not just your body?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that 'creation' in the verse means 'decree' to avoid admitting death is an existent. Are you tempted to reinterpret clear texts of the Quran to fit your own assumptions about reality? Think of an area where you might be rationalizing away a difficult truth. Will you submit to the apparent meaning of the Quran, even when it challenges your intellectual comfort?
